The Contingent's Need for a Cause: A Necessary Judgment
Definition of the contingent and the necessity of a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the contingent as equal in existence and non-existence, and why its need for a cause is a necessary intuitive judgment.

Core Idea
The contingent's need for a cause is a self-evident truth, not a derived conclusion.

This lesson introduces the first proof from al-Jurjani's Sharh al-Mawaqif. He begins by defining the contingent and explaining what it means for possibility to necessitate a cause. He then argues that after conceptualizing the terms, the mind judges this necessity intuitively, using the analogy of a scale to show that even children recognize that an equal cannot incline without an external factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the definition of al-mumkin and the key phrase 'لا يترجح أحد طرفيه إلا لأمر'.
2
Understand the scale analogy
The scale pans are equal; no one would accept that one tilts without a cause. This illustrates the intuitive nature of the judgment.
3
Identify the conclusion
The author concludes that this is the meaning of 'imkan being necessitating to the cause'. Note that the judgment is necessary (daruri).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع في أبحاث الممكن لذاته
إثباته منهجان: الأول دعوى الضرورة، فإن الممكن ما يتساوى طرفاه) أي وجوده وعدمه بالنظر إلى ذاته( ومعنى كونه) أي كون الإمكان الذي هو ذلك التساوي( محوجاً) للممكن( إلى السبب أنه لا يترجح أحد طرفيه) على الأخر( إلا لأمر) مغاير للممكن( يرجح أحدهما على الآخر، والحكم بعد (تصورهما) أي تصور الموضوع الذي هو معنى إمكان الممكن وتصور المحمول الذي هو معنى كونه محوجاً إلى السبب(ضروري)، يحكم به بديهة العقل بعد ملاحظة النسبة بينهما، ولذلك يجزم به(الصبيان) الذين لهم أدنى تمييز ألا ترى أن كفتي الميزان إذا تساوتا لذاتيهما وقال قائل: ترجحت إحداهما على الأخرى بلا مرجح من خارج لم يقبله مميز، وعلم بطلانه بديهة، فالحكم بأن أحد المتساويين لا يترجح على الآخر إلا بمرجح مجزوم به عنده بلا نظر وكسب، وهذا معنى كون الإمكان محوجاً إلى السبب (بل) الحكم بالاحتياج إلى المتساويين إلى المرجح (مركوز في طباع البهائم) أيضاً، (ولذلك) تراها (تنفر من صور الخشب) فإنه لما كان وجود الصوت وعدمه متساويين بالنسبة إلى ذات الصوت تخيلت البهائم من رجحان وجوده على عدمه أن هناك مرجحاً رجحه عليه فنفرت وهربت منه (قلنا: ذلك أي نفورها لحدوثه لا لإمكانه) فإنه لما حدث الصوت بعد عدمه تخيلت البهائم أن لا بد له من محدث لا أنها تخيلت تساوي طرفي الصوت، وأن لا بد هناك من مرجح.
Third Observatory - Fourth Goal: On the investigations of the contingent in essence.
Its proof has two methods: the first is the claim of necessity. For the contingent is that whose two sides are equal (i.e., its existence and non-existence with respect to its essence). And the meaning of its being (i.e., the being of possibility which is that equality) necessitating the contingent to the cause is that one of its two sides does not incline towards the other except by something other than the contingent that inclines one of them over the other. And the judgment after their conceptualization (i.e., the conceptualization of the subject which is the meaning of the possibility of the contingent and the conceptualization of the predicate which is the meaning of its being necessitating to the cause) is necessary; the mind judges by it intuitively after observing the relationship between them. For this reason, even children who have the slightest discernment affirm it. Do you not see that if the two pans of a scale are equal in themselves and someone says: 'One of them inclined over the other without an external determining factor,' no discerning person would accept it, and its falsity is known intuitively. So the judgment that one of two equals does not incline over the other except by a determining factor is certain for him without reflection and acquisition. And this is the meaning of possibility being necessitating to the cause. Rather, the judgment of the need of two equals for a determining factor is also ingrained in the nature of beasts. For this reason you see them shying away from wooden figures. For when the existence and non-existence of sound were equal with respect to the essence of sound, the beasts imagined from the inclination of its existence over its non-existence that there was a determining factor that inclined it, so they shied away and fled from it. We said: That is, their shying away is due to its occurrence, not to its possibility. For when the sound occurred after its non-existence, the beasts imagined that it must have an originator, not that they imagined the equality of the two sides of sound and that there must be a determining factor.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the contingent's need for a cause. It is a definition and proof combined. The author states that the proof has two methods; the first is the claim of necessity. He defines the contingent as that whose two sides (existence and non-existence) are equal with respect to its essence. The meaning of possibility being necessitating to the cause is that one of the two sides does not incline over the other except by something external that inclines it. The judgment that an equal needs a determining factor to incline is necessary: after conceptualizing the subject (possibility) and predicate (being necessitating), the mind judges intuitively. To illustrate, he gives the example of a balance scale: if the pans are equal, no one would accept that one inclines without an external factor. Even children judge this. He then adds that this judgment is also ingrained in beasts: they flee from wooden figures because they imagine that the sound must have a cause, though the author responds that their flight is due to the sound's occurrence, not its possibility. The passage thus establishes the intuitive necessity of the principle that an equal needs a determining factor.

Key Terms
الممكن
الإمكان
الضرورة
الترجيح
السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the judgment that a contingent needs a cause is a derived conclusion from other premises.
Correction: Al-Jurjani explicitly states that the judgment is necessary (daruri) and the mind judges it intuitively (badiha) after conceptualizing the subject and predicate, without any need for reasoning or acquisition.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the meaning of 'imkan being necessitating to the cause'?
A) That the contingent has no cause
B) That one of its two sides does not incline over the other except by an external determining factor
C) That the contingent is necessary in itself
D) That the contingent is impossible

Key Takeaway
The contingent's need for a cause is a necessary, self-evident judgment understood intuitively after conceptualizing its possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says that even children intuitively know that two equal pans cannot tip without an external weight. When you consider your own existence—the fact that you are alive right now—do you see it as a self-evident sign that there must be a Cause? Or do you treat your existence as if it were self-sufficient? What would change in your daily life if you truly lived with this intuitive knowledge?
?
Imagine your life as a scale: before you existed, the pans were equal. Something tipped the balance to bring you into existence. Who or what was that external weight? Have you ever genuinely thanked that Cause for bringing you into existence? If not, what is stopping you from doing so today?
?
If the need for a cause is a necessary, self-evident truth known by the mind intuitively, then ignoring it requires suppressing a basic rational insight. Are there areas of your life where you act as if you are the ultimate cause—where you ignore your contingency? What concrete step can you take this week to acknowledge your dependence on the Cause of causes?

The Beast's Instinct and the Objection
Illustration from beasts and response to objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani uses the instinct of beasts to further illustrate the necessity of a cause, and how the objection that beasts fear wooden figures due to occurrence rather than possibility is resolved.

Core Idea
Even beasts instinctively recognize that a balanced state needs an external cause, but their reaction is due to the onset of sound, not the equality.

In this lesson, we examine the second illustration al-Jurjani provides: the behavior of beasts shying away from wooden figures. He argues that this shows the judgment of needing a cause is ingrained in nature. However, he also addresses a potential objection: that the beasts' fear is actually due to the occurrence of sound, not the possibility, and clarifies the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the claim that the judgment is ingrained in beasts.
2
Analyze the beast example
The beasts hear a sound from a wooden figure and flee because they imagine there is a cause for the sound. Al-Jurjani uses this to support his argument.
3
Understand the objection and response
The objection says beasts flee because of the occurrence of sound, not its possibility. The response is that the beasts imagine a cause for the occurrence, which still implies a need for a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثالث - المقصد الرابع في أبحاث الممكن لذاته
إثباته منهجان: الأول دعوى الضرورة، فإن الممكن ما يتساوى طرفاه) أي وجوده وعدمه بالنظر إلى ذاته( ومعنى كونه) أي كون الإمكان الذي هو ذلك التساوي( محوجاً) للممكن( إلى السبب أنه لا يترجح أحد طرفيه) على الأخر( إلا لأمر) مغاير للممكن( يرجح أحدهما على الآخر، والحكم بعد (تصورهما) أي تصور الموضوع الذي هو معنى إمكان الممكن وتصور المحمول الذي هو معنى كونه محوجاً إلى السبب(ضروري)، يحكم به بديهة العقل بعد ملاحظة النسبة بينهما، ولذلك يجزم به(الصبيان) الذين لهم أدنى تمييز ألا ترى أن كفتي الميزان إذا تساوتا لذاتيهما وقال قائل: ترجحت إحداهما على الأخرى بلا مرجح من خارج لم يقبله مميز، وعلم بطلانه بديهة، فالحكم بأن أحد المتساويين لا يترجح على الآخر إلا بمرجح مجزوم به عنده بلا نظر وكسب، وهذا معنى كون الإمكان محوجاً إلى السبب (بل) الحكم بالاحتياج إلى المتساويين إلى المرجح (مركوز في طباع البهائم) أيضاً، (ولذلك) تراها (تنفر من صور الخشب) فإنه لما كان وجود الصوت وعدمه متساويين بالنسبة إلى ذات الصوت تخيلت البهائم من رجحان وجوده على عدمه أن هناك مرجحاً رجحه عليه فنفرت وهربت منه (قلنا: ذلك أي نفورها لحدوثه لا لإمكانه) فإنه لما حدث الصوت بعد عدمه تخيلت البهائم أن لا بد له من محدث لا أنها تخيلت تساوي طرفي الصوت، وأن لا بد هناك من مرجح.
Third Observatory - Fourth Goal: On the investigations of the contingent in essence.
Its proof has two methods: the first is the claim of necessity. For the contingent is that whose two sides are equal (i.e., its existence and non-existence with respect to its essence). And the meaning of its being (i.e., the being of possibility which is that equality) necessitating the contingent to the cause is that one of its two sides does not incline towards the other except by something other than the contingent that inclines one of them over the other. And the judgment after their conceptualization (i.e., the conceptualization of the subject which is the meaning of the possibility of the contingent and the conceptualization of the predicate which is the meaning of its being necessitating to the cause) is necessary; the mind judges by it intuitively after observing the relationship between them. For this reason, even children who have the slightest discernment affirm it. Do you not see that if the two pans of a scale are equal in themselves and someone says: 'One of them inclined over the other without an external determining factor,' no discerning person would accept it, and its falsity is known intuitively. So the judgment that one of two equals does not incline over the other except by a determining factor is certain for him without reflection and acquisition. Commentary
This passage presents the first proof for the contingent's need for a cause. It is a definition and proof combined. The author states that the proof has two methods; the first is the claim of necessity. He defines the contingent as that whose two sides (existence and non-existence) are equal with respect to its essence. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the beasts' flight proves they judge by the principle of possibility.
Correction: Al-Jurjani clarifies that the beasts fear the sound because it occurred, not because they conceptualize the equality of its two sides.

Checkpoint
Why do beasts shun wooden figures according to al-Jurjani's response?
A) Because they need a cause for the sound
B) Because they imagine the sound's occurrence must have an originator
C) Because they recognize the equality of sound's existence and non-existence
D) Because they fear the wood itself

Key Takeaway
The instinct of beasts confirms that a change needs a cause, though it is the occurrence, not possibility, that prompts their reaction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a recent event in your life—good or bad—that seemed to come out of nowhere. Did you instinctively search for a cause? How does this innate response point you to the Creator who is the ultimate cause of all things? What would it mean for your faith if you denied this instinct?
?
When you witness something happening without an obvious cause, does your heart immediately turn to Allah as the originator? Or do you fall into heedlessness, attributing events to chance or coincidence? Reconcile the beast's instinct with your own daily reliance on causes. Are you more or less aware than a beast of the need for a determining factor?
?
The objection in this lesson reminds us that even correct instincts can be misattributed. In your own life, have you ever mistaken a secondary cause for the primary one—like giving more weight to your own efforts than to Allah's will? How can this passage adjust the direction of your gratitude and reliance?

Defending the Necessity of the Judgment Against an Objection
Al-Jurjani answers the objection that the judgment of need is not like obvious primary truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani defends the necessity of the judgment that possibility necessitates a cause against the objection that it is not as obvious as primary truths like 'one is half of two.'

Core Idea
Necessary judgments differ in obviousness due to abstraction and familiarity, not certainty.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a strong objection: if the judgment that possibility necessitates a cause is truly necessary and primary, why does it not feel as obvious as 'one is half of two'? The answer reveals a subtle point about how the conceptual grasp of terms and habitual exposure affect our perception of obviousness, while the underlying certainty remains the same.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The opponent argues that all primary truths are equally obvious and universally agreed upon. Understand the force of this objection: if your judgment is truly primary, there should be no difference in obviousness or disagreement.
Notice the opponent's assumption: primary truths have no variation.
2
Follow al-Jurjani's answer
The answer distinguishes between the degree of certainty (which is equal) and the ease of abstracting the terms or habitual familiarity. Some necessary truths become more apparent through frequent use.
The difference is not in the judgment itself but in our cognitive access to it.
3
Consider the final point on disagreement
Even primary truths can be denied by a few people, as some have denied all basic axioms. Therefore, disagreement does not disprove necessity.
This reinforces that the judgment can be necessary even if some oppose it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو كان الحكم بأن الإمكان محوج إلى السبب المؤثر (ضرورياً) أولياً كما زعمتم لم يكن بينه وبين قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين فرق)، إذ لا تفاوت بين الأوليات ( ولم تختلف فيه أيضاً (العقلاء)؛ لأن بداهة عقولهم حاكمة به حينئذ قلنا : قد مر جوابه ( وهو أن الفرق والتفاوت ليس باعتبار الجزم واحتمال النقيض، بل هو للتفاوت في تجريد الطرفين أو للإلف والعادة بسبب كثرة وقوع تصور طرفي أحد الضروريين دون تصور طرفي الآخر، وأنه يجوز أن يخالف في البديهي قوم قليل كيف وقد أنكر طائفة البديهيات رأساً.
For if it is said: If the judgment that possibility necessitates the effective cause were necessary and primary as you claim, there would be no difference between it and our saying 'one is half of two,' since there is no difference among primary truths, nor would rational people differ about it, because then the intuition of their minds would judge it. We said: Its answer has already passed, which is that the difference and variation are not with respect to certainty and possibility of the opposite, but rather it is due to variation in the abstraction of the two terms, or due to familiarity and habit because of the frequent occurrence of the conceptualization of the two terms of one of the necessary judgments without the conceptualization of the two terms of the other, and that it is possible for a few people to oppose a primary truth — how so, when a group has outright denied primary truths?
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues: if the judgment 'possibility necessitates the cause' were primary and necessary as claimed, there would be no difference between it and obviously primary statements like 'one is half of two,' since all primary truths are equally obvious, and rational people would not disagree about it. The answer: the difference is not in the degree of certainty or possibility of the opposite, but in the ease of abstraction of the two terms, or due to familiarity and habit because some necessary judgments are more frequently conceptualized. Moreover, it is possible for a few people to oppose a primary truth, as some have even denied primary truths outright.

Key Terms
الضروري
الأولي
البديهي
الفرق
التجريد
الإلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That all necessary judgments must be equally and immediately obvious to everyone, with no variation in apparent obviousness.
Correction: The author explains that the difference in perceived obviousness is not due to a difference in certainty but due to variation in how easily the terms of the judgment are abstracted or how familiar they are through habitual use.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the apparent difference in obviousness between the judgment of need and statements like 'one is half of two'?
A) The judgment of need is actually less certain
B) The difference is due to the degree of certainty of each
C) The difference is due to variation in abstraction or familiarity with the terms
D) The judgment of need is not a necessary truth

Key Takeaway
The apparent difference in obviousness among necessary truths is due to variation in abstraction and familiarity, not in the degree of certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a truth you hold that you consider obvious. Is its obviousness due to genuine necessity or simply because you are familiar with it? How would your certainty hold up if you stripped away all your habits and assumptions?
?
The passage notes that even primary truths have been denied. Is there a truth you have avoided accepting because it requires a level of abstraction you find uncomfortable? What would it take for you to truly grasp it?
?
When you encounter someone who disagrees with a necessary truth of faith, do you attribute it to bad faith or ignorance? This lesson suggests the obstacle may be lack of abstraction or familiarity. How can you compassionately help them see what you see, without dismissing their struggle?

Defending Self-Evidence: Degrees and Denials
Al-Jurjani responds to the claim that the need for a determinant is a self-evident first principle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's argument that the need for a determinant is a self-evident principle even if it differs in clarity from mathematical axioms and is not universally accepted.

Core Idea
Self-evidence does not require universal agreement or equal clarity.

The objector tries to undermine the proof by claiming that if the need for a determinant were truly self-evident, it would be as clear and universally accepted as basic mathematical truths like 'one is half of two'. Al-Jurjani's reply shows that self-evident principles can vary in clarity and that denial by a minority does not negate their self-evidence. This passage is a key defense of rational principles in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objector's statement that if the judgment were self-evident, it would be like 'one is half of two' and would not be disputed by rational people.
Notice the objector assumes all self-evident principles are equally clear and universally accepted.
2
Identify the two claims in the objection
The objection makes two implicit claims: (1) all self-evident principles have the same degree of clarity, and (2) no rational person can disagree with a self-evident principle.
3
Read the reply and its first point
Al-Jurjani states that the difference in clarity is not about certainty but about how easily the two sides of the principle are conceptualized, often due to familiarity.
This explains why some self-evident principles seem more obvious than others.
4
Examine the reply's second point
He adds that even self-evident principles can be denied by a small group, and notes that a sect has denied all self-evident principles entirely.
This refutes the objection that disagreement disproves self-evidence.
5
Reflect on the defense
Consider how this answer preserves the self-evidence of the need for a determinant while accounting for disagreement and varying clarity.
The principle is still necessary and self-evident to those who properly conceptualize the terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( فإن قيل : لو كان الحكم بأن الإمكان محوج إلى السبب المؤثر (ضرورياً) أولياً كما زعمتم لم يكن بينه وبين قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين فرق)، إذ لا تفاوت بين الأوليات ( ولم تختلف فيه أيضاً (العقلاء)؛ لأن بداهة عقولهم حاكمة به حينئذ قلنا : قد مر جوابه ( وهو أن الفرق والتفاوت ليس باعتبار الجزم واحتمال النقيض، بل هو للتفاوت في تجريد الطرفين أو للإلف والعادة بسبب كثرة وقوع تصور طرفي أحد الضروريين دون تصور طرفي الآخر، وأنه يجوز أن يخالف في البديهي قوم قليل كيف وقد أنكر طائفة البديهيات رأساً .
(If it is said: If the judgment that possibility necessitates an effective cause were necessary and primary as you claim, there would be no difference between it and our statement 'one is half of two', for there is no discrepancy among primary principles, nor would the rational people differ regarding it, because the primacy of their intellects would then judge it. We reply: Its answer has already passed, namely that the difference and discrepancy is not with respect to certainty and the possibility of the opposite, but rather it is due to difference in isolating the two sides or due to familiarity and habit because of the frequent occurrence of the conceptualization of the two sides of one necessary [principle] without the conceptualization of the two sides of another, and it is permissible that a small group may oppose the self-evident—how so when a sect has denied self-evident principles entirely?)
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues that if the need for a determinant were self-evident, it would be like 'one is half of two' – universally accepted. The reply distinguishes degrees among self-evident principles: some are more conceptually isolating or more familiar than others, so they differ in clarity. Moreover, a few people have even denied obvious self-evident principles, so lack of universal agreement does not negate self-evidence.

Key Terms
ضروري
أولي
بديهي
الأوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All self-evident principles are equally clear and cannot be denied by anyone.
Correction: Self-evident principles vary in clarity due to differences in conceptual isolation and familiarity, and a few people may deny them without disproving their self-evidence.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the claim that the necessity of a determinant is a self-evident principle?
A) He admits it is self-evident and universally accepted.
B) He denies that it is a first principle.
C) He claims that self-evident principles can vary and a few have denied them.
D) He says it is not self-evident because rational people disagree.

Key Takeaway
The need for a determinant is a self-evident rational principle, even if it differs in obviousness from basic math and is not universally accepted; disagreement does not negate self-evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed that if something is self-evident, everyone must agree. When you face someone who denies the need for a Creator, do you doubt your own certainty? How can al-Jurjani's response strengthen your trust in rational proofs even when others reject them?
?
Al-Jurjani says that familiarity can make a self-evident truth seem more obvious. Think of a truth about God that you have become so accustomed to that you no longer feel its weight. How would your life change if you approached it as if for the first time, fully isolating its implications?
?
The passage notes that even the most basic principles can be denied. Is there a truth you avoid confronting because accepting it would demand a change in your behavior? What would it take for you to see it as self-evident and act accordingly?

The Second Proof: The Possible Essence Requires Equality
Al-Jurjani introduces the premise of the second proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the starting premise of the second proof for the need of a determinant: that the possible in itself is indifferent to existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
The possible essence itself does not favor existence or non-existence.

This lesson introduces a foundational premise in the proof for the existence of a determinant. Al-Jurjani presents the second method, beginning with the claim that the possible essence (al-mahiyya al-mumkina) by itself requires equality of existence and non-existence. Understanding this premise is crucial for grasping the subsequent argument that an external determinant must tip the balance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the English translation. Notice the key phrase 'مقتضية للتساوي' (necessitates equality).
The text cuts off, but the main premise is clear.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms: ماهية ممكنة (possible essence), تساوي (equality), وجود (existence), عدم (non-existence). Understand their meanings in this context.
3
Reflect on the premise
Ask yourself: Why must the possible essence be equal to both existence and non-existence? What would happen if it were not equal?
This sets up the need for a determinant.

This is the beginning of a new proof. The author introduces the second method for establishing the need for a determinant. The first way within this method states that the possible essence (al-mahiyya al-mumkina) necessitates equality—that is, it is indifferent to existence and non-existence. The text cuts off at 'fa-law' (so if...), which would likely complete the argument: if it were not for an external determinant, neither side would be preponderated.

The Simultaneity of Preponderance with Existence
Sanusi argues that preponderance is simultaneous with existence and mentally considered.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why preponderance (tarajjuḥ) is simultaneous with existence and a mental consideration, not a prior existent attribute.

Core Idea
Preponderance is simultaneous with existence, not prior.

This lesson tackles a key debate in kalam: does the possible's existence require a prior preponderance? Sanusi clarifies that preponderance is not a separate existent but a mental consideration that accompanies existence. Through dialectical and veridical arguments, he shows that no extra locus is needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
Read the passage and locate Sanusi's main assertion: 'بل يترجح مع الوجود' (Rather, it becomes preponderant with existence).
This is the key point of the lesson.
2
Follow the dialectical exchange
Notice how Sanusi presents an opponent's view and then rebuts it. The opponent holds that preponderance must be prior and existent.
The rebuttal shows that even if prior, it would be an attribute of existence.
3
Understand the veridical resolution
Sanusi declares 'والحق' (the truth) and explains that preponderance is a mental consideration, not an existent. This resolves the need for a separate locus.
The term 'أمر اعتباري' (mental consideration) is crucial.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters for causality: if preponderance is mental, no infinite regress of preponderances arises.
This connects to broader kalam discussions on causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجب وجوده إلى حد الوجوب حتى يوجد مبني على أنه محتاج إلى علة، وهو المتنازع فيه . بل يترجح مع الوجود، وحيئذ جاز أن يقوم الترجح بالممكن حال كونه موجوداً، فلا حاجة إلى محل آخر هو المؤثر. ( وأيضاً ) إن سلم كون الترجح سابقاً على وجود الممكن، فالترجح) السابق (صفة) الوجود، فلا يقوم بغيره لامتناع قيام الصفة بغير موصوفها، فلا يتصور قيامه بالمؤثر. والحق أن الترجح والوجوب المتجدد لا أن يكون موجوداً لأن العدمي قد يتجدد، بل هو أمر اعتباري يتصف به الممكن حال ما يكون متصوراً، فلا يستدعي محلاً آخر موجوداً في الخارج.
قوله : (بل يترجح مع الوجود) وماقيل من أن الترجح إذا كان موجوداً لا يكون مع الوجود، إذ قد تقرر أن الصفة الوجودية يجب تأخرها عن وجود الموصوف، فليس بشيء؛ لأن فيه اعترافاً ببطلان الاستدلال؛ لأنه حينئذ يكون قديماً بالأثر متأخراً عن وجوده .
قوله : ( فالترجح السابق الخ أي الترجح الذي سلم سبقته، فما قيل أن السبقة ينافي كونه صفة الوجود فيه اعتراف ببطلان الاستدلال .
قوله : ( والحق) الخ ما مر كان جواباً جدلياً مبنياً على تسليم كونه وجودياً، كما أثبته الخصم، وهذا الجواب تحقيقي فلذا قال : والحق .
قوله : (قد يتجدد ) كالعمى بعد البصر.
قوله : (اعتباري) إذ لو كان موجوداً في الخارج يلزم ترتب الترجحات الموجودة في الخارج وكون الحركة بعد السكون والعلم بعد الجهل موجودين ليس دائراً على تجددهما ولظهوره ترك ذكره .
قوله : ( يتصف به أي الاتصاف به انتزاعي، ومصداقه الأثر الموجود في الخارج.
Its existence must [reach] the point of necessity until it exists, based on its need for a cause, which is the disputed point. Rather, it becomes preponderant with existence, and then it is permissible for the preponderance to inhere in the possible while it is existing, so there is no need for another locus that is the effecter. And also, if the priority of preponderance over the existence of the possible is conceded, then the prior preponderance is an attribute of existence, so it cannot inhere in other than it due to the impossibility of an attribute subsisting in other than its subject, so its subsisting in the effecter is inconceivable. The truth is that preponderance and renewed necessity are not such that [the preponderance] be existent, because the non-existent may become renewed; rather it is a mental consideration with which the possible is described while it is conceived, so it does not require another existent locus in the external world. His statement: 'Rather, it becomes preponderant with existence' — and what is said that if the preponderance is existent it cannot be with existence, since it is established that the existential attribute must be posterior to the existence of the subject, is nothing; because it contains an acknowledgement of the invalidity of the inference; for then it would be prior in effect and posterior to its existence. His statement: 'the prior preponderance, etc.' i.e. the preponderance whose priority is conceded; so what is said that priority contradicts its being an attribute of existence contains an acknowledgement of the invalidity of the inference. His statement: 'the truth, etc.' — what passed was a dialectical answer based on conceding its being existential, as the opponent established, and this answer is veridical, so he said: the truth. His statement: 'may become renewed' like blindness after sight. His statement: 'mental consideration' — because if it were existent in the external world, there would follow the ordering of existent preponderances in the external world, and that motion after rest and knowledge after ignorance are existent, not revolving upon their renewal, and due to its obviousness he omitted its mention. His statement: 'is described by it' i.e. the description by it is derivative, and its referent is the existent effect in the external world.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents a definition and proof: the author addresses the claim that the possible requires a cause, specifically the idea that the preponderance (tarajjuḥ) of existence over non-existence must be prior to existence. The passage is an objection-and-answer followed by a resolution.

The argument begins by stating a premise: the possible's existence must reach necessity for it to exist, which is based on its need for a cause. This is the point under dispute. The author then asserts: 'Rather, it becomes preponderant with existence' (bal yatarajjaḥu maʿa al-wujūd). This means the preponderance is simultaneous with existence, not prior. Therefore, there is no need for a separate locus (the effecter) for the preponderance to inhere in, because it inheres in the possible while it exists.

Then an objection is considered: 'If it is conceded that preponderance is prior to the possible's existence, then that prior preponderance would be an attribute of existence, and an attribute cannot subsist in other than its subject. Therefore it cannot subsist in the effecter.' This refutes the opponent's position by showing that even if preponderance were prior, it would have to be an attribute of existence, not of the agent.

The correct view is then stated: 'The truth is that preponderance and renewed necessity are not such that they are existent; rather they are a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī).' The possible is described by this mental consideration while it is conceived, not in external existence. Thus no separate existent locus is required.

The commentary then expands each part:
- On 'bal yatarajjaḥu maʿa al-wujūd': The objection that an existential attribute must be posterior to the subject is rebutted because that would imply acknowledgement of the inference's invalidity.
- On 'the prior preponderance': Similarly, conceding priority does not help the opponent.
- On 'the truth': The earlier answer was dialectical (jadaliyy), conceding the opponent's assumption; this answer is veridical (taḥqīqī).
- On 'may become renewed': Examples like blindness after sight show that non-existential things can be renewed.
- On 'mental consideration': If preponderance were external, there would be an infinite regress of preponderances. The description is derivative (intizāʿī), and its external referent is the effect.

الترجح
الترجح مع الوجود
الوجوب المتجدد
الأمر الاعتباري
الانتزاعي
المصداق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Preponderance must be an existing attribute that precedes existence.
Correction: Preponderance is a mental consideration simultaneous with existence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view of preponderance (tarajjuḥ)?
A) It is an existent attribute that precedes existence.
B) It is a mental consideration simultaneous with existence.
C) It is an attribute of the effecter.
D) It is identical with the possible's essence.

Key Takeaway
Preponderance is a mental consideration (amr iʿtibārī) that accompanies existence, not a prior existent attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about why something in your life happens — a success, a trial, an opportunity — do you instinctively look for a separate 'cause' or 'factor' that existed before the event? Preponderance as an Attribute of the Thing
A second refutation stating that preponderance is an attribute of the thing itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how preponderance is correctly attributed to a thing and how the objection is refuted.

Core Idea
Preponderance, as a fact about a thing, is an attribute of that thing itself.

This short passage presents a second refutation in a debate about preponderance. The opponent argues that even if preponderance is an attribute of existence, it still qualifies as an attribute of the thing. The author responds by referring to earlier refutations and then concedes a key point: the fact that a thing's existence preponderates is indeed an attribute of it. This clarifies the nuanced relationship between attributes and their subjects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the opponent's statement ('you say') and the author's response ('I say').
2
Understand the concession
Notice that the author does not completely reject the opponent's claim. Instead, he concedes that the fact of preponderance (being such that its existence preponderates) is an attribute of the thing. This nuance is crucial.
3
Connect to broader context
Reflect on why this debate matters. The nature of preponderance has implications for causality and divine attributes. This passage refines the discussion by distinguishing between preponderance as an attribute of existence and as an attribute of the thing.
Think about how attributes are assigned to subjects in theology.

Time's Need for a Cause: Possibility Not Origination
Demonstrates that need for a cause arises from possibility, using time as an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need of a possible thing for a cause is grounded in its possibility, not its temporal origination, as illustrated by the example of time.

Core Idea
Possibility alone necessitates a cause, even for a continually existing thing.

This lesson unpacks a key kalam argument that uses time as a test case. Time appears necessary because it is continuous, but upon analysis it is composed of passing instants, making it possible in itself. The argument shows that its need for an external cause arises from its possibility, not from any temporal beginning. This establishes a foundational principle: the need for a cause is based on the very nature of possibility, not on the fact of coming into existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the logical flow. Notice how the author sets up a contradiction to prove time's qualified necessity and then establishes its possibility.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The argument has three main moves: (1) showing that non-existence before or after time leads to a contradiction, (2) concluding time's qualified necessity, and (3) showing its intrinsic possibility due to composition from instants, leading to the final conclusion.
Look for the words 'ويجتمع الوجود والعدم' and 'فهو واجب' and 'وأنه ممكن لذاته'.
3
Understand the contradiction
The author argues that if time were non‑existent at some point and existent later, then at the moment of non‑existence time would also be existent (because time is continuous), which is absurd. This forces the conclusion that time cannot be non‑existent in the past or future.
4
Connect to the main principle
The final line states 'possibility is the cause of need for another, not origination'. Reflect on why this matters: even a thing that always exists still needs a cause because it is possible in itself.
This principle is central to the kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوده أو بعده ،فبزمان ) أي ( فيكون تقدم العدم على وجوده أو تأخره عنه بزمان لأن المتقدم إذا لم يمكن أن يجامع المتأخر كان التقدم زمانياً. ( ويجتمع الوجود والعدم ) ؛ لأن الزمان حال ما كان معدوماً كان موجوداً، فيجتمع وجوده وعدمه معاً، هذا خلف . (فهو ) أي الزمان لامتناع عدمه كذلك (واجب) مستمر وجوده دائماً، ) وأنه ممكن لذاته؛ لتركبه من آنات منقضية)، فلا يكون وجوبه لذاته، لما مر من استحالة تركب الواجب بالذات خصوصاً إذا كانت الأجزاء منقضية متعاقبة، ( فوجوبه بالغير، فيكون الإمكان علة الحاجة إلى الغير دون الحدوث، إذ لاحدوث هاهنا .
Its existence or after it, then by time, meaning the priority of non-existence over its existence or its posteriority to it by time, because the prior, if it cannot coexist with the posterior, is temporal priority. And existence and non-existence would coexist; because at the moment when it was non-existent it was existent, so its existence and non-existence coexist, which is absurd. So it, i.e., time, due to the impossibility of its non-existence in this way, is necessary, its existence continuous always, and that it is possible by itself due to its composition from passing instants, so its necessity is not by itself, as has passed concerning the impossibility of the composition of the necessary by itself, especially if the parts are passing and successive. So its necessity is from another, and thus possibility is the cause of the need for another, not origination, since there is no origination here.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a logical argument demonstrating that the need of a possible thing for a cause is due to its possibility, not its temporal origination. It uses time as an example. The argument proceeds as follows: First, it states that non-existence before or after time's existence would imply temporal priority or posteriority, because a prior thing that cannot coexist with a posterior is temporally prior. Second, if time were non-existent at some point and existent at another, then at the moment of its non-existence it would also be existent (because time is continuous), leading to a contradiction. Therefore, non-existence before or after time is impossible. Third, this impossibility of non-existence in a qualified sense makes time necessary in a qualified sense—continuously existent—but it is possible by itself because it is composed of passing instants, and the necessary by itself cannot be composed. Hence its necessity is from another (i.e., its continuous existence is due to an external cause), and its need for that cause is due to its possibility, not any origination in time. The conclusion is that possibility itself is the ground of needing a cause.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الحدوث
الوجوب بالغير
الآنات المنقضية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That only things which begin to exist (hadith) need a cause for their existence.
Correction: The argument shows that even a continuously existing thing like time, which has no temporal beginning, needs a cause due to its intrinsic possibility (imkan). Hence, the need for a cause is grounded in possibility, not origination.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, what is the basis for a thing's need for an external cause?
A) Its temporal origination (hadath)
B) Its being composed of parts
C) Its possibility (imkan) by itself
D) Its continuous existence

Key Takeaway
A thing's need for an external cause is rooted in its possibility (imkan), not in its having a temporal beginning (huduth).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If time itself depends on a cause for its continuous existence, what does that mean for your own existence? Are you not a possible being composed of moments? How does this realization affect your sense of dependency on Allah?
?
The author uses time as an example to show that even what seems permanent needs a cause. What in your life do you treat as self-sufficient? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?
?
If possibility alone necessitates a cause, then your very possibility as a created being cries out for a Creator. Have you silenced that cry with distractions? What would it mean to listen to it today?

Time's Simple Nature: Dialectical versus Veridical Proof
Explains that the premise of time's composition is dialectical and clarifies the simple nature of time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that time is composed of instants is only dialectical, and what the true nature of time (the flowing now) implies for theological reasoning about time.

Core Idea
Time is a simple flowing now, not composed of indivisible instants.

In this commentary passage, the author critically examines a philosophical argument that time is composed of instants (an-at). He shows that this premise is not accepted by philosophers and that the argument is only dialectical (ilzami), not veridical (tahqiqi). This leads to the conclusion that time is a simple continuous entity, known as the 'flowing now' (al-an al-sayyal). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct theological reasoning about time and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get an overview of the discussion. Note that it is a commentary analyzing a previous argument.
2
Identify the two types of proof
The passage distinguishes between ilzami (dialectical) and tahqiqi (veridical) proof. Find where this distinction is made and understand why the composition argument is only ilzami.
3
Understand the correct meaning of 'instants'
The passage reinterprets 'instants' (an-at) as actually indivisible parts of time, not as independent units. This clarifies the intended meaning.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The passage concludes that time is the 'flowing now' (al-an al-sayyal), a simple entity without composition. Reflect on how this contrasts with the composition view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( لتركبه من آنات الخ) لا يخفى أن هذه المقدمة باطلة عند الحكماء، لاستلزامه الجزء. فبناء هذا الاستدلال على قول الفلاسفة معناه استعمال مقدمة مسلمة عندهم فيها، لا أن مقدماته مسلمة عندهم هكذا قيل وليس بشيء، لأن الاستدلال حينئذ لا يكون إلزامياً؛ لبطلان هذه المقدمة عندهم ولا تحقيقيا لعدم حقية المقدمة الأولى في الواقع عند المستدل فالصواب أن يقال المراد بالآنات أجزاء الزمان الغير المنقسمة فعلاً، ومعنى تركيبه منها تحليله إليها وكونها حاصلة فيه بالقوة . قوله : ( فيكون إلخ) اللازم مما ذكر أن يكون الممكن الغير الحادث محتاجاً إلى الغير، ولا منه أن يكون الإمكان علة، إلا أن يبنى على عدم القول بعلة ما سوى الإمكان والحدوث. قوله : دون الحدوث أي لا يكون له مدخل أصلاً . وجه آخر في الرد على الخصم فإن قلت : الترجح وإن كان صفة للوجود إلا أن ترجح الشيء صفة لذلك الشيء، قلت : قد نبهنا فيما مر غير مرة على أن الشارح رد أمثال هذا في أول البيان من حواشي المطول . نعم كون الشيء بحيث يترجح وجوده صفة له . تتاليها من : أن قوله (واجب) مستمر (وجوده أشار بقوله : مستمر وجوده إلى أنه المراد بالوجوب، لا الوجوب الذاتي؛ لأن الواجب الذات ما يستحيل عدمه مطلقا والمستحيل هاهنا هو العدم المقيد بكونه قبل الموجود أو بعده . قوله: (لتركبه من آنات منقضية) فيه بحث؛ لأن عدم تركب الزمان من الآنات وعدم مسلمات الحكمة وكأنه أراد من قول الفلاسفة الذي جعله مبنى للدليل مجرد الزمان موجود يمتنع عدمه المقيد، لا أن كل مقدماته قول الفلاسفة، أو أراد بالآنات الأجزاء الغير المنقسمة خارجاً وإن انقسمت فرضاً ووهماً . وفيه بعد تسليم عدم التلازم بين الانقسام الفرضي والخارجي هاهنا أن تركب الزمان من تلك الأجزاء يمنع قدمه واستمرار وجوده؛ لأن تلك الآنات أجزاء له لا أفراد حتى يدعى قدمه بالنوع بمعنى أن فرداً من أفراده موجود دائماً، والتحقيق أن الزمان المدعى قدمه عند الفلاسفة الآن السيال، وهو أمر بسيط لا تركب فيه، كما سيأتي تحقيقه إن شاء الله تعالى فالصواب في بيان أنه ممكن لذاته بيان عدم استحالة عدمه مطلقاً وإن استحال عدمه المقيد، كما أشرنا إليه آنفاً .
His saying: (due to its composition from instants etc.) It is not hidden that this premise is false according to the philosophers, because it entails the part. So the construction of this proof upon the statement of the philosophers means using a premise conceded by them in it, not that all its premises are conceded by them. Thus it was said, but it is nothing, because then the proof would not be compelling (ilzami) due to the falseness of this premise according to them, nor would it be veridical (tahqiqi) due to the lack of truth of the first premise in reality according to the prover. So the correct thing is to say: by 'instants' is meant the parts of time that are actually indivisible, and the meaning of its composition from them is its analysis into them and that they are contained in it potentially. His saying: (so it is etc.) The necessary consequence of what was mentioned is that the non-originated possible is in need of another, and not that possibility is a cause, unless it is built on the denial of a cause other than possibility and origination. His saying: without origination means it has no entry at all. Another way in refuting the opponent: for if you say: preponderance is a quality of existence, but the preponderance of a thing is a quality of that thing. I say: we have pointed out multiple times before that the commentator refuted such things in the beginning of the Bayan from the glosses of al-Mutawwal. Yes, the fact that a thing is such that its existence is preponderant is a quality of it. Their contiguity from: that his saying (necessary) continuous (its existence) indicated by his saying 'continuous its existence' that what is meant by necessity is not essential necessity; because the necessary by itself is that whose non-existence is absolutely impossible, and what is impossible here is the non-existence qualified by being before the existent or after it. His saying: (due to its composition from passing instants) there is investigation in it; because the non-composition of time from instants is not a conceded matter of wisdom. It is as if he intended from the statement of the philosophers which he made the basis for the proof merely that time exists and its qualified non-existence is impossible, not that all its premises are statements of the philosophers. Or he intended by instants the parts that are externally indivisible, even if they are divisible by supposition and estimation. And there is in it, after conceding the lack of correlation between suppositional and external division here, that the composition of time from those parts prevents its eternity and the continuity of its existence; because those instants are parts of it, not individuals, so that one could claim its eternal existence by type meaning that an individual of its individuals always exists. The reality is that time, whose eternity is claimed by the philosophers, is the flowing now, which is a simple thing without composition, as its verification will come if God wills. So the correct thing in demonstrating that it is possible by itself is to demonstrate the non-impossibility of its non-existence absolutely, even if its qualified non-existence is impossible, as we referred to shortly before.
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that critically analyzes the argument about time's composition from instants. It states that the premise of composition is false according to the philosophers (al-hukama'), because it implies the existence of a part (which is problematic). The proof built on this premise is therefore only a dialectical argument (ilzami) using their own conceded premise, not a veridical proof (tahqiqi). The correct interpretation is that 'instants' refer to actually indivisible parts of time, and composition means potential analysis. The commentary then explains that the conclusion of the argument is that the non-originated possible needs another, but not that possibility is a cause unless one denies any other cause. It also addresses the meaning of 'necessary' here: it is not essential necessity but qualified continuous existence. After discussion, the commentator concludes that time is a simple flowing 'now' (al-an al-sayyal) without composition, and its possibility is shown by the absolute possibility of its non-existence, even if qualified non-existence is impossible.

Misconception: The argument that time is composed of instants is a direct veridical proof.
Correction: The commentary shows that this argument is only dialectical (ilzami) because the premise is false according to philosophers.

Time is simple and continuous. Your life is not a series of disconnected instants but a flowing stream. What is one thing you are procrastinating that you now realize you cannot postpone, because the 'now' is all you have? Make a specific commitment to act on it today.

Conditional Necessity and Essential Possibility
Defines conditional necessity and shows it does not negate essential possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how conditional necessity (necessity conditioned by a predicate) differs from essential possibility and why the former does not negate the latter.

Core Idea
Conditional impossibility does not erase essential possibility.

This lesson tackles a key distinction in kalam: when we say something is impossible under a certain condition, that doesn't mean it is impossible in itself. The author explains how the impossibility of influence (ta'thir) in the state of existence or non-existence is actually a conditional necessity, and why that leaves essential possibility intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation once. Notice how the author distinguishes between a conditional judgment and an unconditional one.
Focus on the terms 'darura bi-shart al-mahmul' and 'al-imkan al-dhati'.
2
Identify the two key terms
Identify 'conditional necessity' (darura bi-shart al-mahmul) and 'essential possibility' (imkan dhati). Understand that the first is a necessity that depends on a condition (like the effect's existence), while the second is about the essence itself, without attributes.
3
Follow the argument
The passage argues: the impossibility of influence under the condition of existence or non-existence is a conditional necessity. Why does this not negate essential possibility? Because essential possibility considers the essence alone, without attributes. Conditional impossibility tied to an attribute does not affect the essence's own possibility.
This is the core reasoning: essence vs. attributes.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Understand that conditionally necessary statements do not affect the essential nature of things. This is a tool for defending the possibility of influence (and thus creation by God) against objections.
This distinction is crucial in kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك) الذي ذكرتموه من استحالة التأثير حال الوجود أو حال العدم، ( ضرورة بشرط المحمول، فإن التأثير في وجود الأثر بشرط الوجود أو بشرط العدم محال، فسلب التأثير في الوجود مثلا ضروري بشرط اتصاف الأثر بالوجود أو العدم، ومثل ذلك يسمى ضرورة بشرط المحمول، (وهو) أي هذا المذكور أعني الضرورة المشروطة بالمحمول ( لا ينافي الإمكان (الذاتي )؛ لأن الملاحظ فيه الذات دون مالها من الصفات، فامتناع التأثير بشرط إحدى هاتين الصفتين لا ينافي إمكانه بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن في زمان كل واحدة منهما
That which you have mentioned, of the impossibility of influence (ta'thir) in the state of existence or in the state of non-existence, is a necessity with a condition of the predicate (darura bi-shart al-mahmul). For influence in the existence of the effect with the condition of existence or with the condition of non-existence is impossible. So the negation of influence in existence, for example, is necessary with the condition that the effect be characterized by existence or non-existence. And such a thing is called 'necessity with a condition of the predicate'. And it, i.e. this mentioned thing, namely the necessity conditioned by the predicate, does not contradict essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati), because in it the essence is considered without its attributes. So the impossibility of influence under the condition of one of these two attributes does not contradict its possibility with respect to the essence of the possible in the time of each one of them.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'necessity with a condition of the predicate' (darura bi-shart al-mahmul) and applies it to the first shubha (doubt) about the impossibility of influence (ta'thir) in the state of existence or non-existence. The author explains that the claim of impossibility is actually a conditional necessity: it is impossible for influence to occur under the condition that the effect is already existent or non-existent. However, such conditional impossibility does not negate the essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) of the possible entity (al-mumkin). The reasoning is that essential possibility considers the essence (dhat) itself, abstracted from any attributes. Therefore, the impossibility of influence when a specific condition (like existence or non-existence) is attached does not entail that influence is impossible when considering the essence alone without conditions. This is a key distinction in kalam: a conditional judgment does not affect the unconditional nature of the essence.

الإمكان الذاتي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that impossibility under any condition negates all possibility.
Correction: The passage shows that conditional impossibility (e.g., under the condition of existence) does not negate essential possibility, which considers the essence without conditions.

Checkpoint

B) Because the predicate is always true.

D) Because the condition is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Conditional necessity does not eliminate essential possibility because the latter considers the essence independently of any conditions.

Refining the First Doubt: Where Does Influence Occur?
Refined analysis and solutions to the first shubha on causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the refined analysis resolves the first doubt about causality by distinguishing between conditional and temporal interpretations of the disjunction, and by offering alternative solutions involving an intermediate time or priority.

Core Idea
Influence applies to the essence of the possible, not conditioned by existence or non-existence.

The first doubt presented a disjunction: influence must occur either in existence or non-existence, and both seem impossible. But this disjunction can be interpreted in different ways. In this passage, the author provides a refined analysis (tahrir) that clarifies the logical options and offers multiple solutions, thereby saving the possibility of causal influence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the key term 'تحرير' (refinement) which signals a precise logical analysis.
2
Identify the two interpretations of the disjunction
The passage distinguishes between two ways to understand the phrase 'influence either in existence or non-existence': conditional (bi-shart) or temporal (fi zaman). Find where each is introduced.
3
Understand the denial of exhaustive division
Under the conditional interpretation, the exhaustive division (hasr) is rejected because influence can apply to the essence of the possible without any condition. See how the author phrases this.
The key is that influence is on the essence (dhat), not conditioned by existence or non-existence.
4
Explore the alternative answers
After the temporal interpretation, the passage presents two alternative answers: the intermediate time (zaman al-wasita) and the priority of influence over the effect. Examine each and note how they avoid combining existence and non-existence.
These answers show the flexibility of the kalam position on causality.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how each solution effectively answers the original objection. Write a short note in your own words summarizing the main point.
The original objection claimed influence is impossible in both states; the refinement shows this is not exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحريره أن يقال قولك التأثير إما حال الوجود أو حال العدم وكلاهما باطل إن أردت به التأثير إما بشرط الوجود أو بشرط العدم فالحصر ممنوع فإن التأثير في ذات الممكن من حيث هو لا بشرط الوجود، ولا بشرط العدم، وإن أردت به أنه في زمان الوجود أو زمان العدم اخترنا أنه في زمان الوجود كما مر . ومنهم من أجاب بأن التأثير في زمان الخروج من العدم إلى الوجود، وليس ذلك زمان الوجود ولا زمان العدم بل في زمان الواسطة بينهما. ومن النافين للواسطة من جوز تقدم التأثير على حصول الأثر فقال : التأثير حال العدم في آن وحصول الأثر في آن آخر يعقبه وليس في ذلك اجتماع الوجود والعدم أصلاً.
And its refinement is that it is said: Your statement 'influence either in the state of existence or in the state of non-existence, and both are invalid' – if you mean influence either with the condition of existence or with the condition of non-existence, then the exhaustive division is denied, for influence occurs in the essence of the possible insofar as it is, not with the condition of existence nor with the condition of non-existence. And if you mean that it is in the time of existence or the time of non-existence, we choose that it is in the time of existence, as has passed. And some have answered that influence is in the time of coming out from non-existence to existence, and that is not the time of existence nor the time of non-existence, but rather in an intermediate time between them. And among those who deny the intermediate, some allowed the priority of influence over the occurrence of the effect, saying: influence is in the state of non-existence in an instant, and the occurrence of the effect is in another instant following it, and there is no combination of existence and non-existence at all.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage provides the refined analysis (tahrir) and complete solution to the first shubha. It begins by dissecting the original disjunction: 'influence either in the state of existence or non-existence'. The author clarifies that if this disjunction is interpreted as conditional (i.e., with the condition of existence or non-existence), then the exhaustive division is rejected because influence can occur without either condition, specifically with respect to the essence of the possible entity (dhat al-mumkin) considered in itself—neither with the condition of existence nor with the condition of non-existence. If, however, the disjunction is interpreted temporally (i.e., in the time of existence or the time of non-existence), then the answer is that influence occurs in the time of existence, as previously mentioned. The passage then discusses alternative answers: some scholars posit an intermediate time (zaman al-wasita) between non-existence and existence, during which influence occurs. Others, who deny the existence of such an intermediate, allow the influence to precede the effect in time, such that influence occurs in an instant of non-existence and the effect occurs in a subsequent instant, with no simultaneity of existence and non-existence. This solution avoids the original objection that influence is impossible in both states.

Key Terms
تحرير (tahrir)
الحصر (al-hasr)
زمان الواسطة (zaman al-wasita)
تعاقب (ta'aqub)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The disjunction 'influence either in existence or non-existence' is exhaustive and both options are impossible, so no influence can occur.
Correction: The refinement shows that the disjunction is not exhaustive when interpreted conditionally (influence can apply to the essence unconditionally), and even temporally, influence can occur in the time of existence or in an intermediate/prior instant.

Checkpoint

A) The exhaustive division is denied because influence can occur without any condition on the essence.
B) Both are impossible, so no influence is possible.
C) Influence occurs in an intermediate time.
D) Influence occurs in the time of non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The first doubt is resolved by showing that influence applies to the essence of the possible unconditionally, and even if temporally considered, it occurs in the time of existence or in an intermediate/prior instant, avoiding the problem.

What would it mean for you to live today fully aware that His power is constantly acting on your essence, regardless of your state?
?

Third Doubt: Need and Effectivity as Non-Existential Attributes
Al-Iji presents a doubt and its answer about need and effectivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of infinite regress against need and effectivity is answered by treating them as non-existential relational attributes.

Core Idea
Non-existential attributes describe reality without causing regress.

This lesson tackles a critical objection in kalam: if need and effectivity were real, they'd require further need and effectivity—an infinite regress. Al-Iji's answer shows that these attributes are non-existential (ʿadamiyya) and relational (iʿtibāriyya), like impossibility and non-existence, which describe things without demanding a real foundation. Let's work through the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage has two clear parts: the doubt and the answer.
2
Identify the structure of the doubt
Notice how the doubt is presented: if need existed, it would need a subject, leading to another need—infinite regress. The same for effectivity. Then conclude that if they don't exist, no description of need or effectivity remains.
The conclusion is presented as the opponent's desired outcome.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer denies the premise that non-existential attributes require real existence to be predicated. Instead, they are relational (iʿtibārī). See the analogy with impossibility and non-existence.
Think about how 'impossible' describes a thing without implying a real property.
4
Reflect on the key insight
The core move: non-existential attributes describe things 'in themselves' (fī nafs al-amr) without needing a real instantiation, thus blocking the regress.
This distinguishes between real existential properties and merely relational descriptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة: الحاجة والمؤثرية لو وجدتا في الخارج تسلسل، وذلك لأن الحاجة لو وجدت لاحتاجت إلى الموصوف بها، إذ لا يتصور قيامها بذاتها، فللحاجة حاجة أخرى فينقل الكلام إلى حاجة الحاجة، وكذا المؤثرية لو وجدت لاحتاجت إلى مؤثرية أخرى؛ إذ يستحيل كونها واجبة بذاتها، وإذا لم تكونا موجودتين لم يكن الممكن متصفاً بالحاجة إلى سبب، لا لإمكانه ولا لغيره، ولم يكن شيء متصفاً بالمؤثرية في الممكن أصلاً، وهو المطلوب. والجواب: أنه لا يلزم من كونهما أمرين عدميين اعتباريين انتفاؤهما عن غيرهما، بمعنى أن لا يكون الشيء في نفس الأمر محتاجاً ومؤثراً، فإن الأمور العارضة العدمية تتصف بها الأشياء في أنفسها، كالامتناع والعدم، فإنهما وصفان اعتباريان لا وجود لهما في الخارج، مع أن الممتنع والمعدوم متصفان بهما قطعاً.
The third doubt: Need and effectivity if they existed in the external [world] would entail infinite regress. That is because if need existed, it would need that which is described by it, since it is inconceivable that it subsists by itself. So need has another need, and then the discussion is transferred to need of need. Likewise for effectivity: if it existed, it would need another effectivity, since it is impossible for it to be necessary by itself. And if they are not existing, then the possible would not be described as needing a cause, neither due to its possibility nor anything else, and nothing would be described as having effectivity in the possible at all – which is what is desired. And the answer: That their being two non-existential, relational (iʿtibāriyyān) things does not necessitate that they are absent from other than themselves, meaning that the thing in itself is not needy and not effective. For non-existential accidental attributes, things are described by them in themselves, like impossibility and non-existence, for they are two relational descriptions that have no existence in the external [world], yet the impossible and the non-existent are described by them without doubt.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents the third doubt (shubha) regarding need (ḥāja) and effectivity (muʿthiriyya) in the context of investigations of the possible per se. The doubt argues that if need and effectivity were real attributes existing in the external world, they would themselves require further need and effectivity, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul). The reasoning is that need cannot subsist by itself; it must inhere in something, so it needs that thing, generating another need. Similarly for effectivity—it cannot be self-necessary, so it would require another effectivity. Consequently, if they are not existent, no possible would be described as needing a cause or having effectivity, which is the desired conclusion for the opponent. The answer (jawāb) is that need and effectivity are non-existential (ʿadamiyya) and relational (iʿtibāriyya) attributes. Such attributes do not require real existence to be predicated of things; things can be described by them in themselves, just as impossibility (imtināʿ) and non-existence (ʿadam) are relational descriptions with no external existence, yet the impossible and the non-existent are described by them. Therefore, no infinite regress follows.

Key Terms
حاجة (ḥāja)
مؤثرية (muʿthiriyya)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
عدمية (ʿadamiyya)
اعتباري (iʿtibārī)
امتناع (imtināʿ)
عدم (ʿadam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that non-existential attributes are mere mental fictions with no real descriptive power.
Correction: The passage clarifies that non-existential attributes like need and effectivity truly describe objects in themselves (fī nafs al-amr), even though they have no external existence, just as impossibility describes the impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the third doubt, why do need and effectivity not lead to infinite regress?
A) Because they are existential attributes that require nothing beyond themselves.
B) Because they are non-existential relational attributes, so they do not need further instances.
C) Because they are self-subsistent and thus require no cause.
D) Because the possible does not actually have need or effectivity.

Key Takeaway
Need and effectivity are non-existential relational attributes that describe things in themselves, blocking the infinite regress objection.

?
The doubt suggests that if need were a real existent, it would lead to endless regress—but Allah has no need. When you find yourself relying on created things as ultimate causes, are you effectively treating them as if they possessed existential effectivity? Objection on Need and Effectivity: Existential vs Non-Existential
An objection and its concise answer referring to prior arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection against the attributes of need and effectivity is formulated as a dilemma and then deflected by referencing earlier kalām arguments.

Core Idea
Every objection in kalām is an opportunity to deepen the argument, not a refutation.

This lesson presents a typical dialectical move in kalām: an objection (fa-in qīla) that tries to trap the previous position. The objector argues that need and effectivity must be either existential or non-existential, and both lead to problems. But the answer is already prepared in earlier discussions. We will see how the objection is constructed and why it is not fatal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Understand the flow of objection and answer.
The answer is only one sentence at the end.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The objection says need and effectivity must be either existential or non-existential. Identify the arguments against each horn.
Existential leads to infinite regress; non-existential leads to a contradiction.
3
Note the references to prior arguments
The objection references 'the preceding rule' (al-ḍābiṭ al-mutaqaddim) and 'what was mentioned concerning necessity' (al-wujūb). These connect to earlier lessons.
The answer also says 'you have already known the answer from what we indicated.'
4
Analyze the answer
The answer does not repeat the full refutation but simply says: criticize the proof of either horn with the defect already identified.
This shows the objection is not new; it reuses earlier flaws.
5
Reflect on the dialectical method
Notice how kalām proceeds by raising objections and then answering them, often by showing the objection is already handled. This trains the mind to see connections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لو ثبتت الحاجة والمؤثرية لشيء واتصف ذلك الشيء بهما، فإما وجوديتان وإما عدميتان؛ إذ لا مخرج عنهما. ويبطل كل بما عرفت. أما إبطال الوجودية فلزوم التسلسل لأنهما من الأنواع المتكررة التي عرف حالها في الضابط المتقدم، وأما العدمية فبأن يقال: هما نقيضا اللاحاجة واللامؤثرية العدميتين، على قياس ما مر في الوجوب. وقد عرفت الجواب عن ذلك فيما أشرنا إليه فيما مر من أجوبة الشبهة وهو أن يقدح في دليل الوجودية أو دليل العدمية بما عرف فيه من الخلل.
For it might be said: If need and effectivity are established for something and that thing is described by them, then they are either existential or non-existential, since there is no way out from these two. And each is invalidated by what you have known. As for invalidation of the existential: due to the necessity of infinite regress because they are among the recurrent types whose case was known in the preceding rule. As for the non-existential: by saying they are the contradictories of the two non-existentials, non-need and non-effectivity, analogous to what was mentioned concerning necessity. And you have already known the answer to that from what we indicated in the preceding answers to the doubt, which is to criticize the proof of the existential or the proof of the non-existential with the defect known therein.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (fa-in qīla) that attempts to corner the previous answer. The objection argues that if need and effectivity are truly predicated of something, they must be either existential (wujūdiyya) or non-existential (ʿadamiyya)—there is no third category. Then it claims both options are false. The existential option leads to infinite regress (tasalsul) because they are recurrent types (anwāʿ mutakarrara) as established in a previous rule (ḍābiṭ). The non-existential option fails because they would be the contradictories (naqīḍ) of non-need and non-effectivity, which are themselves non-existential, leading to a similar problem as in the case of necessity (wujūb) discussed earlier. The answer (al-jawāb) is brief: the refutation of both horns has already been given in the earlier responses to the doubt—namely, one criticizes the proof for the existential or the proof for the non-existential by pointing out the flaw (khalal) already identified in each. Thus the objection is deflected by reference to prior arguments.

عدمية (ʿadamiyya)
نقيض (naqīḍ)
أنواع متكررة (anwāʿ mutakarrara)
ضابط (ḍābiṭ)
وجوب (wujūb)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection presents a new, unanswered difficulty that undermines the previous argument.
Correction: The passage shows that the objection has already been addressed in earlier material; the answer simply refers to those refutations.

Checkpoint
In the objection, what is the claimed problem with the existential option for need and effectivity?
A) It contradicts the rule of the possible.
B) It leads to infinite regress because they are recurrent types.
C) It requires them to be self-subsistent.
D) It cannot be predicated of anything.

Key Takeaway
This objection is not a refutation but a standard dialectical move that has already been answered in earlier arguments; the key is to see how kalām connects objections to prior principles.

Objection on Ontological Status of Need and Effectiveness
The author presents an objection and its answer regarding the ontological status of need and effectiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection against the ontological status of need and effectiveness is structured and how it is answered by attacking the proof of either existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
The objection forces a false dichotomy; the answer dismantles the proofs behind each option.

This passage presents a common theological objection (shubha) concerning the attributes of need and effectiveness. The objector argues that these attributes, if real, must be either existent or non-existent, and both options seem problematic. The author then provides the response, pointing out that the objection can be refuted by showing the weakness in its own proofs. Understanding this dialectical structure sharpens your ability to evaluate arguments in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Notice the structure: if need and effectiveness are established, then they must be either existent or non-existent. The objector claims both are invalid.
Pay attention to the dichotomy presented.
2
Examine the two arguments
The invalidity of existence is argued by infinite regress; of non-existence by analogy to necessity. Understand each argument's reasoning.
The infinite regress argument relies on the concept of repeated types.
3
Learn the answer
The answer is to attack the proof of either existence or non-existence, using a known defect. Reflect on how this avoids the dichotomy.
The answer is not to choose one option, but to show the proofs are flawed.

فإن قيل : لو ثبتتا ) أي لو ثبتت الحاجة والمؤثرية لشيء واتصف ذلك الشيء بهما، فإما وجوديتان وإما عدميتان )؛ إذ لا مخرج عنهما . For if it is said: If they are established, i.e., if need and effectiveness are established for something and that thing is described by them, then either they are existent or non-existent; there is no way out. As for the invalidity of being existent, it is by necessary infinite regress because they are of the repeated types whose state has been known in the aforementioned principle. As for being non-existent, it is said: they are the negations of non-need and non-effectiveness, analogous to what passed regarding necessity. Commentary
This passage presents an objection (shubha) concerning the ontological status of need (haja) and effectiveness (mu'aththiriyya).
