The imaginary third case and why it is not a real division
Objection and answer about a third logical division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a thing that necessitates both existence and non-existence is not a valid rational division, and how it differs from the impossible.

Core Idea
A thing cannot logically necessitate both existence and non-existence.

Sanusi addresses a potential objection: why didn't the earlier division of the possible include a third category—something that requires both existence and non-existence? This imaginary case might seem plausible at first glance, but intellectual analysis shows it cannot be a genuine division. In this passage, the author explains the difference between this hypothetical case and the impossible, which is a legitimate division.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage carefully. It follows a question-answer format. Identify the objection and the author's reply.
2
Understand why the third case is invalid
Notice that the author says the third case is only imagined at first sight, not permissible in division according to the intellect. Contrast it with the impossible, which is a valid division even though it cannot exist.
The key is that the impossible is conceivable as a category, whereas a thing that necessitates both existence and non-existence is not even conceivable.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'جائز القسمية'
This term means 'permissible in division'—i.e., a legitimate category for rational classification. The impossible is permissible in this sense, but the third case is not.
This shows that kalam categories are based on rational conceivability, not actual existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : هاهنا قسم آخر وهو ما يقتضي الوجود والعدم لذاته فلم لم يتعرضوا له في التقسيم؟ قلت هذا القسم يتوهم في بادي الرأي، وليس بجائز القسمية عند العقل أصلاً بخلاف الممتنع، فإنه جائز القسمية، بل واجبها، وإن كان ممتنع الوجود في نفسه، فما يقال من أن هذا القسم داخل في الممتنع لا يقبل أصلاً، كذا نقل من الشارح
For if you say: There is here another division, that which necessitates existence and non-existence by itself; why did they not treat it in the division? I say: This division is imagined at first sight, but it is not permissible in division according to the intellect at all, unlike the impossible, for it is permissible in division, indeed necessary, even if it is impossible in itself. So what is said that this division is included in the impossible is not acceptable at all. Thus it is transmitted from the commentator.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objector points out a missing division: what if a thing necessitates both existence and non-existence by itself? The answer is that such a thing is only imagined at first glance; it is not a valid division of the possible because it is inconceivable. In contrast, the impossible (that which necessitates non-existence) is a valid division, even though it is impossible in reality. The claim that this third case falls under the impossible is rejected.

Key Terms
القسمة
الممتنع
الجائز القسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that a thing that necessitates both existence and non-existence is a legitimate logical category similar to the impossible.
Correction: The passage clarifies that such a thing is not even conceivable as a division; it is not permissible in rational classification at all, whereas the impossible is a permissible division.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the division of something that necessitates both existence and non-existence not valid?
A) Because such a thing is impossible and thus falls under the impossible.
B) Because the intellect does not permit such a division at all.
C) Because it is a possible thing like any other.
D) Because it is only a theoretical category.

Key Takeaway
A thing that logically necessitates both existence and non-existence is intellectually inconceivable and therefore not a valid rational division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are confronted with a situation that seems logically contradictory in your faith, do you accept the limits of the intellect, or do you stubbornly insist on a false resolution? Recall a specific instance and how you handled it.
?
This lesson warns against embracing an imagined division that the intellect deems invalid. Is there an idea or belief you hold that, if you honestly examine it, strains against clear rational principles? What would it cost you to let it go?
?
How often do you seek a middle ground where none exists, especially in matters of aqeedah? Commit to one area where you will submit to what the intellect affirms even if it feels uncomfortable.

Non-Existence Preference for Transient Possibles Refuted
Refutation of non-existence preference for transient possibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that non-existence is intrinsically preferable for transient things like motion and time is flawed.

Core Idea
Transient things lack persistence by essence, not by preference.

Some theologians argued that non-existence is intrinsically better for transient things like motion and time, because otherwise they would persist forever. In this passage, Sanusi presents and refutes this view by distinguishing existence from persistence and showing that these things are not receptive to persistence due to their essential nature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Focus on the claim and the refutation.
Note the terms 'العدم', 'الممكنات السيالة', 'القارة'.
2
Identify the claim and its basis
The claim is that non-existence is preferable for transient possibles. The basis is that if not, they would persist. Understand why persistence is assumed impossible.
Transient things like motion are non-stable by nature.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation distinguishes existence from persistence. Even if a transient thing exists, it does not entail persistence. Its essence requires cessation and renewal, so it cannot persist regardless.
The phrase 'مع تساوي نسبتها إلى أصل الوجود والعدم' means these things are equally related to the origin of existence and non-existence, so no intrinsic preference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومنهم من جوز ذلك) أي كون أحد طرفيه أولى به لذاته ( فقال طائفة : العدم أولى بالممكنات السيالة ) أي غير القارة ( كالحركة والزمان ) والصوت وعوارضها ؛ إذ لولا أن العدم أولى بها لجاز بقاؤها ورد بأن الوجود غير البقاء وغير مستلزم له وماهية تلك الأشياء لاقتضائها التقضي والتجدد ليست قابلة للبقاء مع تساوي نسبتها إلى أصل الوجود والعدم. وقال بعضهم : العدم أولى بالممكنات كلها؛ إذ يكفي لها في عدمها انتفاء جزء من علتها . قوله : (لجاز بقاؤها ) فإن عورض بأنه لو كان العدم أولى لما وجد ، يجاب بأن الوجود لعلة خارجة لا ينافي أولوية العدم لذات الممكن. وأما العدم الطارئ فليس بعلة خارجة، بل هو لذاته، فيناسب ادعاء أولوية العدم للذات في الجملة، وإن كان مردداً بما ذكره الشارح
His statement: 'And among them is one who allowed that' — i.e., that one of its two sides is intrinsically preferable — so a group said: Non-existence is preferable for transient possibles — i.e., non-stable ones — like motion and time, and sound and their accidents; for if non-existence were not preferable for them, their persistence would be possible. It is refuted by the fact that existence is not persistence nor does it entail it, and the essences of those things, due to their requiring cessation and renewal, are not receptive to persistence, with their equal relation to the origin of existence and non-existence. And some said: Non-existence is preferable for all possibles; for it suffices for their non-existence that a part of their cause is absent. His statement: 'for their persistence would be possible' — for if it is objected that if non-existence were preferable, they would not exist, the answer is that existence from an external cause does not contradict the preference of non-existence for the essence of the possible. As for temporary non-existence, it is not from an external cause, but rather from the essence itself, so it is appropriate to claim preference of non-existence for the essence in general, even if it is disputed by what the commentator mentioned.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two views that claim non-existence is intrinsically preferable for some or all possibles. First, a group says non-existence is preferable for transient things like motion, time, and sound, because otherwise they would persist. The refutation is that existence is not the same as persistence, and the essences of these things require cessation and renewal, so they are not capable of persistence regardless of preference. Second, another group says non-existence is preferable for all possibles, because a part of the cause's absence suffices for non-existence. An objection is then raised: if non-existence were preferable, they would never exist. The answer distinguishes: existence from an external cause does not contradict the intrinsic preference for non-existence. However, temporary (actual) non-existence is intrinsic, so the claim of preference is plausible in a general sense, though the commentator disputes it.

Key Terms
العدم
الممكنات السيالة
القارة
البقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That transient things would necessarily persist if non-existence were not preferable.
Correction: The refutation shows that persistence is not entailed by existence; the essences of transient things prevent persistence regardless of preference.

Checkpoint
How does the passage refute the claim that non-existence is preferable for transient possibles?
A) By stating that motion is not a possible.
B) By arguing that existence and persistence are different, and transient things cannot persist due to their essence.
C) By agreeing that non-existence is preferable but that existence can still occur.
D) By saying that the claim is based on a misunderstanding of possibility.

Key Takeaway
The claim that non-existence is preferable for transient things fails because existence does not imply persistence, and these things are by nature incapable of persisting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume many things in your life will persist—your health, your relationships, your career. But this lesson shows that transient things exist by nature without persistence. If existence does not guarantee persistence, then what you hold onto most tightly may slip away not by chance but by its very nature. Are you clinging to something that is by definition temporary? How will you let go of the illusion that it must persist?
?
Non-existence is not 'preferable' for transient things; they simply are not receptive to persistence. Think of a moment of joy or a painful hardship that passed. Neither persisted because of what they were. If you treat every transient blessing as though it owes you persistence, you set yourself up for anguish. What is one transient blessing you have been unwilling to release? Can you accept it as a gift that was never meant to stay?
?
The refutation hinges on the fact that existence and persistence are not the same. You might feel that if something exists, it 'should' continue—that's a deep-seated assumption. But the author says no: existence does not imply persistence. Where in your own life have you mistaken existence for a promise of permanence? How does that mistaken belief manifest in your anxiety, your planning, your attachment?

Preference of Non-Existence for All Possibles Refuted
Objection and answer regarding preference of non-existence for all possibles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the claim that non-existence is preferable for all possibles and how the answer distinguishes intrinsic preference from actual existence.

Core Idea
Intrinsic preference for non-existence does not contradict existence from an external cause.

Some theologians extended the preference of non-existence to all possibles, arguing that absence of a single cause part suffices for non-existence. An objection arises: if non-existence is preferable, how can anything exist? The answer distinguishes between intrinsic preference and the actuality of existence from an external cause. This lesson unpacks that nuanced response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: the claim, then the objection and answer.
The phrase 'وقال بعضهم' introduces a new claim.
2
Understand the second claim
The claim is that non-existence is preferable for all possibles, because a missing part of a cause suffices for non-existence. Reflect on why this might seem plausible.
This is a strong claim that goes beyond transient things.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection states that if non-existence were preferable, things would never exist. The answer: existence from an external cause does not contradict the intrinsic preference. Temporary non-existence (actual) is intrinsic, but existence can still be caused externally.
The answer preserves the possibility of existence while maintaining the claim of preference in a qualified sense.
4
Consider the commentator's dispute
The passage ends by noting that this claim is disputed by the commentator. Reflect on the nuance: the preference claim is plausible in general terms but not universally accepted.
This shows the debate was not settled.

Correction: Existence from an external cause is possible despite intrinsic preference for non-existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in the passage, how can things exist if non-existence is preferable?
A) They cannot; the claim is false.
B) Existence from an external cause does not contradict the preference.
C) They exist by chance, overriding the preference.
D) Non-existence is not actually preferable; it only seems so.

Key Takeaway
The claim that non-existence is preferable for all possibles can be defended by distinguishing intrinsic preference from external causation, but it remains disputed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If non-existence is intrinsically preferable for all possibles, why do you cling so tightly to your own existence? What does your attachment to worldly life reveal about your trust in the Hereafter?
?
The passage says existence comes from an external cause—not from yourself. Do you live as if your very existence is a gift from the Creator, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? How would your day change if you truly felt that each moment of existence is a favor?
?
Consider a time when something you deeply desired was absent—perhaps a job, health, or a relationship. Did you resent its non-existence, or did you recognize that non-existence was natural to its essence? How can this lesson transform your gratitude for what you have?

First Opinion: Non-Existence Prior for Flowing Possibles
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the first opinion on intrinsic priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first opinion that claims non-existence is intrinsically prior for flowing possibles, its supporting argument, and its refutation.

Core Idea
Intrinsic priority cannot be proven even for special categories of possibles.

Al-Jurjani begins by presenting a group who argue that non-existence is more appropriate for certain transient entities like motion and time. They reason that if non-existence were not prior, these things could persist. However, this argument is refuted by distinguishing existence from persistence and by noting the essential nature of these entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first opinion
Read the Arabic text and its translation to grasp the claim and its justification.
Pay attention to the specific category of 'flowing possibles'.
2
Identify the refutation
Notice how al-Jurjani refutes the claim by showing that existence is not persistence and that these entities are not receptive to persistence.
3
Understand the objection and answer
Consider the objection that if non-existence were prior, these things would never exist, and the answer that existence from an external cause does not negate intrinsic priority.
This clarifies the distinction between intrinsic and extrinsic priority.
4
Reflect on the principle
This refutation reinforces the principle that no side of the possible is intrinsically prior.
Intrinsic priority cannot be established by external factors.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومنهم من جوز ذلك) أي كون أحد طرفيه أولى به لذاته ( فقال طائفة : العدم أولى بالممكنات السيالة ) أي غير القارة ( كالحركة والزمان ) والصوت وعوارضها ؛ إذ لولا أن العدم أولى بها لجاز بقاؤها ورد بأن الوجود غير البقاء وغير مستلزم له وماهية تلك الأشياء لاقتضائها التقضي والتجدد ليست قابلة للبقاء مع تساوي نسبتها إلى أصل الوجود والعدم. قوله : (لجاز بقاؤها ) فإن عورض بأنه لو كان العدم أولى لما وجد ، يجاب بأن الوجود لعلة خارجة لا ينافي أولوية العدم لذات الممكن. وأما العدم الطارئ فليس بعلة خارجة، بل هو لذاته، فيناسب ادعاء أولوية العدم للذات في الجملة، وإن كان مردداً بما ذكره الشارح. وقال بعضهم : العدم أولى بالممكنات كلها؛ إذ يكفي لها في عدمها انتفاء جزء من علتها ولا يتحقق وجودها إلا بتحقق جميع أجزاء عللها، فالعدم أسهل وقوعاً. وهو مردود بأن سهولة عدمها بالنظر إلى غيرها لا يقتضي أولويته لذاتها. وقال بعضهم : إذا وجد المؤثر وعدم الشرط كان الوجود أولى بالممكن من العدم، وإذا عدم المؤثر ووجد الشرط كان العدم أولى به، وقيل إذا وجد العلة فالوجود أولى وإلا فالعدم، وفسادهما ظاهر لأن تلك الأولوية مستندة إلى الغير لا إلى ذات الممكن.
His saying: (And among them is one who permitted that) i.e., that one of its two sides is more appropriate for it in itself. So a group said: Non-existence is more appropriate for flowing possibles (al-mumkinat al-sayyala) i.e., non-stationary ones like motion and time and sound and their accidents. For if non-existence were not more appropriate for them, their persistence would be possible. And it is refuted by [the fact] that existence is not persistence nor does it entail persistence, and the essences of those things, due to their requiring passing away and renewal, are not receptive to persistence while their relation to the origin of existence and non-existence is equal. His saying: (their persistence would be possible) For if it is objected that if non-existence were more appropriate, they would not exist, the answer is that existence due to an external cause does not contradict the priority of non-existence for the essence of the possible. As for adventitious non-existence, it is not with an external cause, but rather is in itself, so it suits the claim of priority of non-existence for the essence in general, even if it is doubted by what the commentator mentioned. And some said: Non-existence is more appropriate for all possibles, because for their non-existence it suffices that a part of their cause is absent, while their existence is only realized by the realization of all parts of their causes, so non-existence is easier to occur. And it is refuted by [the fact] that the ease of their non-existence with respect to other than them does not necessitate its priority for their essence. And some said: If the effect-causing factor exists and the condition is absent, then existence is more appropriate for the possible than non-existence; and if the effect-causing factor is absent and the condition exists, then non-existence is more appropriate for it; and it was said: If the cause exists, then existence is more appropriate, otherwise non-existence. And their corruption is apparent because that priority is based on other than it, not on the essence of the possible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage enumerates three opinions that attempt to assign intrinsic priority to one side of the possible, along with their refutations. First opinion: A group claims that non-existence is intrinsically prior for flowing (non-stationary) possibles like motion and time, because if non-existence were not prior, these things could persist. The refutation: Existence is not the same as persistence, and these entities are defined by renewal and are not receptive to persistence; their relation to existence and non-existence is equal. The commentary adds an objection: if non-existence were prior, they would never exist—answered by saying existence can come from an external cause without negating the intrinsic priority of non-existence for the essence. Second opinion: Non-existence is prior for all possibles because it is easier (fewer conditions). Refutation: Ease relative to external factors does not imply intrinsic priority in the essence. Third opinion: Existence or non-existence is prior depending on the presence of cause or effect-causing factor. Refutation: Such priority is based on external factors, not the essence itself. All these refutations reinforce the principle that intrinsic priority cannot be proven.

Key Terms
الممكنات السيالة
العدم أولى
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Flowing possibles intrinsically tend toward non-existence.
Correction: Their nature only prevents persistence; intrinsic priority is not established.

Checkpoint
How is the first opinion (non-existence prior for flowing possibles) refuted?
A) By showing that existence is the same as persistence
B) By showing that these entities are not receptive to persistence due to their nature
C) By showing that non-existence is impossible for them
D) By showing that they are eternal

Key Takeaway
The first opinion fails because it confuses the nature of transience with intrinsic priority for non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the fleeting moments of your day (like time, motion, sounds) as if they have an intrinsic right to non-existence, dismissing them as trivial? What would change if you saw each moment as equally poised between existence and non-existence, and thus a potential act of worship? Commit to recognizing at least one transient thing today as a gift from an external Cause.
?
Have you ever devalued a temporary relationship, a season of life, or a fleeting hobby because it was transient? The refutation shows transience does not imply intrinsic worthlessness. Is there something in your life you have marginalized because it will not last? Repent from that attitude and ask Allah to help you value each temporary blessing as a means of drawing nearer to Him.
?
The objection that 'if non-existence were prior, things would never exist' is answered by the external cause. Reflect on your own existence: do you live as if you have a right to exist, or do you recognize that your existence at this very moment is due to an external cause — Allah's will and providence? What would change in your daily gratitude if you woke up and thought 'I am here not by my own right, but by the will of my Creator'? Make a concrete commitment to say 'Alhamdulillah' with this realization.

Second Opinion: Non-Existence Prior for All Possibles
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the second opinion on intrinsic priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that claims non-existence is easier for all possibles due to fewer conditions, and its refutation.

Core Idea
Ease of occurrence relative to external factors does not imply intrinsic priority.

A second group extends the claim to all possibles, arguing that non-existence is easier because it requires fewer conditions. Al-Jurjani refutes this by showing that ease is relative to external factors, not the essence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and translation to understand the second opinion's argument.
Note the reasoning: fewer causes needed for non-existence.
2
Analyze the refutation
Al-Jurjani refutes by pointing out that ease relative to external factors does not prove intrinsic priority.
The key is distinguishing between intrinsic and extrinsic.

Third Opinion: Priority Based on Cause or Condition
Al-Jurjani presents and refutes the third opinion on intrinsic priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third opinion that assigns priority based on the presence or absence of cause/condition, and its refutation.

Core Idea
Priority based on external factors is not intrinsic priority.

Some argued that existence or non-existence becomes more appropriate for the possible depending on the presence or absence of a cause or condition. Al-Jurjani refutes this as merely extrinsic, not based on the possible itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the two versions
Read the passage containing both variants of the third opinion.
Notice the slight differences: one uses 'mu'aththir' and 'condition', the other uses 'cause'.
2
Understand the refutation
Al-Jurjani states that both are invalid because the priority is based on external factors, not on the essence of the possible.
This reinforces the central theme: intrinsic priority must come from the essence itself.

8 min
Proof Against Intrinsic Priority
Al-Jurjani provides the conclusive proof against intrinsic priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that intrinsic priority is impossible, and how this argument supports the necessity of an external creator.

Core Idea
Intrinsic priority leads to logical contradiction, proving it false.

Al-Jurjani presents a rigorous argument to show that the priority of one of two possible sides cannot be intrinsic—i.e., solely due to the essence. This proof is essential for demonstrating that the world requires an external cause (Allah). By analyzing the consequences of assuming intrinsic priority, the proof forces a contradiction, leaving no alternative but to reject it.

Guided Path
1
Clarify 'intrinsic priority'
First, understand what intrinsic priority means: the essence alone suffices for priority, not merely that the essence has a role. This clarification is crucial for the proof to work.
The text emphasizes that if the essence merely had an input, it wouldn't be 'intrinsic' in the strong sense.
2
Identify the three ways to negate it
The passage lists three methods scholars use to disprove intrinsic priority and notes that the author chooses the second method, avoiding the first due to difficulty.
This sets the stage for the main proof that follows.
3
Walk through the main proof step-by-step
Assume intrinsic priority for one side. Then consider the other side: if it becomes impossible, the possible turns into a necessary (conversion). If it does not become impossible, it can occur—either without a cause (impossible) or with a cause.
Each branch leads to a contradiction.
4
Examine the objection and its refutation
An objection suggests the cause of the other side occurring could be the absence of intrinsic priority. But this would make the absence of priority impossible, leading to the other side being impossible—contradicting the assumption that it occurs.
Thus, the priority must depend on an external cause, not the essence.
5
Conclude the impossibility
In all scenarios, the assumption of intrinsic priority leads to absurdity. Therefore, no side can be intrinsically prior, supporting the need for an external determinant.
This argument is foundational for proving the existence of a creator in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأنه أي كون أحد طرفيه (إلخ) اعلم أن معنى الأولوية لذاته، أن تكون الذات وحده كافية فيها، كما يشير إليه آخر كلام الشارح قدس سره في الاستدلال لا أن يكون للذات مدخل فيها إذ لا يمكن نفيها بهذا المعنى ضرورة مدخلية الذات فيها لكونها صفة لها فثبوت الأولوية الذاتية يستلزم كفاية الذات فيها وكذلك ثبوتها يستلزم كفايتها في وقوع الطرف الراجح فلذلك اكتفى القوم على نفيها فمن قال أن المقصود من نفي الأولوية الذاتية أن لا يلزم انسداد باب إثبات الصانع، ولهم في تحصيل هذا المطلب طرق : أحدها نفي الأولوية الناشئة الذات، عن وثانيها: نفي كفاية الذات في الأولوية وثالثها: أنه على تقدير التسليم لا تكفي الأولوية في وقوع الطرف الراجح والمصنف طوى الطريق الأول، لأن إثباته لا يخلو عن صعوبة وتصدي للطريق الثاني فقد ضل الطريق المستقيم قوله : ( وإلا أي وإن لم يمتنع إلخ) أي إن لم يمتنع الطرف الآخر جاز وقوعه، فإما أن يقع إلخ . قوله : ( وإما أن يقع بعلة) فإن قلت يجوز أن تكون تلك العلة عدم الأولوية الذاتية، فلا يتوقف ثبوت الأولوية للطرف الأول على عدم شيء آخر سوى ذات الممكن، حتى يلزم خلاف المقدر. قلت حينئذ يكون عدم تلك الأولوية ممتنعا لأن الأولوية مقتضى ذات الممكن فيكون وقوع الطرف الآخر ممتنعاً، فلا يكون الممكن ممكناً، هذا خلف. فلا بد أن تكون علته غير مستند إلى ذات الممكن، فيتوقف ثبوت أولوية الطرف الراجح على عدم تلك العلة، فلا تكون تلك الأولوية ناشئة عن الذات .قوله : (كان الوجود أولى بالممكن فيه منع ذكره الشارح في حاشية التجريد، وسيشير إليه هاهنا أيضاً، لأن العلة التامة للعدم حينئذ متحققة، وما وجد تمام علته أولى مما وجد بعض علته وإن كان هو الفاعل المؤثر. قوله : (لأن الطرف الآخر إن امتنع إلخ) حاصله : أنه يلزم على ذلك أحد الأمرين إما الانقلاب أو خلاف المفروض .
His saying: (And that it, i.e., that one of its two sides, etc.) Know that the meaning of priority of the essence is that the essence alone is sufficient for it, as is indicated by the end of the commentator’s speech, may his secret be sanctified, in the proof—not that the essence has an input into it, since it is impossible to negate it in this sense due to the necessity of the essence’s input into it because it is a property of it. So the affirmation of intrinsic priority entails the sufficiency of the essence in it, and likewise its affirmation entails its sufficiency in the occurrence of the preponderant side. Therefore the people sufficed with negating it. So whoever said that the purpose of negating intrinsic priority is to avoid closing the door of proving the Maker—and they have several ways to achieve this: one is negating priority arising from the essence; second is negating the sufficiency of the essence in priority; third is that, assuming it is conceded, priority is not sufficient for the occurrence of the preponderant side. The author folded the first way because proving it is not free from difficulty, and taking the second way has strayed from the straight path. His saying: (Otherwise, i.e., if it does not become impossible, etc.) i.e., if the other side does not become impossible, its occurrence is permissible, so either it occurs, etc. His saying: (Or that it occurs with a cause) For if you say: It is permissible that that cause be the absence of intrinsic priority, so the affirmation of priority for the first side does not depend on the absence of anything other than the essence of the possible, such that a departure from the assumption would follow. I say: In that case, the absence of that priority would be impossible because priority is a requirement of the essence of the possible; thus the occurrence of the other side would be impossible, and the possible would not be possible—this is a contradiction. Therefore its cause must not be based on the essence of the possible, so the affirmation of priority of the preponderant side depends on the absence of that cause, and thus that priority does not arise from the essence. His saying: (Existence was more appropriate for the possible in it) is a denial mentioned by the commentator in the gloss on al-Tajrid, and he will refer to it here as well, because the complete cause of non-existence is then realized, and that which finds its complete cause is more appropriate than that which finds part of its cause, even if that part is the effecting agent. His saying: (Because the other side, if it becomes impossible, etc.) Its summary is that one of two things necessarily follows: either conversion or a departure from the assumption.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents the conclusive proof that intrinsic priority of one side is false. The commentary first clarifies the meaning of 'intrinsic priority' (al-awlawiyya al-dhatiyya): it means that the essence alone is sufficient for the priority, not merely that the essence has a role. Affirming such priority entails that the essence is sufficient for the preponderant side to occur. Then it discusses various methods to negate such priority, noting that the author avoided one difficult method. The main proof is then restated: if one side is intrinsically prior, then consider the other side. If that other side becomes impossible due to the priority, then the possible becomes necessary (conversion). If the other side does not become impossible, it can occur. Its occurrence must be either without a cause (impossible because even the equal side would need a cause) or with a cause. If with a cause, one might object that the cause is the absence of intrinsic priority itself. But that would make the absence of priority impossible (since priority is required by the essence), leading to impossibility of the other side and thus the possible would not be possible. So the cause must be external, meaning the priority is not from the essence. Thus, intrinsic priority leads to contradiction in all cases. The commentary also includes a note on the denial that existence is more appropriate when its cause exists, as mentioned in the gloss on al-Tajrid.

Key Terms
الأولوية الذاتية
كفاية الذات
الطرف الراجح
الانقلاب
خلاف المفروض
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if the other side occurs with a cause that is the absence of intrinsic priority, the proof fails because the priority remains intrinsic.
Correction: The proof shows that such a scenario makes the absence of priority impossible, which would prevent the other side from occurring, thus contradicting the assumption that it does occur. Therefore, the cause must be external, meaning the priority is not intrinsic.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, if the other side occurs with a cause that is the absence of intrinsic priority, what follows?
A) The possible becomes necessary
B) The absence of priority becomes impossible, making the other side impossible
C) The possible is unchanged
D) The priority becomes external

Key Takeaway
Intrinsic priority is impossible because it logically forces either conversion of a possible into a necessary or dependence on an external cause, contradicting the assumption that priority is intrinsic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in a Creator. But have you ever considered whether your own priorities are intrinsic—meaning you follow them without questioning? This lesson shows that intrinsic priority leads to absurdity. Is there a belief or habit you hold that you've never questioned, assuming it is self-evident? Write down one such thing and commit to re-examining it today.
?
The proof shows that assuming something's essence alone makes it necessary leads to contradictions. In your life, what matters to you 'by essence'—without external reason? Could it be that you are treating something as intrinsic (like a relationship, a job, or a worldview) that actually depends on external causes? Are you willing to admit its contingency?
?
The scholars negated intrinsic priority to avoid closing the door to proving the Maker. Rethink: Is there any area of your faith or practice that you have treated as 'just the way it is' without seeking its deeper rational foundation? This lesson calls you to not accept even a concept as self-evident without scrutiny. Will you take one question about your faith seriously and investigate it with the same rigor shown here?

The Two Necessities of the Possible
Al-Jurjani explains the two necessities of the possible: prior from cause, posterior from existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the possible being is surrounded by two necessities: one from its cause prior to its existence, and one from its own existence posterior to it, and how these do not negate its essential possibility.

Core Idea
The possible is wrapped in two necessities, but they come from outside, not from itself.

This passage from al-Jurjani delves into the modal status of the possible being. It clarifies that when we say a possible thing exists, it is not merely 'possible' in a simple sense; it is necessitated in two ways. Understanding these two necessities is crucial for grasping the causal framework in kalam, and for seeing why the possible remains intrinsically possible despite being externally necessitated.

Guided Path
1
Read the source text
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the terms 'prior necessity' and 'posterior necessity'.
2
Identify the two necessities
The passage explicitly states that the existent possible has two necessities. Find them and note their sources: one from the cause, one from its own existence.
Notice the phrase 'وجوبان يحيطان بوجوده'.
3
Understand 'due to another'
The text says both necessities are 'بالغير' (due to another). Reflect on why this is important to preserve the intrinsic possibility of the possible.
This distinguishes between intrinsic and extrinsic modalities.
4
Apply to non-existent possible
The passage concludes with a parallel: the non-existent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities. Consider how this symmetry works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة التامة علة تامة فقد ثبت أن الأولوية وحدها غير كافية . فما لم يجب وجود علته بحيث يستحيل تخلفه عنها، لم يوجد وهو وجوبه السابق على الممكن عن وأخذه وجوده؛ لأنه وجب أولاً وجوده من علته فوجد ، ثم إنه إذا وجد فبشرط الوجود، معه يمتنع عدمه وإلا جاز اجتماع عدمه مع وجوده وأنه وجوبه اللاحق لوجوده، فإنه وجد أولاً فامتنع عدمه، ووجب وجوده . فله أي فللممكن الموجود وجوبان يحيطان بوجوده وهما بالغير؛ لأن الأول بالنظر إلى وجود العلة، والثاني بالنظر إلى وجود الممكن وأخذه معه . فلا ينافيان الإمكان الذاتي ؛ لأنه بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن مع قطع النظر عن كون علته موجودة، وكذا عن كونه موجوداً، وقس على ذلك حال الممكن المعدوم فإنه محفوف بامتناعين: أحدهما علة من عدم وجوده والثاني من عدمه.
The complete cause is a complete cause, so it is established that priority alone is insufficient. So as long as the existence of its cause has not become necessary such that it is impossible for it to be absent from it, it [the possible] does not exist, and that is its prior necessity [which comes] before the possible [exists] and is taken with its existence. Because first its existence from its cause became necessary, so it existed. Then when it exists, with the condition of existence, non-existence is impossible, otherwise its non-existence could coincide with its existence. And that is its posterior necessity [which comes] after its existence, for it existed first, then non-existence became impossible, and its existence became necessary. So for the existent possible there are two necessities that surround its existence, and both are due to another: the first is with respect to the existence of the cause, and the second is with respect to the existence of the possible and its being taken together with it. They do not contradict essential possibility, because it is with respect to the essence of the possible, disregarding whether its cause exists or whether it itself exists. And similarly, the non-existent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities: one pertains to the cause of its non-existence, and the other to its non-existence.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This lesson defines two key concepts: the prior necessity (وجوب سابق) and the posterior necessity (وجوب لاحق). The author explains that mere priority (أولوية) is not sufficient; the possible's existence requires that the cause be necessary in such a way that the effect cannot fail to occur when the cause is complete. This is the prior necessity – it precedes the existence of the effect. Then, once the effect exists, it becomes impossible for it to cease to exist as long as its cause persists (or as long as it exists conditionally). This is the posterior necessity. The existent possible is thus 'surrounded' by two necessities: one before its existence (from the cause) and one after (from its own existence). Both are 'due to another' (بالغير), meaning they are acquired from something else. These do not contradict the essential possibility (الإمكان الذاتي) of the possible, which is considered in itself, apart from any external considerations. Similarly, a non-existent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities: the impossibility of existence due to the lack of a cause, and the impossibility of non-existence if the cause is present. This passage is a key doctrinal point in kalam about the modal status of the possible.

Key Terms
الوجوب السابق
الوجوب اللاحق
الإمكان الذاتي
العلة التامة
الممكن الموجود
الممكن المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Necessity contradicts possibility; if something is necessary, it cannot be possible.
Correction: The passage explains that these necessities are extrinsic (بالغير) and do not negate the intrinsic possibility (الإمكان الذاتي) of the possible, which is considered in itself apart from external factors.

Checkpoint
What are the two necessities that surround the existent possible?
A) Prior necessity from the cause and posterior necessity from its own existence.
B) Necessity from essence and necessity from attributes.
C) Inherent necessity and acquired necessity.
D) Temporal necessity and eternal necessity.

Key Takeaway
The existent possible is necessitated from its cause (prior) and from its existence (posterior), yet remains intrinsically possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You exist right now, but your existence is contingent—dependent on countless factors outside your control. How does it feel to know that your very being is 'wrapped' in necessities from Allah's will and from your own existence? Does this make you more grateful or more anxious? How can you transform that feeling into deeper tawakkul?
?
The passage says the possible non-existent is surrounded by two impossibilities—one from the absence of its cause, one from its own non-existence. Think of a deep desire of yours that hasn't been fulfilled. In what ways might its non-existence be 'impossible' from Allah's wise decree? How should this affect your patience and your du'a?
?
If your essence is merely possible (imkān dhātī), then your value and purpose are not in yourself but in your relationship with the Necessary Being. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient, or are you constantly aware that your existence is a gift from outside? What would change today if you truly internalized that you are 'possible' and He is 'Necessary'?

Possibility as a Necessary Attribute of the Quiddity
Al-Jurjani proves possibility is a necessary attribute of the possible quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility is an inseparable property of the possible quiddity in Ash'ari metaphysics.

Core Idea
Possibility is not accidental; it is a necessary concomitant of every possible thing.

Al-Jurjani continues his analysis of rational judgments by examining possibility (الإمكان). He argues that possibility is not a mere intellectual consideration but an attribute that is intrinsic to the possible quiddity itself. This passage presents a proof by contradiction that possibility cannot be separated from the quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise, logical style typical of kalam works.
2
Identify the main claim and key terms
The passage begins with 'ورابعها أن الإمكان لازم للماهية الممكنة'. This is the claim. Identify the key terms: الإمكان (possibility), الماهية الممكنة (possible quiddity), لازم (necessary), and انفكاك (separation).
3
Walk through the proof by contradiction
The proof considers what would happen if possibility could be separated. Two cases: either possibility is removed from the quiddity (زوال) or it is acquired after not having it (حدوث). Each leads to a contradiction, proving the original claim.
Trace each case to understand why the quiddity would become impossible/necessary or vice versa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها أن الإمكان لازم للماهية الممكنة، لا يجوز انفكاكها عنه أصلاً ، وإلا جاز خلو الماهية عنه، فينقلب الممكن ممتنعاً وواجباً إن كان خلوها عنه بزواله عنها . أو بالعكس أي ينقلب الممتنع أو الواجب ممكناً إن كان خلوها عنه بحدوثه لها بعد ما لم يكن وأنه أي جواز خلوها عنه على أحد
And the fourth of them: that possibility is necessary for the possible quiddity; it is not permissible for it to be separated from it at all. Otherwise, it would be permissible for the quiddity to be free of it, so the possible would turn into impossible or necessary if its freedom from it is by its removal from it. Or the opposite, i.e., the impossible or the necessary would turn into possible if its freedom from it is by its occurrence to it after it was not. And that is, i.e., the permissibility of its freedom from it is upon one [of the two cases].
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This lesson presents the fourth research: that possibility (الإمكان) is a necessary attribute (لازم) of the possible quiddity (الماهية الممكنة). It cannot be separated from the quiddity at all. The proof is by contradiction: if the quiddity could be free of possibility, then either possibility is removed from it (زوال) or it acquires possibility after not having it. If possibility is removed from the possible, then the possible would become either impossible (if its quiddity is no longer possible) or necessary (if it gains necessity instead). Conversely, if the impossible or necessary were to acquire possibility, they would become possible. Thus, the possibility is inseparable; it is a necessary concomitant of the quiddity. This is a standard Ash'ari position that possibility is an attribute of the essence, not a mere mental consideration.

Key Terms
الإمكان
الماهية الممكنة
اللازم
الانفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think possibility is a relative or changeable property that a quiddity can gain or lose.
Correction: The passage shows that possibility is necessary (لازم) and cannot be separated from the quiddity at all. If it could, the quiddity would cease to be possible, which is impossible by definition.

Checkpoint
What would happen if a possible quiddity were separated from possibility?
A) It would become impossible or necessary.
B) It would remain possible.
C) It would become nonexistent.
D) It would become a substance.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is a necessary property of every possible quiddity; it cannot be removed or added.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your very existence is only possible, not necessary. How does accepting this contingency change your sense of dependence on the Necessary Being? Will you live today as if you are a necessary being or as the contingent being you truly are?
?
Consider something you desperately desire—maybe success, love, or security. Do you sometimes feel that this thing is necessary for your happiness? How does the truth that you are only possible—never necessary—free you from making created things into necessities?

The Two Obligations of the Existing Possible
Sanusi defines the two obligations of the possible and their relation to essential possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two obligations (prior and subsequent) that surround the existing possible being and why they do not contradict its essential possibility.

Core Idea
The existing possible is held by two obligations, both from another, while its essence remains possible.

Sanusi introduces a key distinction: an existing possible being is not merely possible; it is surrounded by two obligations—one before existence (due to its cause) and one after existence (due to its persistence). These obligations are not intrinsic but are 'through another' (bil-ghayr). Importantly, they do not negate the essential possibility of the being itself. This lesson lays the foundation for understanding how contingent beings relate to their causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the two obligations
Carefully read the Arabic passage from 'فله أي فللممكن الموجود وجوبان' to 'والثاني من عدمه'. Note how the author introduces the two obligations and the analogy with the nonexistent possible.
The passage uses a compact style; read the translation alongside.
2
Identify the prior and subsequent obligations
Find where the author defines the prior obligation (بالنظر إلى وجود العلة) and the subsequent obligation (بالنظر إلى وجود الممكن). Understand that they are both 'through another'.
3
Understand 'through another'
The obligations are not essential to the possible; they depend on the cause (prior) or on the possible's own existence (subsequent). Contrast this with essential necessity (wājib bi al-dhāt).
This is a cornerstone of kalam reasoning about contingency.
4
Consider the analogy with the nonexistent possible
The nonexistent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities, just as the existent is surrounded by two obligations. This symmetry reinforces the concept of 'through another'.
The analogy shows the structure applies to both existence and non-existence.
5
Reflect on compatibility with essential possibility
The author explicitly states that these obligations do not contradict essential possibility because essential possibility is considered in abstraction from the cause and existence. Ask yourself: Why is that abstraction necessary?
This is a key point that prevents misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فله أي فللممكن الموجود وجوبان يحيطان بوجوده وهما بالغير لأن الأول بالنظر إلى وجود العلة والثاني بالنظر إلى وجود الممكن وأخذه معه فلا ينافيان الإمكان الذاتي لأنه بالنظر إلى ذات الممكن مع قطع النظر عن كون علته موجودة وكذا عن كونه موجودا وقس على ذلك حال الممكن المعدوم فإنه محفوف بامتناعين: أحدهما علة من عدم وجوده والثاني من عدمه. قوله وهو وجوبه السابق أي سبقا ذاتيا لا زمانيا وإلا لكان حاصلا زمان العدم الذي هو العلة التامة فيلزم وجود العلة التامة وعدمها معا ويلزم أن يكون الممكن في زمان عدم العدم واجبا بالغير وممتنعا بالغير. قوله وجوبه اللاحق أي لحوقا ذاتيا لتحققه مع الموجود في زمان ثم إنه لم يظهر وجه لاعتبارهم هذا الوجوب وأي فائدة فيه. قوله وهو وجوبه السابق على وجوده فإن قلت كيف يتصور السبق مع أن الوجوب صفة للوجود قلت بل هو صفة للذات بالنسبة إلى الوجود فيكون كالإمكان في التأخر عن مفهوم الوجود لا عن تحققه ثم إن سبق الوجوب على الوجود ذاتي وسبق العدم عليه زماني فلا يرد أن الممكن قبل وجوده معدوم فهو ممتنع فكيف يكون واجبا بالغير مع تنافي الوجوب والامتناع الغيريين ولأن الوجوب صفة ثبوتية فكيف يجوز اتصاف الممكن به حال عدمه فإن قلت إذا لزم سبق الوجوب لم يتصور كون العلة التامة بسيطة في شيء من المواد لأن الوجوب السابق معتبر مع الفاعل حينئذ وقد جوزه الشارح فيما سيأتي قلت سيذكر جوابه هناك إن شاء الله تعالى.
So for the existing possible he has two obligations surrounding his existence, both through another, because the first is with respect to the existence of the cause, and the second is with respect to the existence of the possible and its being taken with it. They do not contradict essential possibility, because it is with respect to the essence of the possible regardless of whether its cause exists or whether it itself exists. And analogously, the nonexistent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities: one is a cause of its non-existence, and the second is from its non-existence. His words: 'And that is his prior obligation' means a priority in essence, not temporal; otherwise it would occur in time of non-existence which is the complete cause, so the existence of the complete cause and its non-existence would have to coincide, and the possible in the time of non-existence of the cause would have to be necessary through another and impossible through another. His words: 'His subsequent obligation' means a posteriority in essence because it is realized with the existent in time, but it is not clear why they consider this obligation and what benefit it has. His words: 'And that is his prior obligation over his existence' then you may say: How is priority conceived when obligation is an attribute of existence? I say: Rather it is an attribute of the essence in relation to existence, so it is like possibility in being posterior to the concept of existence, not to its realization. Then the priority of obligation over existence is essential, and the priority of non-existence over it is temporal, so it is not objected that the possible before its existence is nonexistent and thus impossible: how can it be necessary through another when necessity and impossibility through another conflict? And because obligation is a positive attribute, how is it permissible for the possible to be characterized by it while nonexistent? Then if you say: If the priority of obligation is necessary, then the complete cause cannot be conceived as simple in any material, because the prior obligation is then considered along with the agent, and the commentator has permitted that later. I say: The answer will be mentioned there, God willing.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the two obligations that encompass the existing possible being. The author first states that the existing possible has two obligations, both through another (bil-ghayr): one prior to existence (wujūb sābiq) and one subsequent to existence (wujūb lāḥiq). The prior obligation is with respect to the existence of the cause—i.e., once the complete cause exists, the effect must exist. The subsequent obligation is with respect to the existence of the possible itself—once it exists, its non-existence is impossible as long as the cause persists. These two obligations do not contradict essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī), because essential possibility is considered with abstraction from the existence of the cause and the existence of the effect itself. He then draws an analogy: just as the existing possible is surrounded by two obligations, the nonexistent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities: one from the non-existence of its cause, and the second from its own non-existence. The commentary that follows clarifies key points: the priority of the prior obligation is essential, not temporal; otherwise it would entail contradictions. The subsequent obligation is also essential, but its utility is questioned. Then an objection is raised: if obligation is an attribute of existence, how can it precede existence? The answer is that obligation is an attribute of the essence in relation to existence, not an attribute of existence itself. Thus its priority is essential, not temporal. The objection that the possible before existence is nonexistent and thus impossible is refuted by distinguishing between impossibility through another (due to lack of cause) and impossibility in itself. Another objection regarding the simplicity of the complete cause is deferred to later discussion.

Correction: The obligations are through another, not essential. The possible remains possible in itself; the obligations are conditional on external factors.

The existing possible being has two obligations (prior and subsequent) both through another, while its essential possibility remains intact.

Clarifications on Priority and Attribution of Obligation
Sanusi clarifies the nature of the prior obligation and answers objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the essential priority of the prior obligation, the answer to the objection that obligation cannot precede existence, and the distinction between different types of impossibility.

Core Idea
The prior obligation's priority is essential, not temporal, because obligation is an attribute of the essence in relation to existence.

Having introduced the two obligations, Sanusi now addresses potential objections. How can a prior obligation exist before the possible itself exists? How can the possible be both necessary and impossible at the same time? The answers involve subtle distinctions between essential and temporal priority, and between impossibility through another and impossibility in itself. This lesson unpacks these clarifications, which are vital for a precise understanding of kalam reasoning about contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification passage
Read the passage from 'قوله وهو وجوبه السابق' to the end. It contains the author's own commentary and objections with answers.
This is a more advanced, question-and-answer style section.
2
Understand essential priority
The prior obligation is prior 'in essence' (ذاتيا), not in time. This means it is logically prior, not temporally prior. The author explains why it cannot be temporal.
Temporal priority would lead to contradictions regarding the complete cause.
3
Grasp the objection and answer about obligation preceding existence
Objection: Obligation is an attribute of existence, so how can it precede existence? Answer: Obligation is an attribute of the essence in relation to existence, not of existence itself. So its priority is conceptual, not temporal.
This is a key metaphysical distinction.
4
Distinguish between the two impossibilities
The possible before existence is impossible through another (due to lack of cause), but it is not impossible in itself. This distinguishes it from the impossible in itself.
5
Note the deferral of the simple cause issue
A further objection about the simplicity of the complete cause and the prior obligation is deferred to a later section. This shows the author's systematic approach.
The answer is promised later (سيذكر جوابه هناك).

So for the existing possible he has two obligations surrounding his existence, both through another, because the first is with respect to the existence of the cause, and the second is with respect to the existence of the possible and its being taken with it. They do not contradict essential possibility, because it is with respect to the essence of the possible regardless of whether its cause exists or whether it itself exists. And analogously, the nonexistent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities: one is a cause of its non-existence, and the second is from its non-existence. His words: 'And that is his prior obligation' means a priority in essence, not temporal; otherwise it would occur in time of non-existence which is the complete cause, so the existence of the complete cause and its non-existence would have to coincide, and the possible in the time of non-existence of the cause would have to be necessary through another and impossible through another. His words: 'His subsequent obligation' means a posteriority in essence because it is realized with the existent in time, but it is not clear why they consider this obligation and what benefit it has. His words: 'And that is his prior obligation over his existence' then you may say: How is priority conceived when obligation is an attribute of existence? I say: Rather it is an attribute of the essence in relation to existence, so it is like possibility in being posterior to the concept of existence, not to its realization. Then the priority of obligation over existence is essential, and the priority of non-existence over it is temporal, so it is not objected that the possible before its existence is nonexistent and thus impossible: how can it be necessary through another when necessity and impossibility through another conflict? And because obligation is a positive attribute, how is it permissible for the possible to be characterized by it while nonexistent? Then if you say: If the priority of obligation is necessary, then the complete cause cannot be conceived as simple in any material, because the prior obligation is then considered along with the agent, and the commentator has permitted that later. I say: The answer will be mentioned there, God willing.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the two obligations that encompass the existing possible being. The author first states that the existing possible has two obligations, both through another (bil-ghayr): one prior to existence (wujūb sābiq) and one subsequent to existence (wujūb lāḥiq). The prior obligation is with respect to the existence of the cause—i.e., once the complete cause exists, the effect must exist. The subsequent obligation is with respect to the existence of the possible itself—once it exists, its non-existence is impossible as long as the cause persists. These two obligations do not contradict essential possibility (al-imkān al-dhātī), because essential possibility is considered with abstraction from the existence of the cause and the existence of the effect itself. He then draws an analogy: just as the existing possible is surrounded by two obligations, the nonexistent possible is surrounded by two impossibilities: one from the non-existence of its cause, and the second from its own non-existence. The commentary that follows clarifies key points: the priority of the prior obligation is essential, not temporal; otherwise it would entail contradictions. The subsequent obligation is also essential, but its utility is questioned. Then an objection is raised: if obligation is an attribute of existence, how can it precede existence? The answer is that obligation is an attribute of the essence in relation to existence, not an attribute of existence itself. Thus its priority is essential, not temporal. The objection that the possible before existence is nonexistent and thus impossible is refuted by distinguishing between impossibility through another (due to lack of cause) and impossibility in itself. Another objection regarding the simplicity of the complete cause is deferred to later discussion.

Misconception: Believing that the prior obligation means the possible is necessary in itself before existence, leading to a contradiction with its non-existence.
Correction: The prior obligation is only through another and is essential, not temporal. The possible's non-existence is also through another, so there is no self-contradiction.

D) Obligation is merely a mental concept with no real priority.

Key Takeaway
The prior obligation's priority is essential, not temporal, because obligation is an attribute of the essence in relation to existence, and the possible before existence is impossible through another, not in itself.

Possibility Necessary to Essence
Sanusi proves that possibility is necessary to the essence, else skepticism follows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility must be necessary for the essence of possible things, and why denying that leads to skepticism.

Core Idea
If possibility could detach from an essence, all rational certainty collapses into sophistry.

This lesson presents the central argument that possibility (imkān) is an inherent and inseparable attribute of any possible essence. The argument shows that if possibility were not necessary, it could change, undermining our confidence in rational judgments about what is necessary, impossible, or possible. Such a scenario leads to universal skepticism, which is self-evidently false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Identify the core claim: possibility is necessary to the essence.
Notice the conditional structure: if not necessary, then skepticism follows.
2
Follow the logical chain
Trace the argument: from the denial of necessity to loss of certainty, to skepticism, then rejection of skepticism as absurd.
3
Understand the justification
Why is skepticism impossible? Because the three modalities (necessity, impossibility, possibility) are grounded in essences and cannot be separated.
This is the key insight: essential attributes define the essence.

The two ways remove security from necessary truths, so confidence in the judgment of the intellect regarding the necessity of necessary things, the impossibility of impossible things, and the possibility of possible things is lifted due to the possibility of some of them turning into others at that time. That is an evident sophistry, because necessity, impossibility, and possibility that are based on the essences of things themselves cannot be conceived as being detached from them; otherwise those essences would not exist due to the negation of their requisites in themselves. His words: 'Rather occurring' because if it is not necessary for the essence, its departure from it is possible, so it is occurring; because everything possible that can depart from its subject is occurring, unlike if it is necessary, then it is a requisite. Commentary
This passage is a proof that possibility (imkān) is necessary (lāzim) for the essence of the possible, together with a refutation of skepticism and an objection-and-answer. The author begins with the argument: if possibility were separable from the essence (i.e., not necessary), then it could be detached *либо* change into something else. That would undermine certainty in the rational judgments about necessary, impossible, and possible things, leading to universal skepticism, which is evident sophistry. The reason is that necessity, impossibility, and possibility are based on the essences themselves and cannot be separated; if they could, the essences would not be what they are. Then an objection is raised: perhaps possibility is not necessary but rather arises accidentally (ḥādith). The answer is that the occurrence of possibility itself requires a cause. If that cause is something else, it leads to infinite regress; if no cause, then the denial of a Creator follows (since possibles could exist without a cause). The commentary then clarifies each part: the 'judgment of the intellect' refers to primary rational judgments that are self-evident, and the argument against skepticism is itself self-evident. The commentator notes that the proof that the three modalities are necessary to the essence is independent. The objection and answer are analyzed: the objection assumes that the cause of need is occurrence (ḥudūth) rather than possibility, but response shows it leads to regress or denial of a Creator. A further discussion: if possibility were necessary for the essence, it would not have another possibility, but that would lead to a problem because possibility as an attribute requires a subject and thus has its own possibility—this is a subtle point the author had previously addressed. Finally, the passage ends with a note that the occurrence of possibility must be possible itself.

سفسطة

لزوم الإمكان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that possibility is an accidental attribute that can be added or removed from an essence without affecting its identity.
Correction: Possibility is an essential attribute; if removed, the essence would cease to be a possible thing.

Checkpoint
What is the direct consequence, according to the argument, of denying that possibility is necessary for the essence?

B) Rational certainty is lost and skepticism follows.
C) Possibility becomes impossible.
D) The intellect's judgment becomes stronger.

Key Takeaway
Possibility is an essential attribute of all possible things; without it, rational certainty is impossible.

Refuting the Objection: Possibility as Occurrence
The author answers an objection that possibility is merely occurring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that possibility is merely occurring (ḥādith) is refuted, and why it leads to either infinite regress or denial of a Creator.

Core Idea
If possibility were an accidental occurrence, its own existence would require a cause, leading to regress or atheism.

Having established that possibility is necessary to the essence, the author now addresses a potential objection: what if possibility is not necessary but arises accidentally (ḥādith)? This lesson explains the response, which shows that if possibility were occurring, its own occurrence would itself need a cause. That either results in an infinite chain of causes or the unacceptable conclusion that possibles can exist without a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
The objection claims that possibility might be occurring (ḥādith) rather than being necessary for the essence.
This is a common philosophical challenge: why must possibility be inherent?
2
Understand the response
The response argues that the occurrence of possibility itself must have a cause. Two alternatives are examined.
The cause could be internal or external.
3
Examine the two alternatives
If the cause is something else, we get infinite regress; if there is no cause, then possibles could exist without a Creator, which is denied.
Both are unacceptable.
4
Consider the nuance
The passage also discusses whether possibility itself can have another possibility, leading to a subtle point about attributes and essences.
This is an advanced refinement.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المستندة إلى ذوات الأشياء في أنفسها لا يتصور انفكاكها عنها وإلا لم تكن تلك الذوات لانتفاء مقتضياتها من حيث هي هي وربما يحتج عليه أي على لزوم الإمكان لماهية الممكن بأن الإمكان إن لم يكن لازماً لها بل حادثاً فنقول إن حدوث الإمكان لها واتصافها به إما أن يكون لأمر يقتضي ذلك الاتصاف وهو أي الإمكان قوله عن حكم العقل أي الحكم الذي يقتضيه بديهة العقل من مدخلية حس أو عادة أعني البديهي وهو الحكم بجواز الجائزات واستحالة المستحيلات ووجوب الواجبات فلا يرد أن إمكان الانقلاب نظراً إلى ذاته لا ينافي الحكم القطعي بعدمه كما في العلوم العادية كما مر في تعريف العلم قوله لأن الوجوب إلخ لا يخفى أن كون ارتفاع الوثوق سفسطة بديهي لا يحتاج إلى البيان فالتقريب تام وإن قوله لأن الوجوب دليل مستقل على كون كل واحد من الجهات الثلاث لازمة للماهية فالظاهر إيراد الواو إلا أنه قصد الشارح قدس سره بيان لم كونه سفسطة ظاهرة البطلان قوله وربما يحتج إلخ هذا الاحتجاج مبني على أن علة الاحتياج هو الحدوث دون الإمكان وإلا فيكفي أن يقال أو لم يكن الإمكان لازماً للماهية لكان جائز الزوال عنها فحصول الإمكان لها إما لأمر يقتضيه فيكون ممكناً ويتسلسل أو لا لأمر يقتضيه فيلزم نفي الصانع لجواز أن يكون وجود الممكنات من غير أمر يقتضيها قوله بل حادثاً لأنه إذا لم يكن لازماً للماهية جاز زواله عنها فيكون حادثاً لأن كل ممكن جائز الزوال عن موصوفه فهو حادث بخلاف ما إذا كان لازماً فإنه يكون مقتضى قوله إن لم يكن لازماً لها بل حادثاً فإن قلت عدم اللزوم قد يكون بالزوال والدليل على تقدير تمامه لم يدل على امتناعه قلت إنما لم يتعرض له المصنف لظهوره بالمقايسة للاشتراك في الدليل وأما ما قيل إذا لم يكن حادثاً يكون قديماً وما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه فتعين عدم اللزوم بأن يكون حادثاً ففيه أن تلك المقدمة على تقدير تمامها إنما هي في الموجودات ألا يرى أن الأعدام الأزلية قد تزول والإمكان ليس منها وهاهنا بحث وهو أن كلامه يدل على أن الإمكان على تقدير لزومه للماهية ليس له إمكان آخر وأنت خبير بأن الإمكان إذا كان صفة للماهية ولوازمها يحتاج إلى الموصوف ويكون له إمكان آخر وينتقض الدليل وقد سبق منا التفصيل في بحث الوجود فليتذكر قوله إما أن يكون لأمر إلخ وأيضاً إذا كان ثبوت الإمكان لها لأمر يقتضيه لا لذاته كان ممكناً بالغير لا ممكناً بالذات هذا والأولى أن يقول إن حدوث الإمكان يكون ممكناً إذ لا
The two ways remove security from necessary truths, so confidence in the judgment of the intellect regarding the necessity of necessary things, the impossibility of impossible things, and the possibility of possible things is lifted due to the possibility of some of them turning into others at that time. That is an evident sophistry, because necessity, impossibility, and possibility that are based on the essences of things themselves cannot be conceived as being detached from them; otherwise those essences would not exist due to the negation of their requisites in themselves. And perhaps one may argue against it—i.e., against the necessity of possibility for the essence of the possible—that if possibility is not necessary for it but rather occurring, then we say: The occurrence of possibility for it and its being characterized by it is either due to something that necessitates that characterization, and that is possibility... His words: 'regarding the judgment of the intellect' means the judgment that the innate intellect requires, involving sense or habit, i.e., the primary judgment which is the judgment of the possibility of possible things, the impossibility of impossible things, and the necessity of necessary things. So it is not objected that the possibility of transformation in itself does not contradict the definitive judgment of its non-occurrence, as in empirical sciences, as passed in the definition of knowledge. His words: 'because necessity, etc.' It is not hidden that the elevation of confidence being sophistry is self-evident and does not need explanation; so the argument is complete, although his saying 'because necessity' is an independent proof that each of the three modalities is necessary for the essence, so it appears that the letter waw is inserted, except that the commentator intended to explain why it is an evident sophistry. His words: 'And perhaps one may argue, etc.' This argument is based on the principle that the cause of need is occurrence (huduth) rather than possibility; otherwise it suffices to say: if possibility were not necessary for the essence, it would be possible to depart from it, so the occurrence of possibility for it is either due to something that necessitates it, in which case it would be possible and lead to infinite regress, or not due to anything necessitating it, in which case the denial of the Creator follows because it is then permissible that the existence of possibles occurs without something that necessitates them. His words: 'Rather occurring' because if it is not necessary for the essence, its departure from it is possible, so it is occurring; because everything possible that can depart from its subject is occurring, unlike if it is necessary, then it is a requisite. His words: 'If it is not necessary for it but rather occurring' then you may say: Non-necessity may be by departure, and the proof, if complete, does not prove its impossibility. I say: The author did not address it because it is obvious by analogy and commonality in the proof. As for what is said: if it is not occurring, it is eternal, and what is proven eternal cannot be non-existent, so non-necessity is determined by being occurring; there is in that: that premise, if complete, applies only to existents. Do you not see that eternal non-existents may cease, and possibility is not among them? Here there is a discussion: his words indicate that possibility, if it is necessary for the essence, does not have another possibility, and you are aware that if possibility is an attribute of the essence and its necessities, it needs the subject and has another possibility, and the proof is refuted. We have already given detail in the discussion of existence; let it be remembered. His words: 'either due to something, etc.' Also, if the affirmation of possibility for it is due to something that necessitates it, not for its essence, then it is possible through another, not possible in itself. This, and it is better to say that the occurrence of possibility is possible, because not
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is a proof that possibility (imkān) is necessary (lāzim) for the essence of the possible, together with a refutation of skepticism and an objection-and-answer. The author begins with the argument: if possibility were separable from the essence (i.e., not necessary), then it could be detached *либо* change into something else. That would undermine certainty in the rational judgments about necessary, impossible, and possible things, leading to universal skepticism, which is evident sophistry. The reason is that necessity, impossibility, and possibility are based on the essences themselves and cannot be separated; if they could, the essences would not be what they are. Then an objection is raised: perhaps possibility is not necessary but rather arises accidentally (ḥādith). The answer is that the occurrence of possibility itself requires a cause. If that cause is something else, it leads to infinite regress; if no cause, then the denial of a Creator follows (since possibles could exist without a cause). The commentary then clarifies each part: the 'judgment of the intellect' refers to primary rational judgments that are self-evident, and the argument against skepticism is itself self-evident. The commentator notes that the proof that the three modalities are necessary to the essence is independent. The objection and answer are analyzed: the objection assumes that the cause of need is occurrence (ḥudūth) rather than possibility, but response shows it leads to regress or denial of a Creator. A further discussion: if possibility were necessary for the essence, it would not have another possibility, but that would lead to a problem because possibility as an attribute requires a subject and thus has its own possibility—this is a subtle point the author had previously addressed. Finally, the passage ends with a note that the occurrence of possibility must be possible itself.

العلة التامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that possibility could be an accidental attribute that arises without any cause.
Correction: The occurrence of such an accident would itself require a cause, leading to logical problems.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what are the two possible outcomes if possibility were occurring (ḥādith)?
A) Either possibility becomes necessary or impossible.
B) Either infinite regress or denial of the Creator.
C) Either the essence changes or remains the same.
D) Either the intellect judges correctly or incorrectly.

Key Takeaway
The objection that possibility is merely occurring fails because it leads to either infinite regress or denial of a Creator, reaffirming that possibility is necessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that if possibility were accidental, your own existence would ultimately be uncaused or regress infinitely. Do you truly trace the ground of your existence back to a Necessary Being, or do you live as if chance governs your life? Will you let this reasoning reshape how you understand your dependence on God?
?
The argument forces you to accept that possibilities require a cause. In your own life, do you act as if your choices and circumstances are merely accidental, or do you acknowledge a deeper necessity behind them? What would it take for you to live consistently with the belief that nothing possible is self-sufficient?
?
If denying necessity leads to absurdity, how do you react when you encounter arguments that challenge your deep-seated assumptions? Are you willing to follow reasoning to its end even when it demands you surrender your intellectual comfort?
