intermediate
8 min
Two proofs on the necessity of possibility
Two arguments for the necessity of possibility and the author's self-evidence claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two kalam arguments for the necessity of possibility in possible essences, and why the author considers the claim self-evident.

Core Idea
The necessity of possibility is proven by infinite regress and the need for a preponderant, but ultimately it is self-evident.

In this passage, the author presents two classic arguments to establish that possibility (imkan) is a necessary attribute of any possible essence. The first argument uses infinite regress, the second the principle of preponderance. After presenting them, the author makes a striking claim: the truth is so clear that it is more evident than the proofs themselves. This lesson unpacks these arguments and the author's concluding remark.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage aloud if possible. Notice how the author presents two arguments and then a final statement. The passage uses technical terms like 'imkan' and 'tasalsul'. Read it twice: once for flow, once for understanding.
2
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two distinct arguments. Look for the markers 'أولا' (first) and 'أو نقول' (or we say). Identify the logical structure of each: one uses infinite regress, the other uses the need for a preponderant.
The first argument is about infinite regress of possibilities; the second is about specification without a preponderant.
3
Understand the self-evidence claim
After presenting the arguments, the author says the truth is more evident than the proofs and is self-evident (badīha). Ask yourself: why would a kalam author say that? Does this undermine the arguments?
The author is signaling that the doctrine is intuitively clear to pure reason, not dependent on these particular proofs.
4
Connect to the commentary
Read the provided commentary and translation. Notice how the commentary explains the two arguments and highlights the author's closing remark. Use the commentary to clarify any unclear points in the Arabic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باعتبار وقوعه صفة لها (ممكن)؛ لحدوثه بهذا الاعتبار، واستناده إلى الغير، فيكون للإمكان إمكان فتتسلسل الإمكانات إلى غير النهاية (أولا) يكون حدوث الإمكان لها لأمر يقتضيه فيلزم نفي الصانع أي لا يثبت وجوده؛ لجواز حدوث الحوادث حينئذ من غير استناد إلى شيء يقتضيها. (أو نقول: حدوثه) للماهية (إن توقف على حادث آخر تسلسل، بأن يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر لا إلى نهاية، وإلا) وإن لم يتوقف حدوثه لها على حادث آخر (فاختصاصه) أي اختصاص حدوث الإمكان بذلك الوقت الذي حدث فيه يكون بلا مرجح هذا خلف. (والحق: أن الدعوى وهي أن الإمكان الذي يقتضيه ذات الممكن من حيث هي لازم لها، يستحيل انفكاكه عنها أظهر من هذين الدليلين)، لأنها قضية بديهية يحكم بها صريح العقل.
By considering its occurrence as a quality of it, (it is possible); because of its occurrence in this respect, and its dependence on another, so possibility would have possibility and possibilities would be in infinite regress. (First) the occurrence of possibility for it would be due to something that necessitates it, leading to the negation of the Maker, i.e., His existence is not established; because accidents could then occur without dependence on anything that necessitates them. (Or we say: its occurrence) for the essence (if dependent on another accident leads to regress, such that every accident is preceded by another accident ad infinitum; otherwise) and if its occurrence for it is not dependent on another accident, (then its specification) i.e., the specification of the occurrence of possibility to that time in which it occurred would be without a preponderant—this is absurd. (And the truth: that the claim, which is that possibility which the essence of the possible necessitates in itself is necessary for it, impossible to separate from it, is more evident than these two proofs), because it is a self-evident proposition judged by pure reason.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the necessity of possibility (imkan) for the possible essence (mahiyya mumkina). The first proof: if possibility occurs as a quality of the essence, then it would itself be possible and dependent on another, leading to an infinite regress of possibilities. The second proof: if its occurrence is not dependent on another accident, then its specification to a particular time would lack a preponderant (murajjih), which is absurd. After presenting these two proofs, the author states that the truth is that the claim—that possibility is necessary for the essence—is more evident than the proofs, being a self-evident (badihi) judgment of pure reason once the terms are properly abstracted. The commentary emphasizes that the author does not rely solely on these proofs but considers the doctrine intuitively clear.

Key Terms
إمكان
ممكن
تسلسل
مرجح
ماهية
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author's position depends entirely on the two arguments presented.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the truth is more evident than these arguments and is a self-evident proposition, meaning it is not reliant on the proofs.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what follows if possibility occurs as a quality of the essence?
A) The essence becomes impossible.
B) Possibilities would be in infinite regress.
C) The Maker's existence is proven.
D) The essence is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of possibility for possible essences is both proven by rational arguments and affirmed as a self-evident truth by pure reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author says the truth is self-evident to 'pure reason.' Can you identify a moment in your life when a deep truth about existence became evidently clear to you without needing proof? What did that feel like, and how does it compare to the author's claim?
?
If the necessity of possibility is self-evident, why do you think many people live as if they are self-sufficient and not dependent on a cause? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized that you are always in a state of possibility—needing a Sustainer at every moment?

First Objection: Separation Possibility Without Actual Occurrence
First objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the necessity of possibility and how the author refutes it by distinguishing between necessity and actual occurrence.

Core Idea
Possibility of separation does not require actual separation.

The author has argued that possibility is necessary for the essence. Now he considers objections. The first objection claims that even if possibility is not necessary, that only implies possible separation, not actual separation. The author's refutation is implicit: the necessity of possibility is about the inherent relationship, not forced actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the objection: lack of necessity entails possibility of separation, not actual occurrence.
The objection is concise; focus on the phrase 'لا يقتضي وقوعه'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that the author separates the concept of necessity from actual event. The objection confuses two meanings.
Think about how necessity in kalam differs from contingency.
3
See the refutation
The author says this objection is repelled by the earlier exposition. Reflect on why this distinction suffices.
The refutation is implied; the objection does not attack the core claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاندفع بهذا التحرير مناقشات: إحدها أن عدم اللزوم بمعنى جواز الانفكاك لا يقتضي وقوعه حتى يكون حادثاً. وثانيتها: أن وقوع الانفكاك يجوز أن يكون بزواله لا بحدوثه، إلا أن يقال: ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، فلا يجوز زوال الإمكان بعد حصوله إلا إذا كان حادثا. وما قيل إن الأعدام الأزلية قد تزول فمدفوع بأنه إن أريد بزوالها وجودها في أنفسها فظاهر البطلان إذ العدم يمتنع وجوده. وإن أريد بزوالها زوالها عن محالها، فلا محل في الأزل ولا زوال. وإنما هو مجرد اعتبار عقلي ينتزعه العقل بعد حدوث الحوادث عن عللها. وثالثتها أنه على تقدير كون الإمكان لازماً للماهية يكون له إمكان آخر؛ لاحتياجه إلى موصوف، مع أن كلامه يشعر بأنه على تقدير لزومه لا إمكان له ووجه الاندفاع ظاهر بالتأمل فيما حررنا.
By this exposition, objections are repelled: First, that the lack of necessity in the sense of the possibility of separation does not entail its occurrence such that it would be an accident. Second, that the occurrence of separation could be by its ceasing, not by its occurrence, except that it may be said: that which is proven eternal, its non-existence is impossible, so possibility cannot cease after it has been attained unless it is an accident. And what is said that eternal non-existents may cease is refuted by the fact that if by their cessation is meant their existence in themselves, then its falsehood is apparent, since non-existence cannot exist. And if by their cessation is meant their ceasing from their loci, then there is no locus in eternity and no cessation. Rather, it is a mere rational consideration which the mind extracts after the occurrence of accidents from their causes. Third, that on the assumption that possibility is necessary for the essence, it would have another possibility because it needs a subject to characterize it, even though his statement suggests that on the assumption of its necessity it has no possibility. The manner of repulsion is apparent through reflection on what we have written.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage addresses three objections (munaqashat) to the claim that possibility (imkan) is necessary for the essence. The first objection: the non-necessity (i.e., possibility of separation) does not prove actual occurrence, so possibility might not actually occur as an accident. The author implies this does not refute necessity. The second objection: separation could be by cessation (zawal) rather than occurrence. The author replies that eternal entities cannot cease, and non-existence has no real locus. The cessation is merely a mental consideration. The third objection: if possibility is necessary, it would need another possibility because it requires a subject. The author says this is not required. The refutations are presented concisely, with the claim that they are clear upon reflection.

Key Terms
انفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the first objection claim?
A) That possibility must actually occur for it to be necessary.
B) That lack of necessity only implies possibility of separation, not actual occurrence.
C) That possibility is not necessary because it can be separated.
D) That necessity requires actual occurrence.

Key Takeaway
The first objection fails because it misinterprets necessity as actual occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your faith do you assume that because Allah can do something, He must do it? How does this lesson’s distinction between possibility and actual occurrence correct that assumption? Write down one specific belief you will adjust.
?
How often do you confuse God’s essential attributes (like power or knowledge) with their temporal manifestations? What step will you take today to distinguish between what is necessary in Allah and what happens in creation?
?
If possibility and actuality are different, how does that change the way you trust Allah’s promises? Name one promise from the Quran that you now realize is not automatic but requires your active response.

Second Objection: Cessation vs. Occurrence and Eternal Non-Existents
Second objection and its detailed refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that possibility might cease rather than occur, and the author's refutation using the impossibility of cessation of eternal entities and the nature of non-existence.

Core Idea
Eternal attributes cannot cease; cessation is a mental consideration.

The second objection proposes that separation could happen by possibility ceasing to exist rather than by it occurring as an accident. The author responds by asserting that what is proven eternal cannot cease to exist. He then tackles a counter-argument about eternal non-existents ceasing, showing that cessation of non-existence is meaningless because non-existence has no locus in eternity; it is merely a mental concept.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection says separation could be by cessation (zawal) of possibility, not by its occurrence as a new accident.
Note the phrase 'بزواله لا بحدوثه'.
2
Grasp the initial refutation
The author invokes the principle: 'that which is proven eternal, its non-existence is impossible'. So, if possibility is eternal, it cannot cease.
This principle is foundational in kalam.
3
Analyze the sub-objection
The objector brings up eternal non-existents (e.g., non-existence of the world before creation) that seem to cease.
This is a common counterexample.
4
See the two-part refutation
First, if cessation means non-existence becoming existence, that's impossible. Second, if it means non-existence leaving a locus, there is no locus in eternity. So cessation is merely mental.
The text says: 'وإنما هو مجرد اعتبار عقلي'.

B) As non-existence becoming existence.
C) As a mere rational consideration extracted by the mind.
D) As the removal of a locus.

Key Takeaway
Eternal entities cannot cease; the cessation of non-existence is purely conceptual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that non-existence has no real locus or power. Where in your life have you been giving mental concepts—like regrets, fears, or expectations—the weight of actual reality? Will you release them today?
?
The author says cessation of non-existence is merely a mental abstraction. Are there 'absences' you dwell on (e.g., past sins, unfulfilled desires) as if they truly exist and control you? How can you start treating them as the mere considerations they are?
?
If eternal non-existents cannot truly cease, what does that say about your own reliance on things that are not yet present? Are you building your peace on mental constructs rather than on the Eternal? What concrete step can you take to shift your focus?

Third Objection: Regress of Possibility
Third objection and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that if possibility is necessary, it would require another possibility, and the author's concise refutation.

Core Idea
A necessary attribute does not require a further possibility.

The third objection argues that if possibility is necessary for the essence, then possibility itself would need a subject, implying another possibility, leading to infinite regress. The author responds briefly, stating that this is not the case, and that the objection is clearly refuted by the earlier exposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection claims that if possibility is necessary, it would need a subject, thus it would be possible itself, leading to regress.
Notice the phrase 'يكون له إمكان آخر'.
2
Understand the author's response
The author says the refutation is apparent from what has been written. Think about why a necessary attribute does not require a prior possibility.
The refutation is not explicitly stated but implied.
3
Reflect on the key insight
A necessary attribute is intrinsic and does not need an external possibility to be attributed to a subject.
This avoids infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاندفع بهذا التحرير مناقشات: إحدها أن عدم اللزوم بمعنى جواز الانفكاك لا يقتضي وقوعه حتى يكون حادثاً. وثانيتها: أن وقوع الانفكاك يجوز أن يكون بزواله لا بحدوثه، إلا أن يقال: ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، فلا يجوز زوال الإمكان بعد حصوله إلا إذا كان حادثا. وما قيل إن الأعدام الأزلية قد تزول فمدفوع بأنه إن أريد بزوالها وجودها في أنفسها فظاهر البطلان إذ العدم يمتنع وجوده. وإن أريد بزوالها زوالها عن محالها، فلا محل في الأزل ولا زوال. وإنما هو مجرد اعتبار عقلي ينتزعه العقل بعد حدوث الحوادث عن عللها. وثالثتها أنه على تقدير كون الإمكان لازماً للماهية يكون له إمكان آخر؛ لاحتياجه إلى موصوف، مع أن كلامه يشعر بأنه على تقدير لزومه لا إمكان له ووجه الاندفاع ظاهر بالتأمل فيما حررنا.
By this exposition, objections are repelled: First, that the lack of necessity in the sense of the possibility of separation does not entail its occurrence such that it would be an accident. Second, that the occurrence of separation could be by its ceasing, not by its occurrence, except that it may be said: that which is proven eternal, its non-existence is impossible, so possibility cannot cease after it has been attained unless it is an accident. And what is said that eternal non-existents may cease is refuted by the fact that if by their cessation is meant their existence in themselves, then its falsehood is apparent, since non-existence cannot exist. And if by their cessation is meant their ceasing from their loci, then there is no locus in eternity and no cessation. Rather, it is a mere rational consideration which the mind extracts after the occurrence of accidents from their causes. Third, that on the assumption that possibility is necessary for the essence, it would have another possibility because it needs a subject to characterize it, even though his statement suggests that on the assumption of its necessity it has no possibility. The manner of repulsion is apparent through reflection on what we have written.
Source pages p. 175

Commentary
This passage addresses three objections (munaqashat) to the claim that possibility (imkan) is necessary for the essence. The first objection: the non-necessity (i.e., possibility of separation) does not prove actual occurrence, so possibility might not actually occur as an accident. The author implies this does not refute necessity. The second objection: separation could be by cessation (zawal) rather than occurrence. The author replies that eternal entities cannot cease, and non-existence has no real locus. The cessation is merely a mental consideration. The third objection: if possibility is necessary, it would need another possibility because it requires a subject. The author says this is not required. The refutations are presented concisely, with the claim that they are clear upon reflection.

Key Terms
إمكان
لازم
موصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a necessary attribute requires a prior possibility to be attributed.
Correction: A necessary attribute is intrinsic and does not require an external possibility.

Checkpoint
What is the third objection's claim?
A) That necessity invalidates possibility.
B) That if possibility is necessary, it would need another possibility due to subject requirement.
C) That possibility cannot be necessary because it is an accident.
D) That the author's argument leads to contradiction.

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because necessary attributes are intrinsic and do not lead to regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in theological reasoning about Allah's attributes, do you ever find yourself trapped in endless logical chains, forgetting that His attributes are intrinsic and beyond human analogy? How can this lesson's refutation of regress help you break free from such intellectual pride?
?
Think of a time you rejected a theological truth because your reasoning led to an infinite regress. After understanding that necessary attributes are intrinsic, would you now reconsider? What does this teach you about trusting transmitted knowledge over mere logic?

Doubt about the possibility of the eternal and its refutation
Author raises doubt about possibility of eternal things and gives answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between essential possibility (al-imkan al-dhati) and impossibility due to an external cause, and how this resolves the doubt about eternal things.

Core Idea
Eternal things are essentially possible; their non-existence is impossible only due to a cause.

This lesson addresses a subtle doubt: if something is eternal, its non-existence seems impossible, so how can it be considered possible? The author, drawing from the tradition of kalam, provides a precise answer by distinguishing between possibility in itself and impossibility due to a cause. This distinction is foundational for understanding the rational status of divine attributes and other eternal entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then its literal English translation. Notice how the doubt is introduced and the concise answer is given.
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the doubt
The doubt claims that eternal things cannot be possible because their non-existence is impossible. Locate this in the text.
The doubt is phrased as 'it is not said'.
3
Understand the author's answer
The author responds by making a key distinction: impossibility of non-existence due to a cause does not negate essential possibility. Find this distinction in the passage.
4
Connect to examples
Reflect on the examples given: the world (according to philosophers) and God's real attributes (according to Ash'aris). How does the distinction apply?
Examples help solidify the abstract distinction.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to verify you can apply the distinction correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وربما يشكك عليه إلخ) لا يقال: يمكن إيراد التشكيك بالممكن القديم كالعالم عند الفلاسفة والصفات الحقيقية عندنا بناء على امتناع عدم القديم. ولو أمكن لما امتنع؛ لأنا نقول: امتناع العدم بالنظر إلى العلة لا ينافي الإمكان الذاتي.
His statement: (And perhaps one may cast a doubt upon it, etc.) It is not said: one can raise the doubt regarding the eternal possible, such as the world according to the philosophers and the real attributes according to us, based on the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal. And if it were possible, it would not be impossible; for we say: the impossibility of non-existence with respect to the cause does not contradict essential possibility.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage addresses a doubt (tashkik) against the necessity of possibility. The doubt: if something is eternal (qadim) like the world for philosophers or God's real attributes for the Ash'aris, its non-existence is impossible; so how can it be considered possible? The answer distinguishes between essential possibility (imkan dhati) and external impossibility due to a cause (imtina' al-'adam bi al-nazar ila al-'illa). The impossibility of non-existence because of a cause does not negate the thing's possibility in itself. Thus, even eternal things are essentially possible; their non-existence is impossible only because of an external cause (like God's will or necessary existence).

Key Terms
تشكيك
قديم
إمكان ذاتي
امتناع العدم
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternal things are necessary in themselves, so they cannot be possible.
Correction: Eternal things are not necessary in themselves; their non-existence is impossible only due to an external cause, leaving their essence possible.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the impossibility of non-existence of the eternal not negate its essential possibility?
A) Because the eternal is impossible in itself.
B) Because impossibility is due to the cause, not the essence.
C) Because essential possibility is not real.
D) Because the eternal cannot be possible.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of non-existence due to a cause does not eliminate essential possibility; eternal things remain possible in themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you rely more on your own perceived self-sufficiency or on God's sustaining causality? How does the distinction between essential possibility and impossibility due to cause challenge your sense of independence? This week, commit to identifying one area where you can consciously depend on God's preservation rather than your own strength.
?
If the divine attributes are eternal but essentially possible, what does that teach you about your own relationship with God? Are there aspects of your faith that you treat as 'necessary' (unquestionable) rather than 'possible' (dependent on God's will)? Reflect on one belief you hold and ask: 'Is it because God has made it impossible to not believe, or because I have chosen it?'
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible to change, do you consider the cause that makes it impossible? How does the lesson's distinction between essential and contingent impossibility help you trust that God's causation can alter your circumstances? Pray today for the strength to see the difference between what is inherently impossible and what is only prevented by temporary causes.

The Regress Argument and the Eternal Will
Argument against regress and objection about eternal will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a contingent event requires a preponderant, why the eternal will cannot serve as that preponderant, and how the objection is refuted by daily events.

Core Idea
Possibility must precede the will; the will cannot explain possibility.

The argument begins with a problem: if something contingent occurs without a specifying factor, an infinite regress of possibilities would arise. An objection suggests that the eternal will could be that factor. The commentator shows this is invalid because the will’s attachment presupposes possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and objection
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the statement 'it would be without a preponderant' and the objection that the eternal will could be the specifier.
2
Understand the response
The response says that the will's attachment is a branch of possibility, so the will cannot be the cause. Instead, the object must already be possible for the will to be attached.
Note the phrase 'the attachment of the will is a branch of possibility'.
3
See the refutation by daily events
The commentator then points out that this argument is refuted by daily events—everyday contingent occurrences—which shows the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( يكون بلا (مرجح فيه أنه يجوز أن يكون المخصص هي الإرادة القديمة المتعلقة بحدوثها في وقت مخصوص والجواب : بأن تعلق الإرادة فرع الإمكان، فلا يعلل به مدفوع بأن الثابت أن متعلق الإرادة يجب أن يكون ممكناً، وأنه لا يمكن تعلقه بالواجب والممتنع. وأما توقفه على الإمكان .فكلا . ثم إن هذا الاحتجاج منقوض بالحوادث اليومية كما لا يخفى . بقي هاهنا بحث آخر وهو أن هذا الاحتجاج على تقدير تمامه إنما يدل على أنه لا يجوز كون كل إمكان حادثاً، فيجوز أن يكون إمكان الممكنات حادثاً، وإمكان الإمكان لازماً للماهية، فلا يثبت المدعى الكلية أعني أن الإمكان لازم لكل ماهية ممكنة.
His statement: 'It would be without a preponderant' - in that it is permissible that the specifying factor be the eternal will related to its occurrence at a specific time. And the answer: that the attachment of the will is a branch of possibility, so it cannot be used as a cause. It is refuted by [the fact] that it is established that the object of the will must be possible, and that it cannot be related to the necessary or the impossible. As for its dependence on possibility, no. Then this argument is refuted by daily events, as is not hidden. There remains here another discussion: that this argument, assuming its validity, only indicates that it is not permissible for every possibility to be originated. So it is permissible that the possibility of possible things be originated, and the possibility of possibility be necessary for the essence. So the universal claim is not established, namely that possibility is necessary for every possible essence.
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the statement 'it would be without a preponderant'. The commentator examines an objection: the specifying factor could be the eternal will directed to the event at a specific time. The answer to this objection is that the will's attachment depends on the possibility of the object, so the will cannot explain the possibility; rather, possibility must already exist. The objection is then challenged by noting that daily events (contingent events) are possible, and their specification is by the eternal will. However, a further point is made: even if this argument (that without a preponderant there is regress) is sound, it only shows that not every possibility can be originated. It does not prove that every possibility is necessary. It allows that the possibility of possibles might be originated, while the possibility of that possibility (meta-possibility) could be necessary for the essence. Thus, the universal claim that possibility is necessary for every possible essence is not established.

Key Terms
الإرادة القديمة
الإمكان
المرجح
الحوادث اليومية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot the eternal will be the preponderant?
A) Because the will is not eternal
B) Because the will's attachment depends on possibility
C) Because the will is not related to time
D) Because daily events refute it

Key Takeaway
The eternal will cannot explain contingency because its very attachment presupposes the possibility of its object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask God for something, do you first truly believe it is possible? If not, what does that reveal about your understanding of God's will and your own faith? Commit to examining one specific dua today in light of this lesson.
?
Consider an area of your life where you feel frustrated by change that hasn't happened. Have you accepted the possibility of that change? How might acknowledging that possibility precedes will change how you pray or act? What is one step you will take to align your expectations with this truth?
?
If God's will attaches only to what is possible, then why do you sometimes despair over things that are, in fact, possible? How does this lesson challenge your sense of helplessness? Identify one possible but difficult goal and ask God with certainty that He can bring it about.

The Limitation of the Regress Argument and Meta‑Possibility
Discussion of the universal claim about necessity of possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the regress argument does not prove that possibility is necessary for every possible essence, only that not all possibility can be originated.

Core Idea
The argument rules out universal accidental possibility but not that some possibility may be originated.

The commentator now discusses a subtle point: even if the regress argument is valid, it only shows that not every possibility can be originated. It does not prove that possibility is a necessary attribute of every possible essence. This opens the door for the possibility of possibles to be originated while the possibility of that possibility is necessary.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the argument's limited conclusion
The commentator notes that the argument only indicates that it is not permissible for every possibility to be originated. This is a negative conclusion, not a positive universal.
2
Understand the distinction
There is a distinction between the possibility of possible things (which could be originated) and the possibility of possibility (which is necessary for the essence).
The phrase 'إمكان الإمكان' refers to meta-possibility.
3
See why the universal claim fails
Because the argument allows that possibility of possibles might be originated, it does not establish that possibility is necessary for every possible essence.
The universal claim is that possibility is necessary for every possible essence.

Source Text

Misconception: One might think the regress argument proves that necessity is impossible.

C) That the eternal will is impossible

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Threefold Skeptical Objection Against Necessity of Possibility
Jurjānī presents a threefold skeptical objection that possibility is not necessary for possible essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three skeptical objections raised against the claim that possibility is necessary for possible essences.

Core Idea
The skeptic argues that possibility is not necessary because it can arise and cease.

This passage presents a threefold skeptical challenge to the essential link between possibility and possible things. The first objection focuses on the world's occurrence in time, the second on God's agency, and the third on the status of a possible object after it exists. Each aims to show that possibility is not a necessary attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: each part begins with a case where possibility appears or disappears.
The third objection is in parentheses; it's part of the same enumeration.
2
Identify the three parts
Find each of the three objections: the world's existence, divine agency, and the possible object of power. Notice how each uses a temporal change in possibility.
3
Understand the logical structure
The skeptic is attacking the necessity (لزوم) of possibility for a possible essence. Each case shows possibility is not permanent.
Think about why possibility arising or ceasing would challenge the claim that possibility is necessary.
4
Reflect on the intended target
Ask yourself: what assumption about possibility is the skeptic questioning? This will help you appreciate the answer later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وربما يشكك عليه (أي على لزوم الإمكان للماهية) بأن حدوث العالم (أي وجوده) غير ممكن في الأزل؛ لما ثبت من الدلالة على وجوب حدوثه، بل نقول: وجود الحادث في هذا الآن غير ممكن في الأزل لاستحالة أن يكون الحادث أزلياً، ثم يصير (وجود العالم بل وجود ذلك الحادث) ممكناً فيما لا يزال، فقد ثبت الإمكان لشيء بعد ما لم يكن له فلا يكون لازماً. وكذلك فاعلية الباري تعالى (للعالم بل للحوادث اليومية) غير ممكنة في الأزل، ثم إنها تصير ممكنة فيما لا يزال. (وأيضاً فيحدث للممكن المقدور (مع) بقاء (الوجود امتناع المقدورية)؛ لأن الموجود يمتنع أن يكون مقدوراً؛ لاستحالة تحصيل الحاصل (بعد إمكانه) أي بعد إمكان مقدوريته حال حدوثه وصدوره من القادر، فقد زال إمكان الشيء بعد ما كان حاصلاً له فلا يكون لازماً.) والجواب عن الأول: أن أزلية الإمكان ثابتة إمكان الأزلية وغير مستلزمة له. وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا: إمكانه أزلي أي ثابت أزلاً كان الأزل ظرفاً للإمكان فيلزم أن يكون ذلك الشيء متصفاً بالإمكان اتصافاً مستمراً غير مسبوق بعدم الاتصاف، وهذا هو الذي يقتضيه لزوم الإمكان لماهية الممكن، وهو ثابت للعالم والحوادث اليومية ولفاعلية الباري لها أيضا، وإذا قلنا: أزليته ممكنة كان الأزل ظرفاً لوجوده، على معنى أن وجوده المستمر الذي لا يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم ممكن، ومن المعلوم أن الأولى لا تستلزم الثانية؛ لجواز أن يكون وجود الشيء في الجملة ممكناً إمكاناً مستمراً، ولا يكون وجوده على وجه الاستمرار ممكناً أصلاً، بل ممتنعاً، فلا يلزم من هذا أن يكون ذلك الشيء من قبيل الممتنعات دون الممكنات لأن الممتنع هو الذي لا يقبل الوجود بوجه من الوجوه. هذا هو المسطور في كتب القوم، ولنا فيه بحث، وهو أن إمكانه إذا كان مستمراً أزلاً لم يكن هو في ذاته مانعاً قوله : ( وجود الشيء في الجملة إلخ) أي مطلقاً غير مقيد بالاستمرار . قوله : ( هو الذي لا يقبل إلخ ) وهذا قابل للوجود الغير المستمر أعني فيما لا يزال . من قوله : : ( ومن المعلوم أن الأول لا تستلزم الثانية قيل : هذا ميل إلى مذهب الحكيم من كون الشيء قابلاً للوجود في زمان دون زمان حيث تفاوت استعداداته، وإنكار لعموم قدرة الله تعالى في جميع الأزمان، كما ذهب إليه المتكلمون . والحق ما ذكره الشارح. قوله ( ولنا فيه بحث وهو أن إمكانه إلخ) قال الأستاذ المحقق في الذخيرة: مقدماته مسلمة إلى قوله بل جاز اتصافه به من كل منها فإنه في حيز المنع ولم يذكر ما يلزم من هذا، وأنه ماذا أراد بالتطويل السابق على أن عدم المنع من قبول الوجود مستمر له، وهذا مما لانزاع فيه؛ لأن استمرار عدم المنع من قبول الوجود واستمرار إمكان الوجود في المآل واحد، واستمرار الإمكان لم ينازع فيه أحد، إلا أن المحققين ادعوا أنه لا يقتضي إلا أن يكون الوجود في الجملة، ولو في وقت من الأوقات جائزاً جوازاً مستمراً، وهو لا يستلزم أن يكون الوجود المستمر جائزاً في
And perhaps one might raise a skeptical objection against it (i.e., against the necessity of possibility for the essence) that the occurrence of the world (i.e., its existence) is not possible in pre-eternity, because of the proof established for its necessary occurrence. Rather, we say: the existence of the originated thing in this now is not possible in pre-eternity due to the impossibility of the originated being eternal, then it becomes (the existence of the world, rather the existence of that originated thing) possible in the future. Thus, possibility is established for a thing after it did not have it, so it is not necessary. Likewise, the agency of the Exalted Creator (for the world, rather for daily events) is not possible in pre-eternity, then it becomes possible in the future. (And also, for the possible object of power, with the continuation of existence, the impossibility of being an object of power occurs; because the existent is impossible to be an object of power, due to the impossibility of obtaining what is already obtained, after its possibility, i.e., after the possibility of its being an object of power at the time of its occurrence and emanation from the powerful. So the possibility of a thing ceases after it was present for it, so it is not necessary.) And the answer to the first [objection]: that the eternity of possibility is established, and the possibility of eternity is not entailed by it. That is because if we say: its possibility is eternal, meaning established eternally, then pre-eternity is a container for possibility, so it follows that thing is qualified by possibility with a continuous qualification not preceded by absence of qualification. This is what the necessity of possibility for the essence of the possible demands, and it is established for the world, daily events, and for the agency of the Creator for them also. And if we say: its eternity is possible, then pre-eternity is a container for its existence, in the sense that its continuous existence that is not preceded by non-existence is possible. And it is known that the first does not entail the second; because it is possible that the existence of a thing in general is possible with continuous possibility, while its existence in a continuous manner is not possible at all, but rather impossible. So it does not follow from this that this thing is of the category of impossibilities rather than possibles, because the impossible is that which does not accept existence in any way. This is what is recorded in the books of the people, and we have a discussion about it: that if its possibility is continuous eternally, then it is not in itself a hindrance. His statement: 'the existence of the thing in general etc.' i.e., absolutely, not restricted to continuity. His statement: 'it is that which does not accept etc.' And this is susceptible to non-continuous existence, i.e., in the future. From his statement: 'and it is known that the first does not entail the second' - it is said: this is a leaning towards the doctrine of the philosopher, that a thing is receptive to existence in one time but not another due to varying dispositions, and a denial of the universality of God's power in all times, as the theologians hold. And the truth is what the commentator mentioned. His statement: 'and we have a discussion about it: that its possibility etc.' The esteemed teacher said in al-Dhakhira: his premises are conceded up to his statement 'rather, it is possible for it to be qualified by it from each of them' - for it is in the realm of rejection. And he did not mention what follows from this, and what he intended by the previous elaboration, that the absence of hindrance from accepting existence is continuous for it, and this is something about which there is no dispute; because the continuity of the absence of hindrance from accepting existence and the continuity of possibility of existence are ultimately one, and the continuity of possibility has not been disputed by anyone. However, the investigators claim that it entails only that existence in general, even if at some time, is permissible with a continuous permissibility, and this does not entail that continuous existence is permissible in...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage presents a threefold skeptical objection against the claim that possibility is necessary for possible essences. First, the world's existence was not possible in pre-eternity (since it had to occur later), so possibility arose after not being present. Second, God's agency for the world was not possible in pre-eternity but became possible later. Third, a possible object of power exists and then becomes impossible to be an object of power (because you cannot acquire what already exists), so possibility ceases. The answer is given only to the first objection: the eternity of possibility is not the same as the possibility of eternal existence. If we say possibility is eternal, it means the thing is continuously possible—it always had the capacity to exist at some time. If we say eternity is possible, it means the thing has continuous existence possible, which is different. The first does not entail the second: a thing can be continuously possible to exist at some time without being possible to exist at every time. So the objection confuses the two. Then follows a gloss discussing whether this view leans toward the philosophers' idea of varying receptivity and denies universal divine power. The commentator defends the view, citing al-Dhakhira, that the continuity of non-prevention of existence is the same as continuity of possibility, and the investigators claim it only entails permissibility of existence at some time, not continuous existence. The text ends incomplete.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three skeptical objections presented?
A) The world's existence was not possible in pre-eternity but became possible later.
B) God's agency for the world was not possible in pre-eternity but became possible later.
C) The impossibility of the eternal existence of God.
D) A possible object of power ceases to be possible after it exists.

Key Takeaway
The skeptic presents three cases to show that possibility is not a necessary attribute of possible essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The skeptic argues that possibility for the world's existence was absent in pre-eternity and only arose later. In your own life, what possibilities have you assumed were always there—like the chance to repent or do good—that you have delayed acting upon? How does this lesson's distinction between possibility as temporary or necessary challenge your assumptions?
?
The third objection states that once a thing exists, it ceases to be an object of power. Have you ever treated your current state (e.g., having faith, being alive, being in a certain relationship) as something permanently 'possible' to change later, ignoring that once actualized, the possibility to change it may be lost? What specific step can you take today to respond to this urgency?
?
God's agency for daily events is said to be impossible in pre-eternity but possible later. How does this shape your understanding of God's relationship with time? Do you pray as if God's power to answer is 'possible now' but might not be later? What does this teach you about the importance of supplicating in the present moment?

Answering the First Objection: Eternity of Possibility vs. Possibility of Eternity
Jurjānī presents the answer to the first objection and a subsequent gloss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the eternity of possibility and the possibility of eternal existence, and how it answers the first skeptical objection.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not mean possible to exist at all times.

The answer to the first objection hinges on a subtle but crucial distinction: the eternity of possibility means that a thing is always possible to exist at some time, not that its continuous existence is possible. The commentator clarifies this and then addresses a possible objection that this view leans toward the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two key phrases: 'its possibility is eternal' and 'its eternity is possible'.
2
Understand the two meanings
The answer distinguishes between 'eternal possibility' (it is always possible to exist at some time) and 'possible eternity' (its continuous existence from eternity is possible).
The first does not imply the second.
3
Apply to the world and divine agency
The world and daily events have eternal possibility, but their eternal existence is impossible because they are originated. Similarly, divine agency has eternal possibility but not possible eternity (since agency is relational).
4
Examine the gloss
The gloss discusses a concern: does this view resemble the philosophers' idea of varying dispositions? The commentator defends the answer and cites al-Dhakhira.
Focus on the distinction and why it does not compromise divine omnipotence.

Commentary
This passage presents a threefold skeptical objection against the claim that possibility is necessary for possible essences. First, the world's existence was not possible in pre-eternity (since it had to occur later), so possibility arose after not being present. Second, God's agency for the world was not possible in pre-eternity but became possible later. The answer is given only to the first objection: the eternity of possibility is not the same as the possibility of eternal existence. If we say possibility is eternal, it means the thing is continuously possible—it always had the capacity to exist at some time. If we say eternity is possible, it means the thing has continuous existence possible, which is different. The first does not entail the second: a thing can be continuously possible to exist at some time without being possible to exist at every time. So the objection confuses the two. The commentator defends the view, citing al-Dhakhira, that the continuity of non-prevention of existence is the same as continuity of possibility, and the investigators claim it only entails permissibility of existence at some time, not continuous existence. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is always possible to exist (i.e., its possibility is eternal), then its eternal existence (continuous without beginning) must be possible.
Correction: Eternal possibility only means the thing is capable of existing at some time; it does not mean that its existence at every time (eternal existence) is possible. The world is a clear counterexample.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what does 'eternity of possibility' (أزلية الإمكان) mean?
A) The thing is possible to exist at every time (continuous existence).
B) The thing is possible to exist at some time, with continuous capacity.
C) The thing is necessary and cannot be otherwise.
D) The thing is impossible to exist at any time.

Key Takeaway
The eternity of possibility for a thing does not imply that its continuous existence from eternity is possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God created the world in time, do you ever feel that because God is eternal, the world should also be eternal? How does the distinction in this lesson challenge that assumption and force you to rely on revelation rather than pure reason?
?
The lesson shows that continuous possibility does not mean actual continuous existence. In your own life, where do you confuse what is perpetually possible with what should always be true? How might that confusion lead to false expectations of God or of yourself?
?
If God’s power is not limited by continuous possibility, then He can act at any moment He wills. Does your daily trust in God reflect that truth, or do you sometimes assume that because something is possible, it must happen immediately or continuously? What would change if you truly believed that possibility and actuality are distinct?

Eternal Possibility vs. Possibility of Eternality
Al-Sharh presents the first objection and its standard answer distinguishing azaliyyat al-imkan from imkan al-azaliyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between eternal possibility (azaliyyat al-imkan) and the possibility of eternality (imkan al-azaliyya), and how this distinction refutes the objection that possibility is not a necessary attribute of contingent things.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility does not imply the possibility of eternal existence.

The objection argues that because things become possible only after being impossible, possibility cannot be a necessary attribute. Al-Sharh's answer introduces a key distinction: a thing may have eternal possibility (its description of possibility is timeless) without that thing being capable of eternal existence. This passage is crucial for understanding how kalam defends the necessary connection between possibility and contingent quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection (first part) which presents three points. The first is the most general, regarding the world's possibility. The second about divine agency, and the third about an existing object of power.
Notice that the objection claims that possibility is not a necessary (lazim) attribute because it can come and go.
2
Identify the core problem
The objection's central claim is that if possibility were necessary, it would always be present. But we see that things are not possible in eternity, then become possible, so possibility is not necessary.
3
Examine the answer's distinction
The answer distinguishes between 'eternality of possibility' (azaliyyat al-imkan) and 'possibility of eternality' (imkan al-azaliyya). The first means the attribute of possibility is eternally true; the second means the thing's existence as an eternal continuous being is possible.
The answer shows that the first does not entail the second.
4
Understand the logical move
The answer argues that a thing can have its existence 'in general' be possible at all times, yet its existence 'in the mode of continuity' might be impossible. Thus, the objection fails because it confuses two different senses of possibility.
This preserves the necessity of possibility for the quiddity.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by noting that this distinction is standard in the books of the mutakallimun. Reflect on how this defense maintains the coherence of the concept of possibility in kalam.

وهي غير الحادث في هذا الآن غير ممكن في الأزل لاستحالة أن يكون الحادث أزلياً، ثم يصير وجود العالم بل وجود ذلك الحادث ممكناً فيما لا يزال، فقد ثبت الإمكان لشيء بعد ما لم يكن له فلا يكون لازماً. وكذلك فاعلية الباري تعالى للعالم بل للحوادث اليومية غير ممكنة في الأزل، ثم إنها تصير ممكنة فيما لا يزال. وأيضاً فيحدث للممكن المقدور مع بقاء الوجود امتناع المقدورية؛ لأن الموجود يمتنع أن يكون مقدوراً؛ لاستحالة تحصيل الحاصل بعد إمكانه أي بعد إمكان مقدوريته حال حدوثه وصدوره من القادر، فقد زال إمكان الشيء بعد ما كان حاصلاً له فلا يكون لازماً. والجواب عن الأول: أن أزلية الإمكان ثابتة إمكان الأزلية وغير مستلزمة له، وذلك لأنا إذا قلنا: إمكانه أزلي أي ثابت أزلاً كان الأزل ظرفاً للإمكان فيلزم أن يكون ذلك الشيء متصفاً بالإمكان اتصافاً مستمراً غير مسبوق بعدم الاتصاف، وهذا هو الذي يقتضيه لزوم الإمكان لماهية الممكن، وهو ثابت للعالم والحوادث اليومية ولفاعلية الباري لها أيضا، وإذا قلنا: أزليته ممكنة كان الأزل ظرفاً لوجوده، على معنى أن وجوده المستمر الذي لا يكون مسبوقاً بالعدم ممكن، ومن المعلوم أن الأولى لا تستلزم الثانية؛ لجواز أن يكون وجود الشيء في الجملة ممكناً إمكاناً مستمراً، ولا يكون وجوده على وجه الاستمرار ممكناً أصلاً، بل ممتنعاً، فلا يلزم من هذا أن يكون ذلك الشيء من قبيل الممتنعات دون الممكنات لأن الممتنع هو الذي لا يقبل الوجود بوجه من الوجوه. هذا هو المسطور في كتب القوم.
It is not the case that the originated thing in this now was not possible in eternity because it is impossible for the originated to be eternal, then the existence of the world, even the existence of that originated thing, becomes possible in what does not cease (the future), so possibility is established for a thing after it was not for it, so it is not necessary. Likewise, the agency of the Exalted Creator for the world, even for daily events, was not possible in eternity, then it becomes possible in what does not cease. Also, for the possible object of power, with the continuation of existence, the impossibility of being an object of power occurs; because what exists is impossible to be an object of power due to the impossibility of attaining the attained after its possibility, i.e. after the possibility of its being an object of power at the time of its occurrence and emanation from the powerful, so the possibility of the thing has ceased after it was present for it, so it is not necessary. The answer to the first: that the eternality of possibility (azaliyyat al-imkan) is established and the possibility of eternality (imkan al-azaliyya) is not implied by it. That is because if we say: its possibility is eternal, i.e. established eternally, then eternity is a container for possibility, so it follows that that thing is described with possibility as a continuous description not preceded by lack of description, and this is what is required by the necessity of possibility for the quiddity of the possible, and it is established for the world and daily events and also for the agency of the Most High for them. And if we say: its eternality is possible, then eternity is a container for its existence, meaning that its continuous existence which is not preceded by non-existence is possible. And it is known that the first does not entail the second; because it is permissible that the existence of a thing in general is possible with continuous possibility, and its existence in the mode of continuity is not possible at all, rather it is impossible, so it does not follow from this that that thing is of the impossible things rather than the possible things, because the impossible is that which does not accept existence in any way. This is what is written in the books of the people (the mutakallimun).
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its standard answer. The objection raises three points to argue that possibility is not a necessary attribute of contingent things: (1) The world's existence in any given 'now' was impossible in eternity, but later becomes possible, so possibility is not constant. (2) Divine agency for the world was impossible in eternity but becomes possible later. (3) An existent object of power ceases to be possible because what already exists cannot be effected again. The answer to the first (and most general) objection distinguishes between 'eternality of possibility' (azaliyyat al-imkan) and 'possibility of eternality' (imkan al-azaliyya). 'Eternal possibility' means that the description of possibility is continuously true of the thing without interruption; this does not imply that the thing's existence is possible as an eternal, uninterrupted existence. It is possible for a thing to have its existence in general be possible at all times, but for it to have an eternal continuous existence may be impossible. Thus, the objection is refuted: the fact that possibility is eternal does not entail that the thing can have an eternal existence, so possibility remains necessary (lazim) for the contingent quiddity.

Key Terms
azaliyyat al-imkan
imkan al-azaliyya
luzum al-imkan
al-mahiyya
al-mumkin

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a thing is possible at all times, then it must be capable of existing at all times continuously.
Correction: Eternal possibility does not imply possibility of eternal existence; a thing may be possible in general but impossible as a continuous existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why does eternal possibility (azaliyyat al-imkan) not entail the possibility of eternal existence (imkan al-azaliyya)?
A) Because eternal possibility is itself impossible.
B) Because continuous existence in general is impossible for all things.
C) Because a thing may be possible in general but impossible as a continuous existence.
D) Because only the world has eternal possibility.

Key Takeaway
Eternal possibility (azaliyyat al-imkan) is a necessary attribute of contingent quiddities and does not imply the possibility of eternal existence (imkan al-azaliyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider that your existence right now is a continuous act of God's creation. If your possibility is eternal but your actual existence is not, how does that reshape your understanding of your dependence on God for every moment? What one habit can you adopt today to remind yourself that you are nothing without Him?
?
The objection in the lesson argues that because possibility seems to come and go in your experience, it cannot be a necessary attribute of your nature. Have you ever felt that your abilities or existence are self-standing because they appear constant? How does the answer—that eternal possibility does not mean your existence is possible eternally—correct your pride and remind you of your contingency?
?
The distinction guards against conflating what is always possible with what can actually happen forever. Where in your life do you assume that because something is always possible for you (e.g., repentance, belief), you can delay it indefinitely? How should the fact that your very existence is not eternal but only momentarily actualized by God compel you to act now?

The Commentator's Critique of Continuous Possibility
Commentator presents his critique of the implication of continuous possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's argument that continuous possibility only entails permissibility in each part individually, not co-existence in all parts simultaneously.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility permits existence at any moment, not all moments together.

In this lesson, we examine the commentator's own critique of the claim that eternal possibility implies the possibility of eternal existence. He carefully distinguishes between permissibility in individual parts of eternity and permissibility in all parts together, showing that the latter does not follow from the former. This is a key distinction in the debate over the implication of continuous possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation to familiarize yourself with the argument.
The passage includes glosses and a quote from a critic.
2
Identify the commentator's main claim
Find where the commentator states his view: if possibility is continuously eternal, then the essence does not prevent existence in any part, but this only allows existence in each part individually, not all together.
Look for the phrase 'لا بدلاً فقط، بل ومعاً أيضاً'.
3
Understand why the distinction matters
Reflect on why the distinction between individual and collective permissibility is important. The commentator uses it to block the inference from eternal possibility to eternal existence.
This distinction is central to the debate with the philosophers.

أجزاء الأزل أعم من الاتصاف به في كل منها معاً، ومستلزم العام لا يجب أن يكون مستلزماً للخاص. فقوله: وجواز اتصافه به في كل منها معاً إلخ أن الذي فرع عليه ما زعمه من استلزام أزلية الإمكان لإمكان الأزلية مما لا طائل تحته. انتهى كلامه. ثم إن ما ذكره الشارح المحقق منقوض إجمالاً بالزمان والحركة؛ لأن ممكن الوجود منهما عند المحققين هو الآن السيال، والحركة بمعنى التوسط، وهما أمران قاران لا أجزاء لهما أصلاً، فإمكانهما أزلي وأزليتهما ممكنة، بل واقعة عند الفلاسفة. وأما الحركة بمعنى القطع والزمان الغير القار فلا إمكان لهما أصلاً، ولا بمقولة الفعل والانفعال، فإن الشارح قرر الاستدلال على امتناعهما ولم يجب عنه We have a discussion about it, and that is: if its possibility is continuously eternal, then its essence is not in itself a preventer of accepting existence in any part of eternity, so its non-prevention is a continuous matter in all those parts. So when we consider the essence in itself, it does not prevent its being described by existence in any of them. His statement: '(its possibility if it is continuously eternal etc.)' meaning if all parts of eternity are containers for possibility. His statement: '(it was not in itself a preventer etc.)' meaning that eternity is a container for non-prevention, i.e., its essence in any part of eternity did not prevent accepting existence, for if it were a preventer in any part, its possibility would be negated in that part, because non-prevention is necessary for possibility. And the negation of the necessary entails negation of the necessitating, so possibility would not be continuous in all parts of eternity. His statement: '(so it becomes etc.)' meaning if eternity is a container for non-prevention, then its non-prevention extends to all parts of eternity such that no part is excluded, so eternity is a container for the continuity of its non-prevention. His statement: '(so when one considers etc.)' means the continuity of non-prevention in all parts of eternity so that no part is excluded necessitates non-prevention from being described by existence in any of them, on the condition that some part is a container for the whole, and there is nothing in his words that necessitates the permissibility of this at all, and more far-fetched is what he attached to it from his statement: 'not only as an alternative, but also simultaneously'. For even if it is granted that the eternality of possibility necessitates the permissibility of being described by existence in every part of eternity, from where does it necessitate the permissibility of conjunction (simultaneity)? And it is known that being described by existence in every part of eternity is more general than being described by it in all of them together, and what is necessitated by the general does not have to be necessitated by the specific. So his statement: 'and the permissibility of being described by it in all of them together etc.' is that which he branches on what he claimed of the implication of eternality of possibility for the possibility of eternality has no substance. End of his words. Then what the verifying commentator mentioned is refuted in general by time and motion; because the possible of existence among them according to the verifiers is the flowing instant (al-an al-sayyal) and motion in the sense of intermediation (al-tawassut), and they are two stationary things (amran qaran) having no parts at all, so their possibility is eternal and their eternality is possible, nay actual according to the philosophers. As for motion in the sense of traversal (al-qat') and non-stationary time (al-zaman ghayr al-qarr), there is no possibility for them at all, nor in the category of action and passion. For the commentator established the proof of their impossibility and did not answer it; so perhaps they are non-existent in his view – that is the doctrine of the later verifiers. Rather, the instantaneous letters (al-huruf al-aniyya) which occur to sounds when they are cut off, like the occurrence of the instant to time and the point to the line, since they have stated – and the commentator also stated – that they have no existence except in the instant of their occurrence, so they have eternality of possibility without possibility of eternality. And the statement that their eternality is possible in view of their essence and quiddity, and the impossibility is due to something else, namely existence in the first time, is not paid attention to; because this other is realized on the assumption of the continuation of their existence, so if their quiddities require passing away after existence, then continuation is impossible for them in themselves, cut off, as is not hidden from the one who reflects. Unless it is permitted that the non-conception of their continuation is due to something external to their quiddities, and you may make the form of the refutation a basis for prevention. And it is possible to be freed from the refutation by preventing the possibility of any non-stationary thing, and its clarification is that the commentator is now in the process of repelling what the people (the mutakallimun) mentioned from their statement: 'eternality of possibility does not imply the possibility of eternality' and as an answer to the doubt about their statement: 'possibility is necessary for the quiddity of the possible'. So by this discussion he is supporting the doubt; so the eternality of possibility for every possible has not been established. And there is no doubt that the occurrence of the refutation depends on the establishment of the eternality of possibility for the non-stationary thing. So the debater may...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion by the commentator ('al-Sharh') following the standard answer. He begins by stating his own view: if possibility is continuously eternal, then the thing's essence does not in itself prevent existence in any part of eternity, so the absence of prevention is continuous. He then glosses key phrases to clarify. A critic objects that this line of reasoning leans toward the philosophers' view that things have varying degrees of receptivity in different times, and undermines the mutakallimun's doctrine of universal divine power. The commentator responds by defending his position, noting that even if eternal possibility allows existence in every part of eternity individually, it does not entail existence in all parts simultaneously (conjunction). The critique continues with a quote from 'al-Ustadh al-Muhaqqiq fi al-Dhakhira', who argues that the commentator's premises are granted but the conclusion does not follow. The commentator then addresses a counterexample from time and motion: the flowing instant and motion as intermediation are stationary entities without parts; they have eternal possibility and their eternality is possible (actual for philosophers). Non-stationary time and traversal motion are impossible entirely. He also discusses instantaneous letters (like sounds) that exist only at the moment of their occurrence; they have eternal possibility but not possibility of eternality. He concludes that the refutation against him is not valid because it presupposes the eternality of possibility for non-stationary things, which is not established. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing to the next page.

Key Terms
istimrar al-imkan
'adam al-man'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible at every moment individually, then it must be possible to exist at all moments simultaneously.
Correction: The commentator argues that permissibility in each part does not entail permissibility in all parts together; the two are distinct and the latter does not follow from the former.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the commentator's critique regarding continuous possibility?
A) Continuous possibility implies that the thing must exist at some time.
B) Continuous possibility only implies that the thing is not prevented in each part individually, not that it can exist in all parts simultaneously.
C) Continuous possibility is impossible for originated things.
D) The commentator agrees entirely with the standard answer without addition.

Key Takeaway
Eternal possibility only implies the absence of prevention in each part of eternity, not the possibility of simultaneous existence in all parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself assuming that because something is possible in each moment, it must be actualized all together in your life? How does this lesson's distinction between possibility in parts and possibility in the whole expose a subtle arrogance in your expectations of God?
?
When you think about the attribute of divine power, do you unconsciously limit it by imagining that what is possible at every moment must be possible simultaneously? How does the commentator's critique force you to refine your understanding of Allah's absolute power?
?
In your own struggles with patience, does the logical fallacy described here mirror your impatience with God's timing? Will you surrender your expectation that all possibilities must be realized at once and trust His wisdom in unfolding events?

Refuting the Commentator: Time, Motion, and Non-Stationary Entities
Refutation of the commentator using examples from time, motion, and instantaneous letters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of non-stationary entities (like the flowing instant and instantaneous letters) is used to refute the commentator's position on the implication of eternal possibility.

Core Idea
Non-stationary entities exist only in a moment, so they have eternal possibility but not the possibility of eternal existence.

In this part, the author responds to the commentator's critique by presenting counterexamples from time and motion. He argues that entities like the flowing instant and motion as intermediation are stationary and can have eternal possibility without implying eternal existence. Moreover, non-stationary entities like instantaneous letters exist only at the moment of their occurrence; they have eternal possibility but their eternal existence is impossible. This undermines the commentator's argument that eternal possibility leads to eternal existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on time and motion
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the distinction between stationary and non-stationary entities.
The passage references concepts like al-an al-sayyal and al-zaman ghayr al-qarr.
2
Understand the classification
Identify the two types of entities: (1) stationary entities like the flowing instant and motion as intermediation, which have eternal possibility and can have eternal existence; (2) non-stationary entities like traversal motion and non-stationary time, which are impossible entirely.
Also, instantaneous letters are discussed separately.
3
See the refutation
Realize that these examples show that eternal possibility does not necessarily imply eternal existence. For instantaneous letters, they have eternal possibility but their quiddity prevents continuation, so they cannot exist eternally.
The author concludes that the commentator's refutation is not valid because it assumes eternal possibility for non-stationary things, which is not established.

أجزاء الأزل أعم من الاتصاف به في كل منها معاً، ومستلزم العام لا يجب أن يكون مستلزماً للخاص. فقوله: وجواز اتصافه به في كل منها معاً إلخ أن الذي فرع عليه ما زعمه من استلزام أزلية الإمكان لإمكان الأزلية مما لا طائل تحته. انتهى كلامه. ثم إن ما ذكره الشارح المحقق منقوض إجمالاً بالزمان والحركة؛ لأن ممكن الوجود منهما عند المحققين هو الآن السيال، والحركة بمعنى التوسط، وهما أمران قاران لا أجزاء لهما أصلاً، فإمكانهما أزلي وأزليتهما ممكنة، بل واقعة عند الفلاسفة. وأما الحركة بمعنى القطع والزمان الغير القار فلا إمكان لهما أصلاً، ولا بمقولة الفعل والانفعال، فإن الشارح قرر الاستدلال على امتناعهما ولم يجب عنه فلعلهما عنده غير موجودين، هو مذهب متأخري المحققين بل الحروف الآنية التي تعرض للأصوات عند انقطاعها كعروض الآن للزمان والنقطة للخط، إذ قد صرحوا وصرح الشارح أيضا بأنها ليس لها وجود إلا في آن حدوثها، فلها أزلية الإمكان دون إمكان الأزلية والقول بأن أزليتها ممكنة نظراً إليها ذاتها وماهيتها والامتناع بالنظر إلى الغير أعني الوجود في الزمان الأول مما لا يلتفت إليه؛ لأن هذا الغير متحقق على تقدير استمرار وجودها فإذا اقتضى ماهياتها التقضي بعد الوجود لم يمكن لها لذاتها استمرار قطعاً، كما لا يخفى على المتأمل. اللهم إلا أن يجوز أن يكون عدم تصور استمرارها لأمر خارج عن ماهياتها على أن لك أن تجعل صورة النقض سنداً للمنع، ويمكن أن يتخلص من النقض بمنع إمكان شيء غير قار، وتوضيحه أن الشارح الآن بصدد دفع ما ذكره القوم من قولهم: أزلية الإمكان غير مستلزم لإمكان الأزلية وجواباً عن التشكيك على قولهم: الإمكان لازم لماهية الممكن فهو بهذا البحث مؤيد للتشكيك فلم يتحقق بعد أزلية إمكان كل ممكن، ولا شبهة أن ورود النقض موقوف على ثبوت أزلية إمكان للأمر الغير القار، فللمناظر أن
We have a discussion about it, and that is: if its possibility is continuously eternal, then its essence is not in itself a preventer of accepting existence in any part of eternity, so its non-prevention is a continuous matter in all those parts. So when we consider the essence in itself, it does not prevent its being described by existence in any of them. His statement: '(its possibility if it is continuously eternal etc.)' meaning if all parts of eternity are containers for possibility. His statement: '(it was not in itself a preventer etc.)' meaning that eternity is a container for non-prevention, i.e., its essence in any part of eternity did not prevent accepting existence, for if it were a preventer in any part, its possibility would be negated in that part, because non-prevention is necessary for possibility. And the negation of the necessary entails negation of the necessitating, so possibility would not be continuous in all parts of eternity. His statement: '(so it becomes etc.)' meaning if eternity is a container for non-prevention, then its non-prevention extends to all parts of eternity such that no part is excluded, so eternity is a container for the continuity of its non-prevention. His statement: '(so when one considers etc.)' means the continuity of non-prevention in all parts of eternity so that no part is excluded necessitates non-prevention from being described by existence in any of them, on the condition that some part is a container for the whole, and there is nothing in his words that necessitates the permissibility of this at all, and more far-fetched is what he attached to it from his statement: 'not only as an alternative, but also simultaneously'. For even if it is granted that the eternality of possibility necessitates the permissibility of being described by existence in every part of eternity, from where does it necessitate the permissibility of conjunction (simultaneity)? And it is known that being described by existence in every part of eternity is more general than being described by it in all of them together, and what is necessitated by the general does not have to be necessitated by the specific. So his statement: 'and the permissibility of being described by it in all of them together etc.' is that which he branches on what he claimed of the implication of eternality of possibility for the possibility of eternality has no substance. End of his words. Then what the verifying commentator mentioned is refuted in general by time and motion; because the possible of existence among them according to the verifiers is the flowing instant (al-an al-sayyal) and motion in the sense of intermediation (al-tawassut), and they are two stationary things (amran qaran) having no parts at all, so their possibility is eternal and their eternality is possible, nay actual according to the philosophers. As for motion in the sense of traversal (al-qat') and non-stationary time (al-zaman ghayr al-qarr), there is no possibility for them at all, nor in the category of action and passion. For the commentator established the proof of their impossibility and did not answer it; so perhaps they are non-existent in his view – that is the doctrine of the later verifiers. Rather, the instantaneous letters (al-huruf al-aniyya) which occur to sounds when they are cut off, like the occurrence of the instant to time and the point to the line, since they have stated – and the commentator also stated – that they have no existence except in the instant of their occurrence, so they have eternality of possibility without possibility of eternality. And the statement that their eternality is possible in view of their essence and quiddity, and the impossibility is due to something else, namely existence in the first time, is not paid attention to; because this other is realized on the assumption of the continuation of their existence, so if their quiddities require passing away after existence, then continuation is impossible for them in themselves, cut off, as is not hidden from the one who reflects. Unless it is permitted that the non-conception of their continuation is due to something external to their quiddities, and you may make the form of the refutation a basis for prevention. And it is possible to be freed from the refutation by preventing the possibility of any non-stationary thing, and its clarification is that the commentator is now in the process of repelling what the people (the mutakallimun) mentioned from their statement: 'eternality of possibility does not imply the possibility of eternality' and as an answer to the doubt about their statement: 'possibility is necessary for the quiddity of the possible'. So by this discussion he is supporting the doubt; so the eternality of possibility for every possible has not been established. And there is no doubt that the occurrence of the refutation depends on the establishment of the eternality of possibility for the non-stationary thing. So the debater may...
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion by the commentator ('al-Sharh') following the standard answer. He begins by stating his own view: if possibility is continuously eternal, then the thing's essence does not in itself prevent existence in any part of eternity, so the absence of prevention is continuous. He then glosses key phrases to clarify. A critic objects that this line of reasoning leans toward the philosophers' view that things have varying degrees of receptivity in different times, and undermines the mutakallimun's doctrine of universal divine power. The commentator responds by defending his position, noting that even if eternal possibility allows existence in every part of eternity individually, it does not entail existence in all parts simultaneously (conjunction). The critique continues with a quote from 'al-Ustadh al-Muhaqqiq fi al-Dhakhira', who argues that the commentator's premises are granted but the conclusion does not follow. The commentator then addresses a counterexample from time and motion: the flowing instant and motion as intermediation are stationary entities without parts; they have eternal possibility and their eternality is possible (actual for philosophers). Non-stationary time and traversal motion are impossible entirely. He also discusses instantaneous letters (like sounds) that exist only at the moment of their occurrence; they have eternal possibility but not possibility of eternality. He concludes that the refutation against him is not valid because it presupposes the eternality of possibility for non-stationary things, which is not established. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing to the next page.

al-haraka bi ma'na al-tawassut
al-haraka bi ma'na al-qat'
