The Theologians' Proof for the World's Temporality
The theologians enumerate their proof for the temporal origination of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational kalam argument for the temporal origination of the world based on the nature of substances and accidents.

Core Idea
The world's temporality is proven by the constant change in substances.

The theologians developed a proof for the world's beginning that does not depend on any prior conception of God. At its heart is a simple observation: everything in the world is either a substance or an accident, and both are subject to change. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own reasoning come through.
Notice how the argument begins by dividing the world into two categories.
2
Identify the two categories
The argument splits the world into substances (a‘yān) and accidents (a‘rāḍ). Understand each term.
Substances are self-subsisting entities; accidents are attributes that inhere in substances.
3
Understand why substances are temporal
Substances are never free from motion or rest, both of which are temporally originated. The principle 'whatever is not free from temporally originated things is itself temporally originated' applies.
This is the key logical move.
4
See how accidents follow
Since accidents inhere in substances, if substances are temporal, then accidents must also be temporal.
This completes the proof for the entire world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولـه : ( من غير تعرض لفاعله حيث قالوا : إن العالم حادث لأنه إما أعيان، وإما أعراض، وكل منهما حادث : أما الأعيان فلأنها لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون وهما حادثان، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث فالأعيان حادثة وإذا كانت الأعيان حادثة كانت الأعراض أيضاً حادثة، لقيامها بها . قوله : ( فحكموا بأن العالم (قديم لشبهة لاحت لهم لا لمجرد ذلك التجويز كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( ورد عليه بأنه يدل إلخ) هذا الرد لنصير الدين الطوسي في شرح الإشارات، ذكره في أوائل النمط الخامس منه ، ويمكن أن يقال : هذا لا ويمكن أن يقال : هذا لا يرد على المصنف قطعاً؛ لأنه إنما حكم بعود النزاع في جواز استناد القديم إلى الفاعل الذي هو الله تعالى إلى كونه موجباً أو مختاراً، لا في قدم العالم وحدوثه كما توهمه الشارح نعم يتوهم وروده على الرازي إن وجد في كلامه أن نزاعهم في قدم العالم وحدوثه عائد إلى ذلك، ويمكن دفعه عنه أيضاً بأن نقول: بعض أدلة الاختيار لا يتوقف حدوث العالم، ولا تعرض فيه لذلك كأدلته النقلية التي فصلها الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار، فإذا أثبت الاختيار بتلك الأدلة أمكن أن يفرع عليها حدوث العالم، كما يمكن العكس أيضاً إذا أثبت حدوثه بدليل لا يتوقف على كونه تعالى مختاراً. وإذا حمل كلام الإمام على هذا كان كلاماً لا غبار عليه اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن الأدلة النقلية لا تعدو إفادة الظن، كما الآمدي . فلا معنى لبناء المطلوب الذي هو إثبات الاختيار على ذلك، ثم تفريع حدوث العالم عليه وليس لهم دليل عقلي على أن ذلك المطلوب لا يتوقف على حدوث العالم. وأنت خبير بأن كلام الشارح في آخر المرصد الرابع في الصفات الوجودية من الإلهيات يشعر بأنهم يثبتون الاختيار تارة بأن إيجاب غير الصفات نقصان، فيتأمل . قوله : ( فإنهم استدلو أولاً إلخ) حيث قالوا : العالم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، وهما حادثان، وما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث .
His statement: 'Without addressing its agent, because they said: The world is temporally originated because it is either substances or accidents, and each of them is temporally originated. As for substances, because they are not free from motion and rest, and both are temporally originated, and whatever is not free from temporally originated things is itself temporally originated. So substances are temporally originated, and if substances are temporally originated, then accidents are also temporally originated, due to their inherence in them.' His statement: 'So they judged that the world is eternal due to a semblance that appeared to them, not merely because of that permission, as is not hidden.' His statement: 'And it was rebutted that it indicates etc.' This rebuttal is by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi in his commentary on al-Isharat, mentioned at the beginning of the fifth pattern. And it can be said: This does not—and it can be said: This does not refute the author at all; because he only ruled that the dispute concerning the permissibility of attributing the Eternal to the Agent who is God Most High goes back to whether He is a necessitator or a free agent, not concerning the eternity of the world and its temporal origination as the commentator imagined. Yes, it might be imagined to apply to al-Razi if it occurs in his speech that their dispute concerning the eternity of the world and its temporal origination goes back to that. And it can also be defended on his behalf by saying: Some proofs for free will do not depend on the temporal origination of the world, nor do they mention it, such as his transmitted proofs that al-Amidi detailed in Abkar al-Afkar. So if free will is proven by those proofs, it becomes possible to derive the temporal origination of the world from it, just as the opposite is also possible if its temporal origination is proven by a proof that does not depend on His being free. And if the Imam's words are interpreted in this way, his words are beyond reproach—except that it could be said: Transmitted proofs are only productive of probability (ẓann), as al-Amidi stated. So there is no point in building the desired conclusion which is the proof of free will on that, then deriving the temporal origination of the world from it, and they have no rational proof that the desired conclusion does not depend on the temporal origination of the world. And you are aware that the commentator's words at the end of the fourth observation on the ontological attributes from the section on divine matters suggest that they sometimes prove free will by saying that necessitating other than attributes is a deficiency, so let it be pondered. His statement: 'For they first argued etc.'—where they said: The world is not free from motion and rest, and both are temporally originated, and whatever is not free from temporally originated things is temporally originated.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of al-Razi's claim by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi (as transmitted by the commentator). It begins by quoting the theologians' own proof for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world: the world consists of substances (a‘yān) and accidents (a‘rāḍ). Substances are never free from motion or rest, both of which are temporally originated; whatever is not free from temporally originated things must itself be temporally originated. Accidents are also temporally originated because they inhere in substances. This proof makes no reference to the divine agent at all, let alone whether He is free or necessitating. Then the commentator notes that the philosophers judged the world eternal based on a semblance (shubha), not merely because they allowed attributing the eternal to a free agent. Then the main rebuttal: al-Tusi points out that al-Razi's statement implies that theologians built their case for ḥudūth on the issue of free choice (ikhtiyār), but the opposite is true: they first proved the world's temporality without any mention of the agent, and only then built on that temporality to prove that its Creator must be free. The commentator then defends al-Razi, saying the rebuttal does not apply to the original author (the text's author, al-Iji?) because he only discussed the attribution of the Eternal (qadīm) to God, not directly the world's eternity. For al-Razi, one can argue that some proofs for free will (e.g., transmitted proofs from al-Amidi) do not depend on ḥudūth, so one could first prove free will and then deduce ḥudūth from it, or vice versa. However, the commentator notes a problem with transmitted proofs: they only yield probability (ẓann), not certainty, so using them for such foundational matters may be problematic. The passage concludes by noting that the commentator's own remarks in a later section suggest that free will can be proven by rational arguments (e.g., that necessitating other attributes is a deficiency), so the issue requires careful consideration.

Key Terms
أعيان
أعراض
حركة
سكون
ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the proof for the world's temporality requires assuming God's existence or free will.
Correction: This proof makes no reference to any agent; it relies solely on empirical observation of change and logical principle.

Checkpoint
According to the theologians' proof cited in this passage, why are substances (a‘yān) considered temporally originated?
A) They are created directly by God
B) They are never free from motion or rest, both of which are temporally originated
C) They are made of atoms that disintegrate
D) They are composed of accidents

Key Takeaway
The world's temporality is established through the inevitable presence of change in its fundamental components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a substance never free from change—your body, your thoughts, your emotions all shift. If change proves temporality, then you came into existence after nothing. How does this undeniable beginning shape your answer to the question 'Why am I here?' What will you do today to live as someone created with purpose, not as a random accident?
?
The proof shows the world is temporal without mentioning God, yet it leaves an inescapable gap: if everything temporal requires a cause, what caused you? You cannot avoid the implication that your existence is dependent. Are you living in a way that acknowledges your absolute need for a Creator, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What specific thought or action today will remind you of your contingency?

Al-Tusi's Rebuttal and the Independence of the Proof
Al-Tusi corrects al-Razi's misunderstanding of the theologians' argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Tusi refuted al-Razi's claim that the proof for the world's temporality depends on the issue of God's free will.

Core Idea
The rebuttal shows the ḥudūth proof stands on its own, independent of any discussion about the agent.

Al-Razi had suggested that the theologians' proof for the world's beginning was based on an assumption about God's free will. Al-Tusi responds sharply, showing that the proof makes no mention of any agent, and that the philosophers' judgment of eternity was due to a different reason. This lesson examines that debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Razi's claim
Read the passage from al-Razi's statement that the philosophers judged the world eternal due to a semblance.
Pay attention to al-Razi's claim that the dispute reduces to free will.
2
Identify al-Tusi's rebuttal
Find where al-Tusi says the rebuttal does not apply to the original author because he only discussed the attribution of the Eternal to God.
This is the core correction.
3
Understand the defense of al-Razi
The commentator defends al-Razi by noting that some proofs for free will do not depend on ḥudūth, e.g., transmitted proofs.
This allows two possible orders of proof.
4
Consider the criticism of transmitted proofs
The passage points out that transmitted proofs only yield probability (ẓann), not certainty, so they are insufficient for foundational issues.
This raises a methodological concern.
5
Note the concluding remark
The commentator hints that there are rational proofs for free will, so the matter requires further study.
This leaves the discussion open.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولـه : ( من غير تعرض لفاعله حيث قالوا : إن العالم حادث لأنه إما أعيان، وإما أعراض، وكل منهما حادث : أما الأعيان فلأنها لا تخلو عن الحركة والسكون وهما حادثان، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث فالأعيان حادثة وإذا كانت الأعيان حادثة كانت الأعراض أيضاً حادثة، لقيامها بها . قوله : ( فحكموا بأن العالم (قديم لشبهة لاحت لهم لا لمجرد ذلك التجويز كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( ورد عليه بأنه يدل إلخ) هذا الرد لنصير الدين الطوسي في شرح الإشارات، ذكره في أوائل النمط الخامس منه ، ويمكن أن يقال : هذا لا ويمكن أن يقال : هذا لا يرد على المصنف قطعاً؛ لأنه إنما حكم بعود النزاع في جواز استناد القديم إلى الفاعل الذي هو الله تعالى إلى كونه موجباً أو مختاراً، لا في قدم العالم وحدوثه كما توهمه الشارح نعم يتوهم وروده على الرازي إن وجد في كلامه أن نزاعهم في قدم العالم وحدوثه عائد إلى ذلك، ويمكن دفعه عنه أيضاً بأن نقول: بعض أدلة الاختيار لا يتوقف حدوث العالم، ولا تعرض فيه لذلك كأدلته النقلية التي فصلها الآمدي في أبكار الأفكار، فإذا أثبت الاختيار بتلك الأدلة أمكن أن يفرع عليها حدوث العالم، كما يمكن العكس أيضاً إذا أثبت حدوثه بدليل لا يتوقف على كونه تعالى مختاراً. وإذا حمل كلام الإمام على هذا كان كلاماً لا غبار عليه اللهم إلا أن يقال : إن الأدلة النقلية لا تعدو إفادة الظن، كما الآمدي . فلا معنى لبناء المطلوب الذي هو إثبات الاختيار على ذلك، ثم تفريع حدوث العالم عليه وليس لهم دليل عقلي على أن ذلك المطلوب لا يتوقف على حدوث العالم. وأنت خبير بأن كلام الشارح في آخر المرصد الرابع في الصفات الوجودية من الإلهيات يشعر بأنهم يثبتون الاختيار تارة بأن إيجاب غير الصفات نقصان، فيتأمل . قوله : ( فإنهم استدلو أولاً إلخ) حيث قالوا : العالم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، وهما حادثان، وما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث .
His statement: 'Without addressing its agent, because they said: The world is temporally originated because it is either substances or accidents, and each of them is temporally originated. As for substances, because they are not free from motion and rest, and both are temporally originated, and whatever is not free from temporally originated things is itself temporally originated. So substances are temporally originated, and if substances are temporally originated, then accidents are also temporally originated, due to their inherence in them.' His statement: 'So they judged that the world is eternal due to a semblance that appeared to them, not merely because of that permission, as is not hidden.' His statement: 'And it was rebutted that it indicates etc.' This rebuttal is by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi in his commentary on al-Isharat, mentioned at the beginning of the fifth pattern. And it can be said: This does not—and it can be said: This does not refute the author at all; because he only ruled that the dispute concerning the permissibility of attributing the Eternal to the Agent who is God Most High goes back to whether He is a necessitator or a free agent, not concerning the eternity of the world and its temporal origination as the commentator imagined. Yes, it might be imagined to apply to al-Razi if it occurs in his speech that their dispute concerning the eternity of the world and its temporal origination goes back to that. And it can also be defended on his behalf by saying: Some proofs for free will do not depend on the temporal origination of the world, nor do they mention it, such as his transmitted proofs that al-Amidi detailed in Abkar al-Afkar. So if free will is proven by those proofs, it becomes possible to derive the temporal origination of the world from it, just as the opposite is also possible if its temporal origination is proven by a proof that does not depend on His being free. And if the Imam's words are interpreted in this way, his words are beyond reproach—except that it could be said: Transmitted proofs are only productive of probability (ẓann), as al-Amidi stated. So there is no point in building the desired conclusion which is the proof of free will on that, then deriving the temporal origination of the world from it, and they have no rational proof that the desired conclusion does not depend on the temporal origination of the world. And you are aware that the commentator's words at the end of the fourth observation on the ontological attributes from the section on divine matters suggest that they sometimes prove free will by saying that necessitating other than attributes is a deficiency, so let it be pondered. His statement: 'For they first argued etc.'—where they said: The world is not free from motion and rest, and both are temporally originated, and whatever is not free from temporally originated things is temporally originated.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a rebuttal of al-Razi's claim by Nasir al-Din al-Tusi (as transmitted by the commentator). It begins by quoting the theologians' own proof for the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of the world: the world consists of substances (a‘yān) and accidents (a‘rāḍ). Substances are never free from motion or rest, both of which are temporally originated; whatever is not free from temporally originated things must itself be temporally originated. Accidents are also temporally originated because they inhere in substances. This proof makes no reference to the divine agent at all, let alone whether He is free or necessitating. Then the commentator notes that the philosophers judged the world eternal based on a semblance (shubha), not merely because they allowed attributing the eternal to a free agent. Then the main rebuttal: al-Tusi points out that al-Razi's statement implies that theologians built their case for ḥudūth on the issue of free choice (ikhtiyār), but the opposite is true: they first proved the world's temporality without any mention of the agent, and only then built on that temporality to prove that its Creator must be free. The commentator then defends al-Razi, saying the rebuttal does not apply to the original author (the text's author, al-Iji?) because he only discussed the attribution of the Eternal (qadīm) to God, not directly the world's eternity. For al-Razi, one can argue that some proofs for free will (e.g., transmitted proofs from al-Amidi) do not depend on ḥudūth, so one could first prove free will and then deduce ḥudūth from it, or vice versa. However, the commentator notes a problem with transmitted proofs: they only yield probability (ẓann), not certainty, so using them for such foundational matters may be problematic. The passage concludes by noting that the commentator's own remarks in a later section suggest that free will can be proven by rational arguments (e.g., that necessitating other attributes is a deficiency), so the issue requires careful consideration.

Key Terms

شبهة
أبكار الأفكار
ظن
نصير الدين الطوسي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the ḥudūth proof depends on the attribute of free will.
Correction: This lesson shows that the proof is independent; it does not mention the agent, and free will can be proven separately.

Checkpoint
According to al-Tusi, why does the rebuttal not apply to the original author (al-Iji)?
A) Because the original author was not discussing the world's eternity
B) Because the original author only discussed the attribution of the Eternal to God, not directly the world's eternity
C) Because the original author agreed with al-Razi
D) Because the rebuttal was not by al-Tusi

Key Takeaway
The theologians' proof for the world's temporality is logically independent of any specific doctrine about God's agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your beliefs about God's actions in your life, do you rely more on rational certainty or on transmitted reports that may only be probable? Which of these foundations would survive a critical examination like the one al-Tusi and al-Razi engaged in?
?
This lesson shows that the proof for the world's temporality does not depend on God's free will. How often do you conflate separate theological truths in your own understanding? Are there areas of your faith where you've built a house of cards, assuming one doctrine depends on another?
?
The passage criticizes building foundational beliefs on probable (ẓannī) proofs. Consider your most deeply held convictions about God. Are they built on certain proofs or on assumptions you haven't tested? What would it cost you to examine them honestly?

Answering the Objection on Intending an Existing Thing
Objection and answer on intention and temporality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that voluntary creation necessarily implies temporality and the answer that distinguishes essential priority from temporal priority.

Core Idea
Intention can be prior in essence without being prior in time.

This lesson examines a critical objection against voluntary creation: how can an eternal being intend to create something temporal without the intention itself being temporal? The source presents both the objection and its incisive answer, clarifying the relationship between intention, creation, and time. By working through this passage, you will see how kalam thinkers defended divine voluntary agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer. Identify the key terms: qaṣd (intention), ījād al-mawjūd (bringing into existence something existing), ḥādith (temporal).
The objection relies on a necessary knowledge claim.
2
Identify the objection and its logic
The objector argues: intending an already existing thing is impossible, so intention must be simultaneous with non-existence of the effect; hence the effect is temporal. Understand why this seems to challenge divine creation.
This objection would make God's creation temporal in a problematic way.
3
Analyze the answer's distinction
The answer introduces a crucial distinction: priority can be by essence (bil-dhāt) not by time. The intention can be simultaneous with the effect in time but prior in essence. Also, sufficiency of intention determines simultaneity or temporal precedence.
This distinction preserves both divine eternity and temporal creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدفعه ما قد قيل من أنا نعلم بالضرورة أن القصد إلى إيجاد الموجود محال، فلا بد أن يكون القصد مقارناً لعدم الأثر فيكون أثر المختار حادثاً قطعاً. وقد يقال: تقدم القصد على الإيجاد كتقدم الإيجاد على الوجود في أنهما بحسب الذات، فيجوز مقارنتهما للوجود زماناً، لأن المحال هو القصد إلى إيجاد الموجود بوجود قبل. وبالجملة: فالقصد إذا كان كافياً في وجود المقصود كان معه وإذا لم يكن كافياً فيه فقد يتقدم عليه زماناً كقصدنا إلى أفعالنا.
And it is repelled by what was said: we know by necessity that intending to bring into existence something already existent is impossible. Thus the intention must be concomitant with the non-existence of the effect, so the effect of the voluntary agent is definitely temporal (ḥādith). And it may be said: the priority of intention over creation is like the priority of creation over existence in that both are by essence, so it is permissible that they be concomitant with existence in time, because the impossible is intending to bring into existence something already existent with a prior existence. In brief: if the intention is sufficient for the existence of the intended, then it is simultaneous with it; and if it is not sufficient for it, then it may precede it in time, like our intention to our actions.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This is an objection and answer. The objector says: we know that intending an already existing thing is impossible, so the intention must be before the effect, making the effect temporal. The answer distinguishes between essential and temporal priority. The intention can be simultaneous with the effect in time but prior in essence, like cause and effect. The sufficiency of the intention determines whether it is simultaneous or precedes. For God, His intention is sufficient, so it is simultaneous with the creation.

Key Terms
قصد
إيجاد الموجود
حادث
كفاية القصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Voluntary creation necessarily implies that the intention and therefore the effect must be temporal, undermining God's eternity.
Correction: Intention can be essentially prior without temporal priority; God's intention is sufficient and thus simultaneous with creation, preserving eternity.

Checkpoint
According to the answer in the passage, what determines whether intention is simultaneous with or precedes its effect in time?
A) The nature of the agent (human vs. divine)
B) Whether the intention is sufficient for the existence of the intended
C) The temporal priority of creation over existence
D) The impossibility of intending an existing thing

Key Takeaway
The objection that voluntary creation implies temporality is answered by distinguishing essential priority from temporal priority, allowing intention to be simultaneous with its effect when it is sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you intend something in your life, is your intention sufficient to bring it about, or do you rely on external factors? How does that affect the timing of your intention relative to the outcome?
?
The passage says intending an already existent thing with prior existence is impossible. Are you ever trying to 'change the past' by wishing it were different, rather than accepting what is and acting from the present?
?
Do you confuse being first in importance with being first in time? In what area of your life do you need to align your priorities with what is essentially prior, not just temporally urgent?

The Third Objection: The Eternal Does Not Need an Efficient Cause
Al-Iji responds to the third objection that reason judges the eternal does not need an efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the need for an efficient cause for the eternal, and how Al-Iji responds by asserting that temporality is not a necessary condition for need.

Core Idea
Reason intuitively knows that the eternal needs no cause; temporality is not a condition for need.

Al-Iji addresses a natural objection: if the eternal (God) has always existed, how can He need a cause? Reason itself seems to say no. In this passage, Al-Iji presents the objection and then carefully responds, clarifying that the need for an efficient cause is not conditioned upon temporality (huduth). The commentary helps unpack the subtle distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the full Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice how Al-Iji presents the objection before responding.
2
Identify the key claim about reason's intuition
The objectors claim that reason (al-ʿaql) judges by intuition (badīhah) that the eternal does not need an efficient cause. Find this in the text.
The phrase 'استحالة إيجاد الموجود' is crucial: bringing the existent into existence is impossible.
3
Understand the response about temporality
Al-Iji says it is not necessary that temporality (ḥudūth) be a condition for need. Note the concession that they may accept temporality as a condition for receiving influence (qabūl al-taʾthīr).
Temporality is a cause for the judgment of need, not for its external existence.
4
Examine the commentary on efficient cause
The commentary explains that 'efficient cause giving existence' includes disclosing (kāshifah) or specifying (mukhaṣṣiṣah) causes, and this excludes divine attributes which do not need such a cause.
Attributes need the essence not existence given by a cause.
5
Reflect on the discussion (baḥth)
The text ends with a discussion pointing to the statement that temporality need not be a condition. Consider why this nuance matters.
This prevents misunderstanding that the eternal has no need at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وعن الثالثة أن العقل ) ببديهته (يحكم بأن القديم) الذي هو مستمر الوجود في الأزل (لا يحتاج) إلى مؤثر يفيده الوجود ; لاستحالة إيجاد الموجود، وهذا هو مطلوبنا . ( ولا يجب ) منه (كون الحدوث شرطاً للحاجة ومعتبراً فيها وحده أو مع غيره، على أنا قد نلتزم شرطية الحدوث في قبول التأثير؛ إذ قد أجبنا عن إبطال اعتبار الحدوث بما سبق. قوله : ( إلى مؤثر يفيده الوجود إما كاشفة أو مخصصة وفائدته دفع النقض بصفاته تعالى لأنها ليست محتاجة إلى مفيد الوجود وإلا تقدم الذات عليها بالوجود بل إلى ماهيته تعالى لاقتضائها إياها وقد مر ذلك . قوله : ( كون الحدوث شرطاً للحاجة لجواز أن يكون لازماً لها متأخراً عنها بالذات . قوله : (بما سبق من أنه علة للتصديق بالحاجة لا لثبوتها في الخارج. قوله : ( وعن الثالث أن بداهة العقل إلخ يشكل هذا الحكم بالصفات مع أنه لا يخلو عن دعوى الضرورة في محل الخلاف . قوله : (بما سبق من أن المراد أن الحدوث علة للحكم والتصديق بالحاجة فقط . قوله : ( وهاهنا (بحث) ناظر إلى قوله لا يجب كون الحدوث شرطاً.
And concerning the third (objection): that the intellect, by its intuition, judges that the eternal—which is continuously existent in pre-eternity—does not need an efficient cause that gives it existence, because bringing an existent into existence is impossible, and this is our desired point. And it is not necessary from this that temporality be a condition for need and considered in it alone or with others, although we may concede temporality as a condition for receiving influence, since we have already answered the invalidation of considering temporality by what preceded. His saying: 'to an efficient cause that gives it existence' is either disclosing or specifying, and its benefit is to repel the objection to His attributes—may He be exalted—because they do not need something that gives existence, otherwise the essence would be prior to them in existence, but rather they need His essence—may He be exalted—because it necessitates them, and that has been mentioned. His saying: 'that temporality is a condition for need' is because it might be a necessary concomitant of it, posterior to it in essence. His saying: 'by what preceded' is that it is a cause for the judgment and affirmation of need, not for its external existence. His saying: 'and concerning the third objection, that the intuition of reason, etc.' this judgment is problematic regarding the attributes, and it is not free from a claim of necessity in a point of dispute. His saying: 'by what preceded' is that the intended meaning is that temporality is a cause for the judgment and affirmation of need only. His saying: 'and here there is a discussion' directed to his saying 'it is not necessary that temporality be a condition.'
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer passage. Al-Iji responds to the third objection against the claim that the eternal does not need an efficient cause. He first states the objection's core: reason intuitively judges that the eternal, being unceasingly existent in pre-eternity (azal), does not need an efficient cause that brings it into existence, because bringing an already-existing thing into existence is impossible (this is their desired conclusion). Then he addresses the issue of temporality (huduth) as a condition for need: it is not necessary that huduth be a condition for need or considered in it alone or with other factors. However, he concedes that they may accept huduth as a condition for receiving influence, but they have already answered the invalidation of considering huduth. Then the commentator (probably al-Jurjani) explains each phrase: the phrase 'efficient cause that gives it existence' excludes the divine attributes—they do not need such a cause; the phrase 'temporality as a condition for need' is possible but temporality would merely be a concomitant; 'by what preceded' refers to the earlier claim that huduth is a cause for the judgment of need not for its external reality; the intuition of reason is challenged regarding the attributes but still claimed; and finally the discussion (bahth) introduced here points to the statement that huduth need not be a condition.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim)
حدوث (huduth)
بديهة (badihah)
شرط (shart)
مؤثر (mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that the eternal (God) has absolutely no need of any cause, including for His attributes.
Correction: The eternal (God) does not need an efficient cause for His existence, but His attributes depend on His essence (they need His essence as a cause).

Checkpoint
According to the third objection's answer, what does reason intuitively judge about the eternal?
A) The eternal needs an efficient cause to continue existing.
B) The eternal does not need an efficient cause because it is continuously existent in pre-eternity.
C) The eternal needs a cause for its attributes but not for its essence.
D) The eternal's temporality is a condition for its need.

Key Takeaway
The eternal (God) does not need an efficient cause for His existence, but His attributes depend on His essence; temporality is not a condition for need but can be a cause for the judgment of need.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your worship, do you ever harbor a hidden belief that God needs your prayers or good deeds? This lesson teaches that God is eternally self-sufficient and needs nothing. Will you examine your heart tonight and repent from any trace of such thinking, affirming His absolute independence?
?
If God's attributes like His mercy and power are not independent but necessitated by His essence, how does that reshape your awe of Him? Do you realize that you cannot isolate His mercy from His holiness? Ponder: is there a attribute you have been misusing as a license for sin? Commit to knowing God as He truly is, not as you imagine Him.

Huduth as a Condition: The Logical Implication and Its Refutations
Al-Iji presents and refutes the implication that huduth is a condition for influence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the apparent implication that huduth is a condition for influence does not hold, due to the distinction between conceptual identity and extensional equivalence, and the nature of dependence.

Core Idea
Negation of pre-eternity is not identical to temporality — a logical distinction that preserves divine independence.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if the eternal (qadim) is utterly impervious to influence, then any acceptance of influence would require the removal of that imperviousness — which is the negation of qidam, and that negation is temporality (huduth) in created things. So doesn't that make huduth a condition for receiving influence? Al-Iji shows why this reasoning fails by clarifying the difference between conceptual identity and extensional overlap, and by explaining that dependence on the negation of an impediment is not the same as dependence on the positive attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the initial objection
Read the passage from the beginning through the statement that huduth appears to be a condition. Understand the apparent reasoning.
The objection is that the eternal's non-acceptance is due to its qidam, so acceptance depends on negation of qidam, which is huduth.
2
Understand the gist
The commentator explains that pre-eternity is an impediment, so acceptance depends on removing it. The removal is the negation of qidam, and this negation is extensionally equivalent to huduth.
3
Examine the first refutation
The first refutation is that the dependence is not causal; it is merely implicational. So conditionality is not established.
The objection conceded implication but not effecthood.
4
Examine the second refutation
The second refutation notes that negation of qidam and huduth are conceptually different; one is non-existence, the other a positive attribute. Thus conditionality does not transfer between correlates.
This relies on the principle that conditionality of one correlate does not imply conditionality of the other.
5
Conclude
Neither refutation allows huduth to be proven as a condition. The discussion continues with another subtlety.
This paves the way for the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا بحث : وهو أن القديم إذا لم يقبل التأثير أصلاً كان قبوله موقوفاً على انتفاء القدم الذي هو الحدوث في الموجودات، فيكون شرطاً له بلا شبهة، وأما الجواب عن ذلك الإبطال فقد عرفت ما فيه . قوله : ( وهو أن القديم إلخ ) حاصله أن القديم إذا لم يقبل التأثير لقدمه كان القدم عن التأثير فكان قبول التأثير موقوفاً على انتفاء القدم لأن انتفاء المانع مما يتوقف عليه المعلول وانتفاء القدم هو الحدوث من حيث الصدق وإن تغايرا في المفهوم فيكون التوقف على انتفاء القدم توقفاً على الحدوث وبما حررنا اندفع ما قيل التوقف بمعنى المعلولية والتأخر غير مسلم والاستلزام مسلم ولا فساد فيه لأنه لا يثبت شرطية الحدوث وما قيل لا نسلم أن انتفاء القدم عين الحدوث فإن الأول عدمي ومفهوم إضافي بخلاف الثاني. غاية الأمر التلازم ولا يلزم من شرطية أحد المتلازمين شرطية الآخر. التأثير في أول زمان الوجود كما هو الظاهر فقد لا يسلم جدوى هذا الفرق ؛ لأن المانع من التأثير وهو لزوم تحصيل الحاصل لما كان مرتفعاً بتحصيل وصف البقاء في الزمان اللاحق الذي لم يكن حاصلاً في الزمان السابق سواء كان الباقي قديماً أو حادثاً لم يكن لتحقق أول زمان الوجود وانتفائه دخل في الاستناد إلى الفاعل.
And here there is a discussion: that if the eternal does not accept influence at all, its acceptance is contingent upon the negation of pre-eternity (qidam), which is temporality (huduth) in existents, so it would be a condition for it without doubt. As for the answer to that invalidation, you have already known what is in it. His saying: 'that the eternal, etc.' its gist is that if the eternal does not accept influence due to its pre-eternity, then pre-eternity would be an impediment to influence, so acceptance of influence would be contingent upon the negation of pre-eternity, because the negation of the impediment is among the things on which the effect depends, and the negation of pre-eternity is temporality in terms of extensional equivalence, even if they differ in concept. Thus the dependence on the negation of pre-eternity is a dependence on temporality. And by what we have articulated, what was said is repelled: that dependence in the sense of effecthood and posteriority is not conceded, but the implication is conceded, and there is no corruption in it because it does not establish the conditionality of temporality. And what was said: we do not concede that the negation of pre-eternity is identical with temporality, for the former is non-existent and a relational concept unlike the latter. At most there is correlation, and the conditionality of one correlate does not necessitate the conditionality of the other. (There is also) influence in the first time of existence, as is apparent, so the benefit of this distinction may not be conceded, because the impediment to influence—which is the necessity of obtaining what is already obtained—is removed by obtaining the attribute of continuance in the later time that was not obtained in the earlier time, whether the thing remaining is eternal or temporal. The existence or non-existence of the first time of existence has no bearing on the attribution to the agent.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage is a discussion (bahth) within the response to the third objection. It examines the logical implication that if the eternal (qadim) is completely impervious to influence (ta'thir), then its acceptance of influence would depend on the negation of its pre-eternity (qidam). The negation of qidam is temporality (huduth) in existing things, so huduth would appear to be a condition for influence. The commentator then explains: the gist is that the eternal's non-acceptance due to its pre-eternity means pre-eternity is an impediment, so acceptance depends on removing that impediment (i.e., negation of qidam). This negation is extensionally equivalent to huduth, even though they differ in meaning—negation of qidam is a non-being and relational concept, while huduth is a positive attribute. So dependence on negation is not identical to dependence on huduth. They then refute two objections: 1) the dependence is merely implicational, not causal, so conditionality is not proven; 2) the two are not identical, so conditionality of one does not entail conditionality of the other. Finally, the discussion addresses a potential distinction regarding influence at the first moment of existence: it is argued that the impediment (obtaining what is already obtained) is removed by obtaining the attribute of continuance (baqa') at a later time, regardless of whether the being is eternal or temporal, so the first moment has no special role. This reinforces that huduth is not a necessary condition.

Key Terms
قديم (qadim)
قدم (qidam)
حدوث (huduth)
تأثير (ta'thir)
مانع (mani')
علة (illah)
تلازم (talazum)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the negation of pre-eternity (qidam) is identical to temporality (huduth) both in extension and concept, and thus if one is a condition, the other must also be a condition.
Correction: The text clarifies that they are extensionally equivalent but differ in concept. Negation of qidam is a non-being and relational concept, while huduth is a positive attribute. Therefore, conditionality of one does not necessitate conditionality of the other.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion regarding the conditionality of huduth based on the refutations?
A) Huduth is proven to be a condition for influence.
B) The implication is rejected, so huduth is not a condition.
C) The implication is conceded but conditionality is not proven due to non-causal dependence.
D) The negation of qidam and huduth are identical, so conditionality transfers.

Key Takeaway
Huduth is not a necessary condition for influence because the dependence on negation of qidam is not causal, and the two concepts are distinct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that Allah's pre-eternity does not prevent Him from influencing creation; rather, it is not a condition that creation must have temporal beginning. How does this affect your trust in Allah's ability to act in your life right now, even when you feel distant from Him? What would change if you truly believed that He is not bound by time in His care for you?
?
The discussion distinguishes between conceptual distinctness and extensional overlap. In your own understanding of Tawheed, are there attributes of Allah that you have mistakenly treated as identical when they are only correlated? How might that error have affected your worship or reliance upon Him? Resolve to study one divine attribute this week with the aim of understanding its unique meaning.

The First Moment and the Impediment of Obtaining What Is Already Obtained
Al-Iji refutes the idea that influence requires a first moment of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that influence only occurs at the first moment of existence does not make huduth a condition, because the true impediment is removed by continuance, not by the first moment.

Core Idea
Continuance, not origination, removes the barrier to influence — so huduth is irrelevant.

A remaining subtlety is the suggestion that influence can only occur at the first moment of existence, implying that only temporal beings can receive influence. Al-Iji refutes this by analyzing the impediment: the 'luzum tahsil al-hasil' (necessity of obtaining what is already obtained). He argues that this impediment is removed when the agent bestows the attribute of continuance (baqa') at a later time, a moment that was not previously obtained. This holds for both eternal and temporal beings. Thus the first moment of existence has no special role, and huduth does not become a condition for influence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the proposed distinction
Some might argue that influence only applies at the first moment of existence, so only temporal beings can receive influence. Read the passage's opening.
The phrase 'التأثير في أول زمان الوجود' indicates this.
2
Identify the impediment
The impediment is 'luzum tahsil al-hasil' — obtaining what is already obtained. This would prevent influence if the attribute already exists.
The text mentions 'المانع من التأثير وهو لزوم تحصيل الحاصل'.
3
See how continuance removes the impediment
The agent can bestow the attribute of continuance (baqa') at a later time, which was not obtained before. This new attribute is not already possessed, so the impediment is removed.
The text says 'مرتفعاً بتحصيل وصف البقاء في الزمان اللاحق الذي لم يكن حاصلاً في الزمان السابق'.
4
Apply to both eternal and temporal
This applies regardless of whether the being is eternal (qadim) or temporal (hadith). The first moment of existence has no bearing on the possibility of receiving influence.
The text states 'سواء كان الباقي قديماً أو حادثاً'.
5
Conclude
Thus the distinction does not prove huduth is a condition. The divine can receive influence at any time through bestowal of new attributes.
This reinforces Allah's ability to act freely.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهاهنا بحث : وهو أن القديم إذا لم يقبل التأثير أصلاً كان قبوله موقوفاً على انتفاء القدم الذي هو الحدوث في الموجودات، فيكون شرطاً له بلا شبهة، وأما الجواب عن ذلك الإبطال فقد عرفت ما فيه . قوله : ( وهو أن القديم إلخ ) حاصله أن القديم إذا لم يقبل التأثير لقدمه كان القدم عن التأثير فكان قبول التأثير موقوفاً على انتفاء القدم لأن انتفاء المانع مما يتوقف عليه المعلول وانتفاء القدم هو الحدوث من حيث الصدق وإن تغايرا في المفهوم فيكون التوقف على انتفاء القدم توقفاً على الحدوث وبما حررنا اندفع ما قيل التوقف بمعنى المعلولية والتأخر غير مسلم والاستلزام مسلم ولا فساد فيه لأنه لا يثبت شرطية الحدوث وما قيل لا نسلم أن انتفاء القدم عين الحدوث فإن الأول عدمي ومفهوم إضافي بخلاف الثاني. غاية الأمر التلازم ولا يلزم من شرطية أحد المتلازمين شرطية الآخر. التأثير في أول زمان الوجود كما هو الظاهر فقد لا يسلم جدوى هذا الفرق ؛ لأن المانع من التأثير وهو لزوم تحصيل الحاصل لما كان مرتفعاً بتحصيل وصف البقاء في الزمان اللاحق الذي لم يكن حاصلاً في الزمان السابق سواء كان الباقي قديماً أو حادثاً لم يكن لتحقق أول زمان الوجود وانتفائه دخل في الاستناد إلى الفاعل.
And here there is a discussion: that if the eternal does not accept influence at all, its acceptance is contingent upon the negation of pre-eternity (qidam), which is temporality (huduth) in existents, so it would be a condition for it without doubt. As for the answer to that invalidation, you have already known what is in it. His saying: 'that the eternal, etc.' its gist is that if the eternal does not accept influence due to its pre-eternity, then pre-eternity would be an impediment to influence, so acceptance of influence would be contingent upon the negation of pre-eternity, because the negation of the impediment is among the things on which the effect depends, and the negation of pre-eternity is temporality in terms of extensional equivalence, even if they differ in concept. Thus the dependence on the negation of pre-eternity is a dependence on temporality. And by what we have articulated, what was said is repelled: that dependence in the sense of effecthood and posteriority is not conceded, but the implication is conceded, and there is no corruption in it because it does not establish the conditionality of temporality. And what was said: we do not concede that the negation of pre-eternity is identical with temporality, for the former is non-existent and a relational concept unlike the latter. At most there is correlation, and the conditionality of one correlate does not necessitate the conditionality of the other. (There is also) influence in the first time of existence, as is apparent, so the benefit of this distinction may not be conceded, because the impediment to influence—which is the necessity of obtaining what is already obtained—is removed by obtaining the attribute of continuance in the later time that was not obtained in the earlier time, whether the thing remaining is eternal or temporal. The existence or non-existence of the first time of existence has no bearing on the attribution to the agent.

Correction: The impediment is removed by bestowing continuance at any later moment, not just the first. Thus, both eternal and temporal beings can receive influence.

Checkpoint

A) The first moment of existence.
B) The attribute of continuance (baqa') bestowed at a later time.
C) The negation of pre-eternity.
D) The temporal nature of the being.

Key Takeaway
The first moment of existence is not necessary for influence; continuance removes the impediment, making huduth irrelevant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if God’s influence on your life only matters at the ‘first moment’ of your deeds or repentance? How does this passage challenge you to see every moment as an opportunity for His continuance and renewal? Will you start each day seeking His continuance rather than a fresh start?
?
If the barrier to God’s influence in your heart is not the absence of a beginning but the refusal to receive His continuance, what area of your life are you holding back from allowing Him to sustain? Why do you trust in beginnings but not in ongoing dependence?

Refutation of the Sixth Objection: Imaginary vs. Real Attribution
Commentary on the refutation of the sixth objection using the distinction between imaginary and real attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between imaginary and real attribution and how this distinction refutes the sixth objection against the eternity of God's attributes.

Core Idea
Imaginary attribution cannot justify real attribution in kalam arguments.

This passage presents a key distinction in kalam between imaginary (wahmi) and real (haqiqi) attribution. The author applies this distinction to refute an objection that attempts to use the eternal non-existence of entities to argue against the eternal existence of God's attributes. By carefully examining the nature of attribution, the author shows that the objection relies on a confusion between two different types of cause-effect relationships.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two types of attribution
Look for the terms 'istinad wahmi' and 'istinad haqiqi' and note how the author distinguishes them.
The distinction hinges on whether the two terms exist in external reality.
2
Apply the distinction to the examples given
The author applies the distinction to the case of continuous non-existence and the number four. See how the same logic works.
3
Understand the conclusion
The permissibility of imaginary attribution does not entail the permissibility of real attribution. This is used to block the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة، لكن هذا الاستناد أمر وهمي لا حقيقة له في الخارج)، فلا يلزم من جواز استناد العدم المستمر إلى العدم المستمر استناداً وهمياً جواز استناد الوجود المستمر إلى الوجود المستمر استناداً حقيقياً، وكلامنا في هذا الاستناد ؛ لأن القدم من عوارض الوجود دون العدم. ( وعن السادسة مثله وهو أن يقال الأربعة من الأعداد التي لا وجود لها ، وكذا زوجيتها أيضاً من الاعتبارات العقلية فاستنادها إلى ذات الأربعة استناد لا حقيقة له في الخارج، فلا يلزم من جواز هذا الاستناد دائماً جواز الاستناد الحقيقي دائماً . قوله : ( أمر وهمي إلخ) أي أمر عقلي ينتزعه العقل من استناد الوجود إلى الوجود لا حقيقة له في الخارج إذ ليس الخارج ظرفاً لنفسه لعدم الطرفين في الخارج. قوله : (استناداً حقيقياً) أي استناداً له حقيقة في الخارج لتحقق طرفيه فيه . قوله : ( من الأعداد التي لا وجود لها إلخ) لتركبها من الوحدات التي هي أمور اعتبارية . قوله : ( وكذا زوجيتها إلخ) لأن الموصوف إذا كان اعتبارياً كانت الصفة أيضاً كذلك. وثانيهما ) أي ثاني الأمرين من مباحث القديم ( أنه يوصف به أي بالقدم ( ذات الله تعالى (اتفاقا من الحكماء وأهل الملة (و) يوصف به أيضاً ( صفاته عند الأشاعرة ) ومن يحذو حذوهم فإنهم أجمعوا على أن لله سبحانه صفات موجودة قديمة قائمة بذاته تعالى ( وأما المعتزلة فأنكروه (لفظاً ) أي أنكروا أن يوصف
The cause, but this attribution is an imaginary (wahmi) matter having no reality in the external world, so the permissibility of attributing continuous non-existence to continuous non-existence in an imaginary way does not entail the permissibility of attributing continuous existence to continuous existence in a real way; and our discussion concerns this latter attribution, because eternity (qidam) is an attribute of existence, not non-existence. And concerning the sixth [objection], the same [reply is given], namely that it is said: the number four is among numbers that have no existence, and likewise its evenness is also among intellectual considerations, so its attribution to the essence of four is an attribution having no reality in the external world; thus the permissibility of this attribution eternally does not entail the permissibility of real attribution eternally. His saying: 'an imaginary matter' means a mental matter that the mind extracts from the attribution of existence to existence, having no reality in the external world, since the external world is not a locus for itself due to the non-existence of the two terms in the external world. His saying: 'real attribution' means an attribution having reality in the external world due to the actuality of its two terms in it. His saying: 'among numbers that have no existence' because they are composed of units which are relational matters. His saying: 'and likewise its evenness' because when the subject is relational, the attribute is also likewise.
Source pages p. 199

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the sixth objection in a series of objections against the eternity of God's attributes. The author first summarizes the reply to the fifth objection (about continuous non-existence), then applies the same logic to the sixth. The key distinction is between 'imaginary attribution' (istinad wahmi) and 'real attribution' (istinad haqiqi). Imaginary attribution is a mental construct without external reality, while real attribution requires both terms to exist externally. Since the number four and its evenness are mental constructs, their attribution is imaginary; similarly, the case of continuous non-existence is imaginary. The commentary then clarifies these terms. The next part introduces the second of the two main topics regarding eternity: that it is attributed to God's essence by consensus of philosophers and religious people, and to His attributes according to the Ash'aris, while the Mu'tazila denied it verbally but affirmed it in meaning through their theory of states (ahwal).

Key Terms
استناد وهمي
استناد حقيقي
العدم المستمر
الوجود المستمر
زوجية
اعتبار عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That an imaginary attribution can serve as a valid argument for a real attribution.
Correction: The passage shows that imaginary and real attributions are fundamentally different and cannot be used interchangeably in theological arguments.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction used in the refutation of the sixth objection?
A) Real vs. imaginary attribution
B) Eternal vs. temporal existence
C) Necessary vs. contingent existence
D) Unity vs. multiplicity of attributes

Key Takeaway
The distinction between imaginary and real attribution is crucial for understanding how kalam arguments about God's attributes are constructed and refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the attributes of Allah, are you content with mental concepts, or do you strive to connect them to the reality of your faith? How can you move from imaginary belief to living conviction?
?
Have you ever argued for a religious truth using an analogy that seemed logical but was actually based on mere mental constructs? How can you ensure your arguments are grounded in the reality of revelation and reason?
?
The lesson teaches that eternity is a property of existence, not non-existence. What does this mean for your understanding of your own temporary existence? How does this reality compel you to prioritize your relationship with the Eternal?

Consensus on Eternity for God's Essence and Attributes
States the consensus on eternity for God's essence and the differing views on attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus among philosophers and religious scholars that God's essence is eternal, and the disagreement between Ash'aris and Mu'tazila regarding the eternity of His attributes.

Core Idea
God's essence is eternal by consensus; attributes are eternal for Ash'aris but not for Mu'tazila.

This passage introduces the second major topic within the study of eternity: to what does the attribute of eternity apply? The author states that it applies to God's essence by consensus, and to His attributes according to the Ash'aris, while the Mu'tazila denied it verbally. This sets the stage for a deeper discussion on the nature of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two objects of attribution
Notice that eternity is attributed to both the essence and the attributes, but with different levels of agreement.
2
Understand the consensus on essence
All philosophers and religious people agree that God's essence is eternal.
3
Understand the Ash'ari position on attributes
The Ash'aris affirm that God's attributes are also eternal, existing in His essence.
4
Note the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila verbally denied that attributes are eternal, though some interpretations suggest they affirmed it in meaning through their theory of states.

Source Text

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you learn that even great scholars disagreed on this issue, does it humble you or unsettle you? Will you seek to affirm His attributes as He deserves, or do you risk reducing them in your heart?
